The Ten Claims about God's Essence
Ghazali enumerates ten claims about God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ten fundamental claims that Ghazali makes about God's essence in the first pole of his work.

Core Idea
Ghazali's first pole presents ten key claims affirming God's transcendence and unity.

In this opening passage of the first pole, Imam al-Ghazali lays out the roadmap for his discussion of God's essence. He enumerates ten essential claims that will be elaborated upon, covering God's existence, eternity, incorporeality, and uniqueness. This lesson introduces these claims and sets the stage for the detailed arguments to follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the ten claims.
Notice the compact style of Ghazali's listing.
2
Identify each claim
Go through the list and note each of the ten claims. They appear in a specific order.
Write them down to help remember.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why Ghazali begins with these ten claims and how they form the foundation of Sunni theology.
These reflect key kalam positions against various sects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القطب الأول : النظر في ذات الله تعالى : فنبين فيه وجودَهُ ، وأنه قديم ، وأنه باقي ، وأنه ليس بجوهر ، ولا جسم ، ولا عرض ، ولا محدود بحد ، ولا هو مخصوص بجهةٍ ، وأنه مرئي كما أنه معلوم ، وأنه واحد فهذه عشر دعاوى نبيتها في هذا القطب إن شاء الله تعالى
The first pole: Consideration of God's essence, exalted. We will explain in it His existence, that He is eternal, that He is everlasting, that He is not a substance, nor a body, nor an accident, nor limited by a limit, nor is He particularized by a direction, that He is visible just as He is known, and that He is one. So these are ten claims that we will elaborate in this pole, God willing.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage outlines the contents of the first pole, which deals with God's essence. Ghazali enumerates ten claims that will be explained: (1) God exists, (2) He is eternal (qadim), (3) He is everlasting (baqi), (4) He is not a substance (jawhar), (5) He is not a body (jism), (6) He is not an accident ('arad), (7) He is not limited by a limit (had), (8) He is not particularized by a direction (jiha), (9) He is visible (mar'i) just as He is known (ma'lum), and (10) He is one (wahid). These are fundamental positions in Sunni kalam, affirming God's transcendence and unity.

Key Terms
ذات الله
قديم
باقي
جوهر
جسم
عرض
محدود
جهة
مرئي
واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is visible like a physical object.
Correction: Ghazali asserts visibility (ru'ya) of God in the afterlife but clarifies it is unlike physical sight, as God is not a body.

Checkpoint
How many claims does Ghazali say will be explained in the first pole?
A) Seven
B) Ten
C) Twelve
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first pole introduces ten essential claims that define the Islamic understanding of God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that God is not limited by a direction. Yet when you pray, do you ever subconsciously imagine Him 'up there' or 'somewhere'? How does this lesson challenge your mental image of God? Will you actively train your heart to seek Him without spatial limits?
?
Ghazali lists 'God is visible' as a claim you must believe. If you truly believed you will see God in the afterlife, how would that change the way you long for death? Does your current attachment to this world reveal a weak desire for that vision?
?
You say God is one, but do your anxieties and hopes reveal that you treat other things as gods? Where do you seek ultimate security — in Allah alone or in people, wealth, or plans? What step will you take today to redirect your trust solely to the One?

Introduction to the First Pole: God's Essence
Ghazali introduces the first pole of the book: consideration of God's essence with ten claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the first pole of al-Iqtisad, which introduces ten claims concerning God's essence.

Core Idea
The first pole sets the foundation: ten claims about God's essence.

Imam al-Ghazali organizes his work into poles (aqṭāb), each treating a major topic. The first pole focuses on God's essence and outlines ten claims that will be examined in detail. This brief introduction serves as a roadmap for the subsequent discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the concise, outline-like structure.
2
Understand 'pole'
Recognize that 'pole' (قطب) is a structural term meaning a major section of the book.
3
Note the ten claims
The passage explicitly states that this pole contains ten claims (دعاوى). This indicates a systematic enumeration.
4
Reflect on the topic
Consider why Ghazali begins with God's essence. This is the foundation of Islamic theology.
The essence of God is the primary subject of theological inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القطب الأول: النظر في ذات الله عز وجل، وفيه عشر دعاوى.
The first pole: consideration of the essence of God, may He be exalted, and it contains ten claims.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a section heading that marks the beginning of the first major topic (pole) of the book. It states that this pole is dedicated to 'consideration of the essence of God' (النظر في ذات الله) and that it comprises ten claims or assertions (دعاوى). The phrase 'عز وجل' affirms God's majesty and exaltation. The brief structure serves as an outline for the subsequent detailed discussions.

Key Terms

النظر في ذات الله
دعاوى

Checkpoint

A) Five claims
B) Ten claims
C) Fifteen claims
D) Twenty claims

Key Takeaway
The first pole of al-Iqtisad is an introduction to the study of God's essence, consisting of ten systematic claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are about to study ten claims about God's essence. Before you proceed, ask yourself: Do you truly believe that your concept of God is based on evidence, or is it inherited? Commit to honestly evaluating each claim as you encounter it.
?
The phrase 'consideration of the essence of God' implies deep intellectual effort. Are you willing to set aside your preconceptions and follow the arguments wherever they lead? Write a personal pledge to approach this pole with intellectual humility and sincerity.

The Proof from Origination for God's Existence
Ghazali states the first claim and provides a proof from the origination of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the world must have a cause because it is originated, and that this cause is God.

Core Idea
Everything that begins to exist needs a cause; the world began, so it has a Creator.

Ghazali begins his book with the foundational proof of God's existence. He uses the universal principle of causation: every originated thing has a cause. By showing that the world is originated, he concludes it must have a cause, which is God. This lesson unpacks that compact argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim and proof
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow. Notice how Ghazali states the claim and immediately gives the proof.
The proof is presented as a syllogism.
2
Identify the premises
Find the two premises: 'every originated thing has a cause' and 'the world is originated'.
These are general and specific.
3
Understand the conclusion
See how the conclusion follows: 'therefore the world has a cause'. Realize that the cause is later identified as Allah.
4
Examine the definition of 'world'
Ghazali defines 'world' as everything other than Allah, specifically bodies and their accidents. This clarifies the subject of the proof.
This definition prevents confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى الأولى
وُجُودُه تَعَالى وتقدس
وبرهانه : أنَّا نقول : كلُّ حادث فلحدوثِهِ سببٌ ، والعالم حادث ؛ فيلزم منه أنَّ له سبباً ونعني بالعالم : كلَّ موجود سوى الله تعالى ، ونعني بكل موجود سوى الله تعالى : الأجسام كلها ، وأعراضها
وشرح ذلك كله بالتفصيل : أنا لا نشك في أصل الوجود ، ثم نعلم أن تقسيم الموجودات
كل موجود : فإما متحيز ، أو غير متحيز ، وأنَّ كلَّ متحيز : إن لم يكن فيه ائتلاف .. فنسميه جوهراً فرداً ، وإن ائتلف إلى غيره . . سميناه جسماً ، وأنَّ غير المتحيز : إما أن يستدعي وجوده جسماً يقوم به ونسميه الأعراض ، أو لا يستدعيه وهو الله سبحانه وتعالى
The First Claim: His existence, exalted and sanctified. And its proof: we say: every originated thing has a cause for its origination, and the world is originated; therefore it follows that it has a cause. And by 'world' we mean every existent other than Allah, exalted. And by every existent other than Allah we mean: all bodies and their accidents. And a detailed explanation of all that: we do not doubt the principle of existence, then we know that the division of existents: every existent is either occupying space or not occupying space. And every spatial thing: if it has no composition in it, we call it an indivisible atom; and if it is composed with another, we call it a body. And the non-spatial: either its existence requires a body in which it inheres, and we call it accidents; or it does not require that, and it is Allah, glorified and exalted.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion of the first claim (al-daʿwā al-ūlā): establishing the existence of God. Ghazali presents a proof (burhān) based on the principality of causation: every originated thing (ḥādith) has a cause for its origination. The world (al-ʿālam) is originated, therefore it must have a cause, which is God. He then defines the world as every existent other than God, specifically all bodies (ajsām) and their accidents (aʿrāḍ). To clarify, he provides a detailed classification (taqsīm) of all existents: they are either spatial (mutahayyiz) or non-spatial (ghayr mutahayyiz). Spatial things are either simple (jawhar fard, indivisible atom) or composite (jism, body). Non-spatial things either require a body to inhere in (accidents) or do not require a body—this is God. This classification grounds the definition of the world as the totality of bodies and accidents, which are all originated, thereby completing the proof of God's existence.

Key Terms
دعوى
برهان
حادث
سبب
عالم
الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it defines the world as everything other than God, thereby assuming God's existence.
Correction: The definition is merely conceptual to clarify what we mean by 'world'. It does not assume God's existence; it distinguishes the world from the Creator. The proof then shows that this world must have a cause, which is God.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's proof, what conclusion follows from the two premises?
A) The world has no cause
B) The world has a cause
C) The world is eternal
D) The world is self-caused

Key Takeaway
The proof from origination uses the universal causal principle to demonstrate that the world, being originated, must have a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does it change your daily awareness to know that every moment you exist is because a Creator continuously brings you into being? Do you live as if you are self-sufficient?
?
If you sincerely believed that everything around you—including your own life, family, and possessions—is originated and utterly dependent on a cause, what would you do differently today? Would you thank that Cause?
?
The proof leaves no room for chance. When you look at your own decisions and their consequences, do you acknowledge that your choices are also originated and have causes? How might that realization push you to take more responsibility for seeking the right causes (e.g., repentance, good deeds)?

A Categorization of All Existence
Ghazali provides a detailed classification of existents to clarify what is meant by 'world'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's detailed classification of all existents into spatial and non-spatial, and how this supports the definition of the world as bodies and accidents.

Core Idea
All existence is either spatial or non-spatial; spatial things are atoms or bodies, non-spatial are accidents or God.

After giving the proof, Ghazali delves into ontology—what exactly exists? He provides a neat classification: everything that exists is either an object that occupies space or not. Those that occupy space are either indivisible atoms or composite bodies. Those that do not occupy space either inhere in bodies (accidents) or are independent (God). This classification solidifies the definition of the world as the sum of bodies and accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview of the classification.
Notice the logical flow from existence to division.
2
Identify the first division
Find the division into spatial (mutahayyiz) and non-spatial (ghayr mutahayyiz).
3
Understand the subdivisions
Spatial things are either atoms (jawhar fard) or bodies (jism). Non-spatial things are either accidents or God.
Atoms have no composition; bodies are composed.
4
Connect to the definition of the world
Notice that the world (everything other than God) consists solely of bodies and accidents, which are the spatial and accident categories.
This clarifies the earlier definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى الأولى
وُجُودُه تَعَالى وتقدس
وبرهانه : أنَّا نقول : كلُّ حادث فلحدوثِهِ سببٌ ، والعالم حادث ؛ فيلزم منه أنَّ له سبباً ونعني بالعالم : كلَّ موجود سوى الله تعالى ، ونعني بكل موجود سوى الله تعالى : الأجسام كلها ، وأعراضها
وشرح ذلك كله بالتفصيل : أنا لا نشك في أصل الوجود ، ثم نعلم أن تقسيم الموجودات
كل موجود : فإما متحيز ، أو غير متحيز ، وأنَّ كلَّ متحيز : إن لم يكن فيه ائتلاف .. فنسميه جوهراً فرداً ، وإن ائتلف إلى غيره . . سميناه جسماً ، وأنَّ غير المتحيز : إما أن يستدعي وجوده جسماً يقوم به ونسميه الأعراض ، أو لا يستدعيه وهو الله سبحانه وتعالى
The First Claim: His existence, exalted and sanctified. And its proof: we say: every originated thing has a cause for its origination, and the world is originated; therefore it follows that it has a cause. And by 'world' we mean every existent other than Allah, exalted. And by every existent other than Allah we mean: all bodies and their accidents. And a detailed explanation of all that: we do not doubt the principle of existence, then we know that the division of existents: every existent is either occupying space or not occupying space. And every spatial thing: if it has no composition in it, we call it an indivisible atom; and if it is composed with another, we call it a body. And the non-spatial: either its existence requires a body in which it inheres, and we call it accidents; or it does not require that, and it is Allah, glorified and exalted.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion of the first claim (al-daʿwā al-ūlā): establishing the existence of God. Ghazali presents a proof (burhān) based on the principality of causation: every originated thing (ḥādith) has a cause for its origination. The world (al-ʿālam) is originated, therefore it must have a cause, which is God. He then defines the world as every existent other than God, specifically all bodies (ajsām) and their accidents (aʿrāḍ). To clarify, he provides a detailed classification (taqsīm) of all existents: they are either spatial (mutahayyiz) or non-spatial (ghayr mutahayyiz). Spatial things are either simple (jawhar fard, indivisible atom) or composite (jism, body). Non-spatial things either require a body to inhere in (accidents) or do not require a body—this is God. This classification grounds the definition of the world as the totality of bodies and accidents, which are all originated, thereby completing the proof of God's existence.

متحيز

جسم
أعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that everything that exists is a body, but Ghazali includes atoms, accidents, and God as distinct categories.
Correction: The classification shows that existence includes bodies, atoms (which are not bodies), accidents, and God. Bodies are only one type of spatial being.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is a 'jawhar fard'?
A) A body
B) An indivisible atom
C) An accident
D) God

Key Takeaway
Every existent falls into one of four categories: atom, body, accident, or God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is non-spatial and does not inhere in anything, how must your understanding of His closeness change? Do you still imagine Him in a particular direction or place? What do you need to correct in your heart to truly accept this reality?
?
The world, according to this passage, is nothing but atoms, bodies, and accidents. What does that mean for your attachments? Are you more attached to what is spatial (possessions, people) or to the fleeting accidents (feelings, thoughts)? How does this classification challenge your priorities?
?
Allah is completely unlike any other existent. Does your worship reflect this? When you pray, are you aware that you are addressing the One who is not bound by space or time? How can you cultivate a deeper sense of awe and devotion based on this truth?

Ghazali's Refutation of the Sceptic on Accidents
Ghazali dismisses the sceptic's denial of accidents and argues that the dispute itself proves their existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the sceptic who denies the existence of accidents by using the sceptic's own actions as proof.

Core Idea
The sceptic's own dispute proves the reality of accidents.

You have likely encountered someone who denies something obvious. Ghazali here faces a sceptic who claims that accidents—like colors or motions—do not really exist. Instead of a long theoretical proof, Ghazali uses a clever argument: the sceptic’s very act of arguing is itself an accident. In this passage, watch how he turns the sceptic’s position against itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Pay attention to Ghazali's confident tone and the rhetorical question he poses.
Notice that Ghazali starts by stating that accidents are known by observation.
2
Identify the two options
Ghazali presents a dilemma: either the sceptic's actions (dispute, clamor, seeking proof) exist or they do not. Write down both possibilities.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
See the conclusion
For each possibility, Ghazali draws a conclusion that undermines the sceptic. Summarize the conclusion in your own words.
The argument works regardless of which option is true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ثبوتُ الأجسام وأعراضها .. فمعلوم بالمشاهدة ، ولا يُلتفت إلى من لا ينازع في الأعراض وإن طال فيها صياحه ، وأخذ يلتمس منك دليلاً عليه فإن شعبه ونزاعه والتماسه وصياحه إن لم يكن موجوداً .. فكيف يُشتغل بالجواب عنه والإصغاء إليه ؟! وإن كان موجوداً .. فهو - لا محالة - غيرُ جسم المنازع ؛ إذ كان جسمه موجوداً من قبل ولم يكن التنازع موجوداً
As for the existence of bodies and their accidents, it is known by observation, and no attention is paid to those who dispute about accidents even if they prolong their clamor. And if he seeks from you a proof for it, then his agitation, dispute, seeking, and clamor—if they are not existent, then how can one occupy oneself with answering him and listening to him? And if they are existent, then they are necessarily other than the disputant's body, because his body existed before and the dispute did not exist.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage addresses the sophist (safsata) who denies the reality of accidents or demands a proof for their existence. Ghazali asserts that the existence of bodies and accidents is known by direct observation (mushāhada) and should not be doubted. He then presents a dialectical argument: the sceptic's own actions—his dispute (nizāʿ), seeking of proof (iltimās), and clamor (ṣiyāḥ)—are themselves accidents. If these actions are non-existent, then the sceptic does not exist as a disputant, so there is no need to respond. But if they are existent, then they are accidents distinct from his body (since his body existed before the dispute arose). Thus, the sceptic's own activity proves the existence of accidents, refuting his own denial. This is a concise reductio ad absurdum.

Key Terms
سفسطة
مشاهدة
أعراض
جسم
نزاع
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali is dismissing all philosophical skepticism as invalid.
Correction: Ghazali is specifically targeting those who deny obvious empirical facts, such as the existence of accidents, not all philosophical inquiry.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali refute the sceptic who denies accidents?
A) By quoting scripture
B) By pointing out that the sceptic's own dispute is an accident
C) By appealing to authority
D) By ignoring him

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the denial of accidents by showing that the sceptic's own disputation is an accident, making his position self-contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you find yourself disputing against truth while your own actions testify against your claim? What would it take for you to let the evidence of your own behavior correct your beliefs?
?
Ghazali shows that the sceptic's clamor is self-defeating. Are there areas in your life where your actions contradict your professed beliefs? Will you commit to aligning them starting today?
?
You have witnessed countless accidents today—your own thoughts, movements, emotions. Yet how easily do you slip into denial of the unseen realities of faith? What is one accident you will acknowledge as a sign of Allah's existence?

The First Claim: Proving God's Existence and Defining the World
Ghazali states the first claim, gives a proof, and defines the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's kalam proof for God's existence, his definition of the world as everything other than God, and how he refutes skeptical objections.

Core Idea
Every event needs a cause; the world is an event, so it has a cause — God.

Ghazali begins his theological project by establishing the first claim: that God exists. He presents a concise proof rooted in the observation that everything that comes into being requires a cause. To make this proof work, he carefully defines what he means by 'the world'—a term that includes all bodies and their accidents. He then addresses the skeptic who might deny the reality of bodies and accidents, using a clever argument that even the skeptic's own actions are accidents, thus proving their existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the proof structure
Notice how Ghazali presents the syllogism: every event has a cause, the world is an event, therefore it has a cause. Then he defines 'world' as everything other than God.
The proof is concise; pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Understand the division of existents
Ghazali divides existents into occupying space (atoms and bodies) and non-occupying (accidents and God). This classification underpins his definition of the world.
3
Analyze the refutation of sophistry
He argues that even the skeptic's disputation is an accident, proving the existence of accidents and bodies. Then he distinguishes things known by sense from things known by proof.
This demonstrates that the skeptic's own actions validate the very categories they deny.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
The passage concludes by stating that God's existence is known through proof, not sense, and calls for a return to verification of the proof.
This sets the stage for the rest of the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى الأولى وُجُودُه تَعَالى وتقدس وبرهانه : أنَّا نقول : كلُّ حادث فلحدوثِهِ سببٌ ، والعالم حادث ؛ فيلزم منه أنَّ له سبباً ونعني بالعالم : كلَّ موجود سوى الله تعالى ، ونعني بكل موجود سوى الله تعالى : الأجسام كلها ، وأعراضها وشرح ذلك كله بالتفصيل : أنا لا نشك في أصل الوجود ، ثم نعلم أن تقسيم الموجودات كل موجود : فإما متحيّر ، أو غير متحيز ، وأنَّ كلَّ متحيز : إن لم يكن فيه ائتلاف .. فنسميه جوهراً فرداً ، وإن ائتلف إلى غيره . . سميناه جسماً ، وأنَّ غير المتحيز : إما أن يستدعي وجوده جسماً يقوم به ونسميه الأعراض ، أو لا يستدعيه وهو الله سبحانه وتعالى السفسطة فأما ثبوتُ الأجسام وأعراضها .. فمعلوم بالمشاهدة ، ولا يُلتفت إلى من لا ينازع في الأعراض وإن طال فيها صياحه ، وأخذ يلتمس منك دليلاً عليه فإن شعبه ونزاعه والتماسه وصياحه إن لم يكن موجوداً .. فكيف يُشتغل بالجواب عنه والإصغاء إليه ؟! وإن كان موجوداً . . فهو - لا محالة - غيرُ جسم المنازع ؛ إذ كان جسمه موجوداً من قبل ولم يكن التنازع موجوداً ! فقد عرفت أنَّ الجسم والعرض مدركان بالمشاهدة . فأما موجود ليس بجسم ولا جوهر متحيّر ولا عَرَض .. فلا يدرك بالحس بل بالدليل ، ونحن ندعي وجوده ، وندعي أن العالم موجود به وبقدرته ، وهذا يدرك بالدليل لا بالحس، والدليل ما ذكرناه ، فلترجع إلى تحقيقه :
The first claim: His existence, may He be exalted and sanctified. Its proof: We say: every event has a cause for its occurrence, and the world is an event; therefore it follows that it has a cause. By 'world' we mean: every existent other than God Almighty, and by every existent other than God Almighty we mean: all bodies and their accidents. Explanation of all that in detail: We do not doubt the principle of existence; then we know that the division of existents: every existent is either occupying space or non-occupying space; and every occupant: if it does not admit composition, we call it an indivisible atom; if it composes with another, we call it a body; and the non-occupant: either its existence requires a body in which it inheres, and we call it an accident; or it does not require it, and that is God, glorified and exalted. Sophistry: As for the establishment of bodies and their accidents, it is known by observation, and no attention is paid to one who disputes concerning accidents even if his clamor is prolonged and he seeks from you a proof thereof, for his disputation, contention, request, and clamor—if it is not existent, then how can one occupy himself with answering it and listening to it? And if it is existent, then inevitably it is other than the body of the disputant, since his body existed before and the disputation was not existent. So you have known that body and accident are perceived by observation. As for an existent that is neither a body nor an occupying atom nor an accident, it is not perceived by sense but by proof. We claim its existence, and we claim that the world exists through Him and by His power. This is perceived by proof, not by sense, and the proof is what we mentioned. So let us return to its verification.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage begins the first claim: the existence of God. Ghazali presents the classical kalam proof: every event has a cause, the world is an event, so it has a cause. He then defines the world as everything other than God, consisting of bodies and their accidents. He expands on this by dividing existents into those that occupy space (atoms and bodies) and those that do not (accidents, which inhere in bodies, and God, who does not). He dismisses sophistry by arguing that even the skeptic's own acts of disputation are accidents, proving the existence of accidents and bodies, and then states that God's existence is known through proof, not sense. He concludes by calling for a return to the verification of the stated proof.

Key Terms
حادث
عالم
جسم
عرض
جوهر فرد
متحيز
سفسطة

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the definition of the 'world' (العالم) in this proof?
A) The physical universe including the heavens and earth.
B) Every existent other than God, comprising bodies and their accidents.
C) Only bodies, not accidents.
D) Everything that is perceived by the senses.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first claim establishes that the world, defined as everything other than God, is an event that must have a cause, which is God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every event has a cause, then your own existence is an event. Do you live as if your life has a purpose because it was caused by God? What would change if you truly believed that every moment of your existence is caused by Him?
?
Ghazali argues that even the skeptic's own disputation is an accident proving his point. When you argue against faith, do you realize that your very ability to argue is created by the One you deny? How does that confront your pride?
?
The world is defined as everything other than God. Is there anything in your heart that you love or fear more than God? That thing is part of the world and is caused by Him. Are you ready to let go of that attachment?

The Syllogism for the World's Contingency
Ghazali responds to an objection by stating the syllogism for the world's contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the syllogism Ghazali uses to prove the world's contingency and the two premises it depends on.

Core Idea
The world's contingency is proven by a simple syllogism: bodies never lack accidents, so they must be contingent.

Ghazali begins by acknowledging that the world's contingency is not self-evident but requires demonstration. He then constructs a syllogism from two principles: that every body is never free of contingent events, and that whatever is never free of contingent events is itself contingent. This lesson presents that syllogism and the initial objection it addresses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial objection
Read the first line where the opponent questions how Ghazali would challenge someone who disputes the second principle. Note how Ghazali responds by clarifying that the principle is not primary but provable.
2
Understand the syllogism's structure
Ghazali defines 'world' as bodies and substances. He then states two premises: (1) every body is never free of contingent events, (2) whatever is never free of contingent events is contingent. The conclusion follows that every body is contingent.
The two premises are the minor and major premises of a categorical syllogism.
3
Notice the rhetorical question
Ghazali ends by asking which premise the opponent disputes. This sets the stage for the subsequent objections and defenses in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : بِمَ تنكرونَ على من ينازع في الأصل الثاني وهو قولكم : إن العالم حادث ؟ تحريجة: فإن نوزعتم في كون العالم حادثاً؟ فنقول : هذا الأصل ليس بأوّلي ، بل نثبته ببرهان منظوم من أصلين آخرين ؛ وهو أنا نقول : إذا قلنا : إنَّ العالم حادث .. أردنا بالعالم الآن الأجسام والجواهر فقط ، فنقول : كلُّ جسم فلا يخلو عن الحوادث ، وكلُّ ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث ؛ فيلزم منه : أن كل جسم فهو حادث ، ففي أي الأصلين النزاع ؟ فإن قيل : لم قلتم : إنَّ كلَّ جسم أو متحيز فلا يخلو عن الحوادث ؟ تحريجة ولم يلازم قلنا : لأنَّها لا تخلو عن الحركة أو السكون ، وهما حادثان فإن قيل : ادعيتُم وجودهما ثم حدوثهما ولا الحدوث ؟ فلا نسلم لا الوجود تحريجة: لا تسلم قلنا : هذا سؤال قد طُول الجواب عنه في تصانيف الكلام، وليس يستحق هذا التطويل ؛ فإنَّه قطُّ لا يصدر عن مسترشد ؛ إذ لا يستريب عاقل قط في ثبوت الأعراض في ذاته ؛ من الآلام والأسقام ، والجوع والعطش ، وسائر الأحوال ، ولا في حدوثها ، وكذلك إذا نظر إلى أجسام العالم.. لم يسترب في تبدل الأحوال عليها ، وأنَّ تلك التبدلاتِ حادثة ، وإن صدر
If it is said: By what do you reject the one who disputes the second principle, which is your statement that the world is contingent? Objection: For if you were disputed concerning the world being contingent? We say: This principle is not primary, but we establish it by a syllogistic proof from two other principles; that is, we say: When we say 'the world is contingent', we mean by 'world' at present only bodies and substances. So we say: Every body is never free of contingent events, and whatever is never free of contingent events is itself contingent. It follows from this that every body is contingent. So which of the two principles is the dispute? If it is said: Why do you say that every body or spatial entity is never free of contingent events? Objection: And there is no necessary connection. We say: Because they are never free of motion or rest, and both are contingent events. If it is said: You claimed their existence and then their contingency, not the contingency? So we do not accept neither the existence nor the contingency? Objection: We do not accept. We say: This question has been answered at length in the books of kalām, and it does not deserve such elaboration; for it never issues from a seeker of guidance; since no rational person ever doubts the existence of accidents in bodies—such as pains, illnesses, hunger, thirst, and other states—nor their contingency. Likewise, when one looks at the bodies of the world, he does not doubt the changing of states upon them, and that those changes are contingent. And if...
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the second principle: the world is contingent. Ghazali first notes that this principle is not self-evident (awwalī) but requires demonstration. The proof is a syllogism: (1) Every body (jism) or spatial entity (mutaḥayyiz) is never free of contingent events (ḥawādith). (2) Whatever is never free of contingent events is itself contingent (ḥādith). Conclusion: every body is contingent, hence the world is contingent. Ghazali then addresses two objections. The first objection denies the minor premise that bodies are never free of accidents; the opponent claims no necessary connection. Ghazali responds by pointing to motion (ḥaraka) and rest (sukūn), both of which are contingent and unavoidable in bodies. The second objection denies the existence or contingency of these accidents altogether; the opponent refuses to accept either. Ghazali dismisses this as unreasonable, arguing that no rational person doubts the existence and contingency of observable accidents like pain, hunger, or change of states. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is clear: the contingency of the world is proven by the ubiquity of changing accidents in bodies.

Key Terms
جسم (jism)
جوهر (jawhar)
حادث (ḥādith)
برهان (burhān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world's contingency is self-evident and needs no proof.
Correction: Ghazali states that this principle is not primary (awwalī) but requires a syllogistic proof from two other principles.

Checkpoint
What is the major premise of Ghazali's syllogism to prove the world is contingent?
A) Every body is never free of contingent events.
B) Whatever is never free of contingent events is itself contingent.
C) The world is composed of bodies and substances.
D) Motion and rest are contingent events.

Key Takeaway
The world's contingency is demonstrated by a syllogism whose two premises are: (1) bodies always contain accidents, and (2) anything always containing accidents is itself contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before this lesson, did you assume the world's contingency was obvious, or did you rely on a proof? How much of your faith is based on intellectual certainty versus inherited belief?
?
Ghazali presents a syllogism to prove the world's need for a Creator. Are you willing to examine your own beliefs with the same logical rigor, even if it challenges comfortable assumptions?
?
If someone asked you tomorrow why you believe in God, would you be able to articulate this syllogism? Commit to memorizing it and using it.

Defending the Minor Premise: Bodies and Accidents
Ghazali defends the minor premise against two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defends the claim that bodies are never free of contingent events, and why he considers the denial of accidents unreasonable.

Core Idea
Bodies cannot escape accidents like motion or rest, which are obviously contingent.

After presenting the syllogism, Ghazali faces an objection: why must bodies always have accidents? And if they do, why are those accidents contingent? Ghazali gives a concise answer—bodies are never free of motion or rest—and then dismisses the deeper denial of accidents as irrational, citing observable examples like pain and hunger.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection
The opponent asks: why do you say every body or spatial entity is never free of contingent events? And they claim there is no necessary connection.
2
Note Ghazali's reply
Ghazali answers that bodies are never free of motion or rest, both of which are contingent events. This directly supplies the missing link.
Motion and rest are observable and unavoidable.
3
Understand the second objection
The opponent then denies the existence and/or contingency of motion and rest themselves. Ghazali considers this unreasonable.
4
See Ghazali's dismissal
Ghazali argues that no rational person doubts the existence of accidents like pain, hunger, and change of states, nor their contingency. He appeals to direct observation.
The passage ends abruptly, but the core defense is complete.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the second principle: the world is contingent. Ghazali first notes that this principle is not self-evident (awwalī) but requires demonstration. The Philosophers' Stance and the Key Principle of Temporal Origination
Ghazali identifies the philosophers as opponents and states their position, then introduces the key principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' position on the world's temporal origination and the key principle that underlies Ghazali's proof.

Core Idea
The philosophers accept change in the world but deny the world itself is originated.

This passage sets the stage for Ghazali's argument. He first identifies the philosophers as the main opponents and summarizes their view: they admit that all motions and changes are temporally originated, but claim that the underlying matter is eternal. The crux of the debate is the principle that anything never free from originated events must itself be originated. Ghazali then begins to apply this principle to the atom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp its flow. Notice the two main sections: the philosophers' view and the principle with its application.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث' – it is central.
2
Understand the philosophers' view
The philosophers divide the world into heavens (perpetual motion) and four elements (perpetual change of forms). They affirm individual motions and forms are originated, but consider the underlying matter (hylic) eternal.
They do not deny the origination of accidents, only the substance.
3
Identify the disputed principle
Ghazali states that the philosophers dispute the principle: 'Whatever never ceases to have temporally originated events is itself temporally originated.' This is the key logical tool.
This principle will be used to prove the world's origination.
4
See how the principle applies to the atom
Ghazali applies the principle to the atom (jawhar): it is never free from motion or rest, both originated. Therefore, the atom itself must be originated.
The argument uses the impossibility of the eternal ceasing to exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من خصم معاند . . فلا معنى للاشتغال به ، وإن فُرِضَ فيه خصم معتقد لما يقول فهو فرض محال إن كان الخصم عاقلاً الفلاسفة هم رأس الخصم في حَدَثِ العالم الفلاسفةُ ، وهم مصرحون بأنَّ أجسام العالم القائلين بقدم العالم تنقسم إلى السماوات وهي متحركة على الدوام، وواحد حركاتها حادثة ، ولكنها دائمةٌ متلاحقة على الاتصال أزلاً وأبداً ، وإلى العناصر الأربعة التي يحويها مقعرُ فَلَك القمر ، وهي تشترك في مادة حاملة لصورها وأعراضها ، (1) وتلك المادة قديمة ، والصور والأعراض حادثة ومتعاقبة عليها أزلاً وأبداً ، فإنَّ الماء ينقلب بالحرارة هواءً ، والهواء يستحيل بالحرارة ناراً وهكذا بقية العناصر ، وأنَّها تمتزج امتزاجات حادثة فيتكون منها المعادن والنبات والحيوان . لم ينكر الفلاسفة فلا تنفكُ العناصر عن هذه الصور الحادثة أبداً ، ولا تنفك السماوات عن الحركات الحادثة أبداً ، وإنما ينازعون في قولنا : ( إنَّ ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث ) ، فإذن لا معنى للإطناب في هذا الأصل ، ولكنَّا لإقامة الرسم نقول : الجوهر بالضرورة لا يخلو عن الحركة والسكون ، وهما حادثان ؛ أما الحركة .. فحدوثها محسوس ، وإنْ فُرِضَ جوهر ساكن كالأرض .. ففرضُ حركته ليس بمحال ، بل نعلم جوازة بالضرورة (٢) ، وإذا وقع ذلك الجائز . . كان حادثاً ، وكان مُعدِماً للسكون ، فيكون السكون أيضاً قبلَهُ حادثاً؛ لأن القديمَ لا يعدمُ ، كما سنذكرُهُ في إقامة الدليل على بقاء الله تعالى (۳)
From an obstinate opponent ... there is no point in occupying oneself with him. Even if one assumes an opponent who believes what he says, it is an impossible assumption if the opponent is rational. The philosophers are the head of the opponent regarding the temporal origination of the world. They explicitly state that the bodies of the world (those who affirm the world's pre-eternity) are divided into the heavens, which are perpetually moving, each of their motions being temporally originated, but they are continuous in succession eternally, and into the four elements contained by the concavity of the lunar sphere. These share a matter (hylic matter) that bears their forms and accidents. That matter is eternal, while the forms and accidents are temporally originated and succeed one another upon it eternally. Water turns into air by heat, air becomes fire by heat, and so on with the rest of the elements. They mix in temporally originated mixtures, forming minerals, plants, and animals. The philosophers do not deny the elements' never being free from these temporally originated forms, nor the heavens' never being free from the temporally originated motions. They only dispute our statement: 'Whatever never ceases to have temporally originated events is itself temporally originated.' Therefore, there is no point in elaborating on this principle. However, to establish the demonstration we say: The atom (jawhar) necessarily cannot be free from motion and rest, both of which are temporally originated. As for motion, its temporality is sensed. Even if one posits a stationary atom like the earth, positing its motion is not impossible; rather, we know its possibility by necessity. When that possible occurs, it is temporally originated and eliminates the preceding rest, so the rest before it is also temporally originated, because the eternal does not cease to exist, as we will mention when establishing the proof for the endurance of God (3).
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a definition-and-argument section. Ghazali begins by dismissing stubborn opponents but identifies the philosophers as the main adversaries regarding the world's temporal origination. He first describes their view: they accept that the heavens have perpetual motions (each individual motion is originated) and that the four elements undergo constant change of forms and accidents, but they consider the underlying matter (hylic) to be eternal. Thus, they do not deny that forms and accidents are originated; they deny that the substance itself is originated. The crucial point of contention is the principle: 'Whatever never ceases to have temporally originated events is itself temporally originated.' Ghazali says there is no need to elaborate on this principle, but to establish the demonstration he applies it: the atom (jawhar) must always have either motion or rest, both of which are originated. Motion's origination is empirically evident; even for a stationary body like the earth, motion is possible, and if it occurs it is originated and thus the preceding rest is also originated (since the eternal cannot be annihilated). This sets up the proof that the atom itself is temporally originated.

Key Terms
حدث العالم
ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث
الجوهر
الحركة
السكون
الهيولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers deny that any part of the world is temporally originated.
Correction: The philosophers actually admit that all motions, forms, and accidents are originated; they only claim that the underlying matter is eternal.

Checkpoint
What is the principle that the philosophers reject, according to Ghazali?
A) Motion is an accident.
B) Whatever never ceases to have temporally originated events is itself temporally originated.
C) The atom is composed of parts.
D) The world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers accept that all individual events are originated but claim the world's substance is eternal, so Ghazali counters with the principle that anything never without originated events is itself originated.

?
The philosophers accepted change in all observable things but denied that the world itself was created. Do you ever treat certain aspects of your life (your habits, identity, relationships) as if they are eternal and unchangeable, while ignoring that they are created and must return to God?
?
If every atom of your being is constantly changing and dependent on originated events, what does that mean for your sense of self-sufficiency? Are you truly living as someone who knows they are created every moment, or do you act as if you are self-sustaining? Commit to a specific act of worship today that reminds you of your dependence.

The Second Principle: Refuting the Objection of an Eternal World with Events
Ghazali states the second principle and refutes the objection that the eternal could be a locus for accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that an eternal world never free from events leads to the logical impossibility of an infinite past having come to an end.

Core Idea
An infinite past cannot be completed; therefore the world must be originated.

Ghazali has introduced his second principle: whatever is never free from events is itself originated. But an objector asks: Could the world be eternal while always containing originated events? Ghazali responds by showing that this assumption forces us to accept that an infinite sequence of past events has come to an end, which is logically impossible. This lesson examines the objection and Ghazali's first impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: the interlocutor questions why the world cannot be eternal (qadim) while still being a locus for originated events (hawadith).
This challenges the second principle directly.
2
Follow Ghazali's response
Ghazali argues that if the world were eternal and never free from events, then there would be an infinite number of past events. Since we are in the present, all those events must have elapsed.
This leads to the claim that an infinite series has been completed.
3
Recognize the contradiction
The idea that 'the infinite has terminated' is a clear absurdity (muhal). Grasp why this is the first impossibility Ghazali presents.
This is the foundation for two further impossibilities on the next page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحريجة : فلم لا فإن قيل : فقد بقي الأصل الثاني ، وهو قولكم : إنَّ ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث ، فما الدليل عليه ؟ يكون القديم محلاً للحوادث؟ قلنا : لأنَّ العالم لو كان قديماً مع أنه لا يخلو عن الحوادث.. لثبتت حوادث لا أول لها ، وللزم أن تكون دوراتُ الفلك غير متناهية الأعداد . وذلك محال ؛ لأنه يفضي إلى المحال ، وما يفضي إلى المحال فهو محال ، ونحن نبيِّن أنه يلزم عليه ثلاث محالات : الأول : أن ذلك لو ثبت .. لكان قد انقضى ما لا نهاية له ووقع الفراغ عنه وانتهى ، ولا فرق بين قولنا : انقضى وبين قولنا : انتهى ، ولا بين قولنا : تناهى ، فيلزم أن يقال : قد تناهى ما لا يتناهى ، ومن المحال البين
A challenge: Why not? For if it is said: 'The second principle remains, which is your saying: Whatever is not free from events (hawadith) is itself originated (hadith). So what is the proof for it? Could the eternal (qadim) be a locus for events?' We say: Because if the world were eternal while never free from events, then events with no first [occurrence] would be established, and the rotations of the celestial sphere would be infinite in number. That is impossible (muhal), because it leads to the impossible, and whatever leads to the impossible is impossible. We will show that three impossibilities follow from it: The first: that if that were established, then what is infinite would have passed away and been finished and ended. There is no difference between our saying 'passed away' and 'ended' nor between 'ended' and 'terminated'. So it would be said: that which is infinite has terminated, which is a clear impossibility.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer regarding the second principle of the argument for the world's origination. The interlocutor asks: Why cannot the world be eternal (qadim) while nevertheless always containing originated events? In other words, why must the attribute of not being free from events entail that the world itself is originated? Ghazali answers that if the world were eternal yet never without events, then the events themselves would have no beginning (lā awwal la-hā), leading to an infinite number of past celestial rotations. This is impossible (muhal) because it leads to contradictions. He then enumerates three specific impossibilities that follow. The first impossibility is that an infinite series of past events would have come to an end (in the present moment), meaning that an infinite has been completed or terminated. But 'infinite' by definition cannot be terminated. So asserting that an infinite number of past events has elapsed is contradictory. Ghazali insists that terms like 'passed away' (inqaḍā), 'ended' (intahā), and 'terminated' (tanāhā) all imply finitude. Thus the assumption leads to saying 'the infinite has terminated', which is a clear absurdity. This refutes the objection that the eternal could be a locus for originated events without itself being originated.

حادث
محل
لا نهاية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world could be eternal while always containing originated events without itself being originated.
Correction: Ghazali shows that if the world were eternal and never free from events, then an infinite number of past events would have elapsed, implying the completion of an infinite sequence – a contradiction.

Checkpoint

A) That the world would be finite in size.
B) That an infinite number of past events would have come to an end.
C) That events would be eternal like the world.
D) That the world would have no motion.

Key Takeaway
The assumption that an eternal world can host an infinite series of past events leads to the contradiction that the infinite has ended, proving the world must be originated.

?
When you feel overwhelmed by the chain of causes and effects in your life, do you ever act as if you are trapped in an endless cycle with no origin? How does Ghazali's proof that the universe had a beginning free you from that despair and point you to the Creator who started it all?

The Objection to the Second Principle and the Three Impossibilities
Ghazali presents an objection and his general answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the challenge posed to the second principle of kalam (that whatever is inseparable from contingencies is itself contingent) and Ghazali's response that an eternal world would lead to three impossible consequences.

Core Idea
If the world were eternal, an infinite past would have to exist—leading to three logical contradictions.

The defender of the world's eternity questions why the world cannot be eternal even though it contains contingent events. In response, Ghazali argues that if the world were eternal and always accompanied by contingencies, then an infinite number of past events would have occurred. This, he claims, leads to three impossibilities, which he will enumerate. This lesson introduces the objection and the overall structure of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening sentence where the opponent questions the second principle. Note that the opponent accepts the principle itself but asks why it cannot apply to an eternal world.
2
Understand Ghazali's answer
Ghazali says if the world were eternal yet inseparable from contingencies, then an infinite number of contingent events would have occurred. This is impossible because it leads to three impossibilities.
The core of the argument is that an infinite past is logically impossible.
3
Preview the three impossibilities
The passage ends with the announcement that three impossibilities follow. These will be detailed in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحريجة : فلم لا فإن قيل : فقد بقي الأصل الثاني وهو قولكم : إنَّ ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث ، فما الدليل عليه ؟ قلنا : لأنَّ العالم لو كان قديماً مع أنه لا يخلو عن الحوادث لثبتت حوادث لا أول لها ، وللزم أن تكون دوراتُ الفلك غير متناهية الأعداد . وذلك محال ؛ لأنه يفضي إلى المحال ، وما يفضي إلى المحال فهو محال ، ونحن نبيِّن أنه يلزم عليه ثلاث محالات : الأول : أن ذلك لو ثبت لكان قد انقضى ما لا نهاية له ووقع الفراغ عنه وانتهى ، ولا فرق بين قولنا : انقضى وبين قولنا : انتهى ، ولا بين قولنا : تناهى ، فيلزم أن يقال : قد تناهى ما لا يتناهى ، ومن المحال البين أن يتناهى ما لا يتناهى ، وأن ينتهي وينقضي ما لا يتناهى . الثاني : أنَّ دوراتِ الفَلَكِ إِنْ لم تكن متناهية فهي : إما شفع ، وإما وتر ، وإما لا شفع ولا وترّ ، وإما شفع ووتر معاً ، وهذه الأقسام الأربعة محال ، فالمُفضَى إليه محال ؛ إذ يستحيل عدد لا شفع ولا وتر ، أو شفع ووتر ؛ فإنَّ الشفعَ : هو الذي ينقسم قسمين متساويين كالعشرة مثلاً ، والوتر : هو الذي لا ينقسم قسمين متساويين كالسبعة مثلاً ، وكلُّ مركب من آحاد : إما أن ينقسم قسمين متساويين ، أو لا متساويين ، فأما أن يتصف بالانقسام وعدم الانقسام ، أو ينفك عنهما جميعاً .. فهو محال وباطل أن يكون شفعاً ؛ لأنَّ الشفعَ إنما لا يكون وتراً لأنه يعوزه واحد فإن انضاف إليه واحد .. صار وتراً ، فكيف أعوز الذي لا يتناهى واحد ؟! ومحال أن يكون وتراً ؛ لأن الوتر يصير شفعاً بواحد ، فبقي وتراً لأنَّه يعوزه ذلك الواحد ، فكيف أعوز الذي لا يتناهى واحد ؟! الثالث : أنه يلزم عليه أن يكون عددان كلُّ واحد منهما لا يتناهى ، ثم أحدُهُما أقل من الآخر ، ومحال أن يكون ما لا يتناهى أقل مما لا يتناهى ؛ لأن الأقل هو الذي يعوزه شيءٌ لو أُضيف إليه .. لصار متساوياً ، وما لا يتناهى كيف يعوزه شيء ؟! وبيانه : أنَّ زُحَلَ عندهم يدورُ في كلِّ ثلاثين سنة دورة واحدة ، والشمس في كل سنة دورة واحدة ، فيكون عدد دورات زحل مثل ثلث عُشْرِ دوراتِ الشمس ؛ إذ الشمس تدور في ثلاثين سنة ثلاثين دورة ، وزحل يدور دورة واحدة ، والواحد من الثلاثين ثلث عشر ، ثم دورات زحل لا نهاية.
Challenge: So why is it? If it is said: 'The second principle remains, which is your statement: Whatever is not free from contingencies is itself contingent. So what is the proof for it?' We say: Because if the world were eternal while being inseparable from contingencies, then contingencies with no beginning would be established, and the rotations of the celestial spheres would necessarily be infinite in number. That is impossible, because it leads to impossibility, and whatever leads to impossibility is impossible. And we will show that three impossibilities follow from it. The first: That if that were established, then what has no end would have passed away, been completed, and come to an end. And there is no difference between our saying 'passed away' and our saying 'ended', nor between our saying 'terminated'. So it would necessarily be said: 'What is infinite has become finite.' And it is an evident impossibility that the infinite should become finite, and that what is infinite should end and pass away. The second: That the rotations of the celestial sphere, if they are not finite, then they are either even, or odd, or neither even nor odd, or both even and odd. And these four divisions are impossible, so that which leads to it is impossible. For it is impossible for a number to be neither even nor odd, or both even and odd. For the even is that which can be divided into two equal parts, like ten for example; and the odd is that which cannot be divided into two equal parts, like seven for example. And every composite of units is either divisible into two equal parts or not; as for being characterized by division and non-division, or being free from both, that is impossible and false. It cannot be even, because the even is only not odd because it lacks one; if a one were added to it, it would become odd. So how could that which is infinite lack a one?! And it is impossible for it to be odd, because the odd becomes even by a one, so it remains odd only because it lacks that one. So how could that which is infinite lack a one?! The third: That it follows from it that there are two numbers each of which is infinite, and yet one of them is less than the other. And it is impossible for the infinite to be less than the infinite, because the less is that which lacks something such that if it were added to it, it would become equal. So how could the infinite lack something?! And its explanation: That Saturn, according to them, rotates once every thirty years, and the sun rotates once every year. So the number of rotations of Saturn is like one-thirtieth of the rotations of the sun; because the sun rotates thirty times in thirty years, and Saturn rotates once. And one-thirtieth is one-thirtieth. Then the rotations of Saturn are infinite...
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive objection-and-answer unit. It begins by stating the second principle (that whatever is inseparable from contingencies is itself contingent) and then presents an imagined objection: 'Why cannot the eternal (i.e., the world) be a locus for contingencies?' Ghazali's answer is that if the world were eternal yet always accompanied by contingencies (like motion and rest), then an infinite number of past events would have occurred. He then proves three logical impossibilities that would follow. The first impossibility is that an infinite series would have been completed and ended, which is contradictory because the infinite by definition cannot be completed. The second impossibility is based on the properties of even and odd numbers: an infinite number of celestial rotations would have to be either even, odd, neither, or both—all of which are impossible for an infinite set. The third impossibility is that two infinites (e.g., the number of Saturn's rotations and the sun's rotations) would both be infinite yet one would be less than the other, contradicting the nature of infinity. The argument is rigorous and follows the standard kalam method of reductio ad absurdum (ilzam).

قديم
محال
لا يتناهى

A) Whatever is inseparable from contingencies is itself contingent.
B) The world is eternal.
C) The world has a beginning.
D) God is one.

Key Takeaway
The core argument against the world's eternity is that an infinite past would be required, and that leads to three logical impossibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does knowing that only Allah is infinite and eternal give you a new perspective on the finitude of your own struggles? Will you entrust your finite burdens to the One who has no beginning?
?
Ghazali's argument shows that a world without a beginning leads to logical impossibility. What would it mean for you to accept that they, too, must have a beginning and an end? Are you willing to let them end today?

The First Impossibility: Completing the Infinite
Ghazali's first impossibility: the completion of an infinite series.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first argument that an infinite past cannot have been completed because that would entail the contradiction of an infinite series having an end.

Core Idea
An infinite series cannot be completed, so the past cannot be infinite.

In the previous lesson, Ghazali announced that an eternal world with contingent events would require an infinite number of past events. He now presents the first of three impossibilities: that such an infinite series would have to have ended (i.e., the present moment would be its completion), but it is impossible for the infinite to be completed or to come to an end. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the first impossibility
The Arabic text states that if the world were eternal, then an infinite number of past events would have reached the present, meaning the infinite has been completed. This is contradictory.
2
Understand the key terms
Note the words 'انقضى' (passed away), 'انتهى' (ended), 'تناهى' (terminated). All imply reaching a limit, which the infinite cannot do.
3
Reflect on the contradiction
The infinite by definition has no end. Saying it has ended is a direct contradiction. This is a simple but powerful argument.
This is a common kalam argument against an infinite past.

Ghazali's answer is that if the world were eternal yet always accompanied by contingencies (like motion and rest), then an infinite number of past events would have occurred. He then proves three logical impossibilities that would follow. The first impossibility is that an infinite series would have been completed and ended, which is contradictory because the infinite by definition cannot be completed. The second impossibility is based on the properties of even and odd numbers: an infinite number of celestial rotations would have to be either even, odd, neither, or both—all of which are impossible for an infinite set. The third impossibility is that two infinites (e.g., the number of Saturn's rotations and the sun's rotations) would both be infinite yet one would be less than the other, contradicting the nature of infinity. The argument is rigorous and follows the standard kalam method of reductio ad absurdum (ilzam).

انقضى
انتهى
تناهى
لا يتناهى

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that an infinite past is possible if time is linear and the universe is eternal.
Correction: Ghazali argues that if the past were infinite, it would have to have been completed to reach the present, which is self-contradictory.

Checkpoint

A) That an infinite number of events would be even and odd simultaneously.
B) That an infinite series would have to be completed, but the infinite cannot be completed.
C) That two infinites would be unequal.
D) That the world would have no beginning.

Key Takeaway
An actual infinite cannot be traversed or completed, so the past cannot be infinite.

The Second Impossibility: Even, Odd, and the Infinite
Ghazali's second impossibility: classification of infinite numbers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second argument that an infinite number of celestial rotations would have to be either even or odd, but an infinite number cannot be either, leading to a contradiction.

Core Idea
An infinite number cannot be even or odd, yet it must be one of them, so infinite rotations are impossible.

Ghazali's second argument against an eternal world focuses on the number of rotations of the celestial spheres. If the world is eternal, then an infinite number of rotations have occurred. But every number is either even or odd. However, an infinite number cannot be even (because it cannot be increased by one to become odd) nor odd (because it cannot be decreased by one to become even). Thus, the assumption of an infinite number of rotations leads to a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the categories
Ghazali enumerates four possibilities: even, odd, neither, or both. He argues that the latter two are impossible by definition, and the former two are impossible for an infinite number.
2
Examine the even/odd definitions
Even numbers can be divided into two equal parts; odd numbers cannot. An even number becomes odd when one is added; an odd becomes even when one is added.
3
Apply to the infinite
An infinite number cannot be increased or decreased by one, so it cannot be even or odd. Therefore, all four options are impossible, which means the assumption of an infinite number is false.
This is a classic argument against actual infinites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحريجة : فلم لا فإن قيل : فقد بقي الأصل الثاني وهو قولكم : إنَّ ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث ، فما الدليل عليه ؟ قلنا : لأنَّ العالم لو كان قديماً مع أنه لا يخلو عن الحوادث لثبتت حوادث لا أول لها ، وللزم أن تكون دوراتُ الفلك غير متناهية الأعداد . وذلك محال ؛ لأنه يفضي إلى المحال ، وما يفضي إلى المحال فهو محال ، ونحن نبيِّن أنه يلزم عليه ثلاث محالات : الأول : أن ذلك لو ثبت لكان قد انقضى ما لا نهاية له ووقع الفراغ عنه وانتهى ، ولا فرق بين قولنا : انقضى وبين قولنا : انتهى ، ولا بين قولنا : تناهى ، فيلزم أن يقال : قد تناهى ما لا يتناهى ، ومن المحال البين أن يتناهى ما لا يتناهى ، وأن ينتهي وينقضي ما لا يتناهى . الثاني : أنَّ دوراتِ الفَلَكِ إِنْ لم تكن متناهية فهي : إما شفع ، وإما وتر ، وإما لا شفع ولا وترّ ، وإما شفع ووتر معاً ، وهذه الأقسام الأربعة محال ، فالمُفضَى إليه محال ؛ إذ يستحيل عدد لا شفع ولا وتر ، أو شفع ووتر ؛ فإنَّ الشفعَ : هو الذي ينقسم قسمين متساويين كالعشرة مثلاً ، والوتر : هو الذي لا ينقسم قسمين متساويين كالسبعة مثلاً ، وكلُّ مركب من آحاد : إما أن ينقسم قسمين متساويين ، أو لا متساويين ، فأما أن يتصف بالانقسام وعدم الانقسام ، أو ينفك عنهما جميعاً .. فهو محال وباطل أن يكون شفعاً ؛ لأنَّ الشفعَ إنما لا يكون وتراً لأنه يعوزه واحد فإن انضاف إليه واحد .. صار وتراً ، فكيف أعوز الذي لا يتناهى واحد ؟! ومحال أن يكون وتراً ؛ لأن الوتر يصير شفعاً بواحد ، فبقي وتراً لأنَّه يعوزه ذلك الواحد ، فكيف أعوز الذي لا يتناهى واحد ؟! الثالث : أنه يلزم عليه أن يكون عددان كلُّ واحد منهما لا يتناهى ، ثم أحدُهُما أقل من الآخر ، ومحال أن يكون ما لا يتناهى أقل مما لا يتناهى ؛ لأن الأقل هو الذي يعوزه شيءٌ لو أُضيف إليه .. لصار متساوياً ، وما لا يتناهى كيف يعوزه شيء ؟! وبيانه : أنَّ زُحَلَ عندهم يدورُ في كلِّ ثلاثين سنة دورة واحدة ، والشمس في كل سنة دورة واحدة ، فيكون عدد دورات زحل مثل ثلث عُشْرِ دوراتِ الشمس ؛ إذ الشمس تدور في ثلاثين سنة ثلاثين دورة ، وزحل يدور دورة واحدة ، والواحد من الثلاثين ثلث عشر ، ثم دورات زحل لا نهاية.
Challenge: So why is it? If it is said: 'The second principle remains, which is your statement: Whatever is not free from contingencies is itself contingent. So what is the proof for it?' We say: Because if the world were eternal while being inseparable from contingencies, then contingencies with no beginning would be established, and the rotations of the celestial spheres would necessarily be infinite in number. That is impossible, because it leads to impossibility, and whatever leads to impossibility is impossible. And we will show that three impossibilities follow from it. The first: That if that were established, then what has no end would have passed away, been completed, and come to an end. And there is no difference between our saying 'passed away' and our saying 'ended', nor between our saying 'terminated'. So it would necessarily be said: 'What is infinite has become finite.' And it is an evident impossibility that the infinite should become finite, and that what is infinite should end and pass away. The second: That the rotations of the celestial sphere, if they are not finite, then they are either even, or odd, or neither even nor odd, or both even and odd. And these four divisions are impossible, so that which leads to it is impossible. For it is impossible for a number to be neither even nor odd, or both even and odd. For the even is that which can be divided into two equal parts, like ten for example; and the odd is that which cannot be divided into two equal parts, like seven for example. And every composite of units is either divisible into two equal parts or not; as for being characterized by division and non-division, or being free from both, that is impossible and false. It cannot be even, because the even is only not odd because it lacks one; if a one were added to it, it would become odd. So how could that which is infinite lack a one?! And it is impossible for it to be odd, because the odd becomes even by a one, so it remains odd only because it lacks that one. So how could that which is infinite lack a one?! The third: That it follows from it that there are two numbers each of which is infinite, and yet one of them is less than the other. And it is impossible for the infinite to be less than the infinite, because the less is that which lacks something such that if it were added to it, it would become equal. So how could the infinite lack something?! And its explanation: That Saturn, according to them, rotates once every thirty years, and the sun rotates once every year. So the number of rotations of Saturn is like one-thirtieth of the rotations of the sun; because the sun rotates thirty times in thirty years, and Saturn rotates once. And one-thirtieth is one-thirtieth. Then the rotations of Saturn are infinite...
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive objection-and-answer unit. It begins by stating the second principle (that whatever is inseparable from contingencies is itself contingent) and then presents an imagined objection: 'Why cannot the eternal (i.e., the world) be a locus for contingencies?' Ghazali's answer is that if the world were eternal yet always accompanied by contingencies (like motion and rest), then an infinite number of past events would have occurred. He then proves three logical impossibilities that would follow. The first impossibility is that an infinite series would have been completed and ended, which is contradictory because the infinite by definition cannot be completed. The second impossibility is based on the properties of even and odd numbers: an infinite number of celestial rotations would have to be either even, odd, neither, or both—all of which are impossible for an infinite set. The third impossibility is that two infinites (e.g., the number of Saturn's rotations and the sun's rotations) would both be infinite yet one would be less than the other, contradicting the nature of infinity. The argument is rigorous and follows the standard kalam method of reductio ad absurdum (ilzam).

Misconception: Some think that an infinite number can be considered even or odd.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the definitions of even and odd rely on the concept of adding or subtracting one, which does not apply to an actual infinite.

Key Takeaway

The Third Impossibility: Unequal Infinites
Ghazali's third impossibility: unequal infinite numbers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's third argument that two infinite series (e.g., Saturn's rotations and the sun's rotations) would be unequal in magnitude, which is impossible for actual infinites.

Core Idea
Two actual infinites cannot be unequal, but an eternal world would require that, so the world cannot be eternal.

In this final argument, Ghazali uses the example of planetary rotations to show that if the world were eternal, then the rotation counts of different planets would be both infinite yet one would be a fraction of the other. For instance, Saturn makes one rotation every thirty years, while the sun makes one every year. Over an infinite past, both numbers would be infinite, but one would be thirty times smaller than the other. This is contradictory because one infinite cannot be less than another.

Guided Path
1
Understand the example
Ghazali compares the rotations of Saturn and the sun. Since Saturn takes 30 years per rotation and the sun takes 1 year, in any finite period, the sun's rotations are 30 times Saturn's. Over an infinite past, both would be infinite, yet the ratio would still hold, so one infinite would be a fraction of the other.
2
Identify the contradiction
If both numbers are infinite, they should be equal in magnitude, but the ratio implies they are not. This is impossible.
This argument challenges the notion that actual infinites can be compared in size.
3
Apply the general principle
The core principle is that an actual infinite cannot be less than another actual infinite. If the world were eternal, such a situation would arise, so it is impossible.

Second Proof: Impossibility of Infinite Rotations via Even-Odd
Ghazali enumerates four logical possibilities for an infinite number and shows each is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's logical argument that infinite celestial rotations lead to a contradiction using the even-odd classification of numbers.

Core Idea
Infinite numbers cannot be even or odd, so celestial rotations cannot be infinite.

Imam al-Ghazali presents a second proof against the possibility of an infinite regress of celestial motions. He uses a purely logical argument based on the properties of numbers: any number must be either even or odd, but an infinite number cannot be either. This shows that the assumption of infinite past rotations is contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice how Ghazali sets up the argument with four possibilities.
Pay attention to the definitions of even and odd.
2
Identify the four categories
List the four logical possibilities Ghazali considers for an infinite number: even, odd, neither, or both.
These four cover all logical possibilities.
3
Understand why each is impossible
For each category, follow Ghazali's reasoning: why an infinite number cannot be even, odd, neither, or both. Focus on the idea that an infinite number cannot lack a single unit.
The key point: infinity cannot be missing anything.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since all four possibilities are impossible, the original assumption that rotations are infinite must be false. Consider the import of this proof for divine existence.
This proof supports the finitude of the past, necessary for creation.

The second: That the rotations of the sphere, if they are not finite, then they are either even, or odd, or neither even nor odd, or both even and odd simultaneously. And these four divisions are impossible, so what leads to them is impossible. For it is impossible for a number to be neither even nor odd, or both even and odd. For the even is that which is divisible into two equal parts, like ten for example. And the odd is that which is not divisible into two equal parts, like seven for example. And every composite of units is either divisible into two equal parts or not. As for being characterized by both divisibility and non-divisibility, or being free from both, that is impossible. And it is false that it is even, because the even is only not odd because it lacks one. If one is added to it, it becomes odd. So how can that which has no end lack one? And it is impossible that it be odd, because the odd becomes even by one, and it remains odd because it lacks that one. So how can that which has no end lack one?
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the infinite regress of celestial rotations. Ghazali begins by stating the assumption: if the rotations are not finite (i.e., infinite in number), then any such number must fall into one of four categories: even, odd, neither even nor odd, or both even and odd simultaneously. He then declares all four impossible. He explains the definitions: an even number is divisible into two equal parts (e.g., ten), and an odd number is not (e.g., seven). Every number composed of units must be either even or odd; it cannot be neither or both. He then demonstrates why an infinite number cannot be even: an even number lacks one to become odd, but an infinite number cannot lack anything. Similarly, it cannot be odd because an odd number lacks one to become even. Since an infinite number cannot lack a unit, it cannot be odd either. Thus, the assumption leads to a contradiction, proving that the number of rotations cannot be infinite.

Key Terms
دورات الفلك (celestial rotations)
شفع (even)
وتر (odd)
محال (impossible)
لا يتناهى (infinite/without end)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument relies on astronomical observations about celestial motions.
Correction: The argument is purely logical, based on the mathematical properties of numbers, not empirical data.

Checkpoint
How many possible categories of number does Ghazali consider for infinite rotations?
A) Two: even and odd
B) Three: even, odd, and both
C) Four: even, odd, neither, and both
D) One: only infinite

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's second proof shows that infinite celestial rotations are logically impossible because the number of rotations would have to be either even or odd, but an infinite number can be neither.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How many beliefs do you hold simply because you've never examined them logically? Are you willing to apply the same rigorous scrutiny Ghazali uses here to any belief in your life that might be irrational? What would it cost you to let go of a cherished but unfounded assumption?
?
This proof shows that an actual infinite cannot exist. In what ways do you live as if your problems, worries, or sins are 'infinite'—overwhelming and never-ending? How would your perspective change if you truly believed that every finite difficulty has a finite resolution, just as every finite number has a definite property?
?
If the universe were eternal, as some claim, then the past would contain an infinite number of events—a logical impossibility according to this proof. Do you rely on rational proofs like this to affirm your faith, or do you depend more on feelings and inherited beliefs? Examine your heart: are you willing to submit your intellect to the truth, even if it challenges a long-held comfort zone?
