The Impossibility of Inequality Among Infinites
Ghazali's third proof: inequality of infinites leads to impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's third proof against infinite celestial rotations, which demonstrates that assuming infinite rotations leads to the logical contradiction of one infinite quantity being less than another.

Core Idea
No infinite can be less than another infinite.

In this passage, Ghazali presents his third argument against the theory of infinite celestial rotations. He argues that if rotations were infinite, we would have to accept that one infinite number is less than another—an impossibility. By using simple astronomical ratios, he demonstrates the absurdity and reinforces the need for a finite beginning to the universe.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Read the passage to find Ghazali's main assertion: that assuming infinite rotations leads to two infinite numbers where one is less than the other, which is impossible.
The key phrase is 'محال أن يكون ما لا يتناهى أقل مما لا يتناهى'.
2
Understand the definition of 'less'
Ghazali defines 'less' as that which lacks something to become equal. Reflect on why an infinite cannot lack anything.
This definition is crucial for the contradiction.
3
Follow the astronomical example
Trace Ghazali's example: Saturn (30-year orbit), Sun (1-year orbit), Moon (12 orbits/year). Note the ratios (1/30, 1/12) and how each set is infinite yet a fraction of another.
The exact ratios are not important; the logical structure matters.
4
Grasp the contradiction
Conclude that since each set is infinite, yet one is a fraction of another, we would have two infinites where one is less. This is impossible, so the premise of infinite rotations is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أنه يلزم عليه أن يكون عددان كلُّ واحد منهما لا يتناهى ، ثم أحدُهُما أقل من الآخر ، ومحال أن يكون ما لا يتناهى أقل مما لا يتناهى ؛ لأن الأقل هو الذي يعوزه شيءٌ لو أُضيف إليه .. لصار متساوياً ، وما لا يتناهى كيف يعوزه شيء ؟! وبيانه : أنَّ زُحَلَ عندهم يدورُ في كلِّ ثلاثين سنة دورة واحدة ، والشمس في كل سنة دورة واحدة ، فيكون عدد دورات زحل مثل ثلث عُشْرِ دوراتِ الشمس ؛ إذ الشمس تدور في ثلاثين سنة ثلاثين دورة ، وزحل يدور دورة واحدة ، والواحد من الثلاثين ثلث عشر ، ثم دورات زحل لا نهاية لها وهي أقل من دورات الشمس ؛ إذ يعلم ضرورة : أن ثلث الشيء أقل من الشيء عشرِ والقمر يدور في السنة اثنتي عشرة مرةً ، فيكون عدد دورات الشمس مثلاً نصف سدس دوراتِ القمر ، وكلُّ واحد لا نهاية له ، وبعضُه أقل من بعض ، وذلك من المحال البينِ
The third: That it follows from it that there are two numbers, each of which is infinite, then one of them is less than the other. And it is impossible for what is infinite to be less than what is infinite, because the less is that which lacks something such that if it were added to it, it would become equal. And how can what is infinite lack something? Its explanation: that Saturn, according to them, rotates once every thirty years, and the Sun rotates once every year. So the number of Saturn's rotations is like one-thirtieth of the Sun's rotations. For the Sun rotates thirty times in thirty years, and Saturn rotates once, and one out of thirty is one-thirtieth. Then Saturn's rotations are infinite and they are less than the Sun's rotations, for it is known necessarily that one-thirtieth of something is less than that thing. And the Moon rotates twelve times in a year, so the number of the Sun's rotations is, for example, one-half of one-sixth of the Moon's rotations, and each one is infinite, and some of it is less than some, and that is manifestly impossible.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage contains the third proof against infinite celestial rotations. Ghazali argues that if rotations are infinite, then we would have two infinite numbers where one is less than the other—an impossibility. The reasoning: a smaller number lacks some amount to equal the larger; but an infinite number cannot lack anything. He illustrates with astronomical examples: Saturn orbits once every 30 years, the Sun once per year, and the Moon 12 times per year. Thus Saturn's total rotations over an infinite time are 1/30 of the Sun's rotations, and the Sun's are 1/6 of the Moon's (since half a sixth is one-twelfth? Actually he says 'number of Sun's rotations is like half a sixth of the Moon's' meaning 1/12?). Each set (Saturn's, Sun's, Moon's) is infinite, yet one is a fraction of another. Since an infinite cannot be less than another infinite, this is a contradiction, proving the impossibility of infinite rotations.

Key Terms
عددان لا يتناهيان
أقل
زحل
الشمس
القمر
محال بين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof depends on the specific astronomical periods being accurate.
Correction: Ghazali uses conventional periods merely as examples; the logical point is about ratios between infinite sets, not astronomical precision.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali conclude from comparing Saturn's and Sun's rotations?
A) Both are infinite and equal
B) Saturn's rotations are more than Sun's
C) Both are infinite but one is a fraction of the other, which is impossible
D) The ratios are unknown

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that the assumption of infinite rotations leads to the logical impossibility of one infinite being less than another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali didn't shy away from following a logical argument to its conclusion, even when it contradicted common belief (eternal rotations). Is there a belief about God or the world you hold that, if you truly followed reason, would collapse? What would it cost you to abandon it? Are you willing to pay that price today?
?
The impossibility of one infinite being less than another seems abstract, but it exposes that not all plausible ideas survive rational scrutiny. In your own life, are you tolerating small inconsistencies—in your actions, your worship, your thoughts—that you know deep down are irrational? Will you correct one of them before the day ends?
?
If the universe cannot be pre-eternal, then it has a beginning, and that beginning points to a Creator. Have you truly internalized that your existence is not accidental but created with purpose? What one change would reflect that conviction in your daily routine starting tomorrow?

The Second Claim: Proof of the Eternal Creator
Ghazali argues for the eternity of the world's maker by rejecting infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the cause of the world must be eternal, and why infinite regress is impossible.

Core Idea
If the world's maker were temporal, the causal chain would never end.

Ghazali presents the second claim: the Maker of the world is eternal. He argues that if the cause were temporal, it would need another cause, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Therefore, the chain must terminate in an eternal being. This passage lays the foundation for understanding God's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and the translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow from the claim to the conclusion.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ويتسلسل' – it indicates the regress.
2
Identify the claim and the premise
The claim is that the Maker is eternal. The premise is: if the Maker were temporal, it would need another cause.
3
Follow the regress argument
If every cause is temporal, you get an infinite regress. Ghazali says this is impossible. Then the only alternative is an eternal cause.
Think of a chain of causes. Why can't it go on forever?
4
Understand the conclusion
The regress must terminate in an eternal being, which we call the Maker. This is necessary and undeniable.
Ghazali says 'لا بدَّ من الاعتراف به بالضرورة' – it is necessarily acknowledged.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى الثانية: صانع العالم قديم. لا ندعي أنَّ السبب الذي أثبتناه لوجود العالم قديم؛ فإنه لو كان حادثاً لافتقر إلى سبب آخر، وكذا ذلك السببُ الآخرُ، ويتسلسل إما إلى غير أول له نهاية وهو محال، وإما أن ينتهي إلى قديم لا محالة يقف عنده، وهو الذي نطلبه ونسميه صانع العالم، ولا بدَّ من الاعتراف به بالضرورة. ولا نعني بقولنا (قديم) إلا أنَّ وجودَهُ غير مسبوق بعدم، فليس تحت لفظ (القديم) إلا إثبات موجود ونفي عدم سابق. القدم صفة سلبية، فلا تظننَّ أنَّ القدمَ معنى زائد على ذات القديم(١)، فيلزمَك أن تقول: ذلك المعنى أيضاً قديم بقدم زائد عليه، ويتسلسل إلى غير نهاية. (١) بل القدم صفة سلبية ترفع ضدَّها كما يعبر عنها علماء هذا الفن، وهذا هو سبب الزيادة. نفي.
The second claim: The maker of the world is eternal. We do not claim that the cause we have proven for the existence of the world is temporal, for if it were temporal, it would need another cause, and likewise that other cause, and so on, either ad infinitum which is impossible, or it must terminate in an eternal being necessarily, at which it stops, and that is what we seek and call the Maker of the world, and it is necessary to acknowledge it necessarily. And we do not mean by our saying 'eternal' except that its existence is not preceded by non-existence. Thus under the word 'the Eternal' there is nothing except affirming an existent and negating previous non-existence. Eternity is a negative attribute, so do not think that eternity is an additional meaning superadded to the essence of the Eternal, otherwise you would be compelled to say that that meaning is also eternal by an eternity superadded to it, and so on ad infinitum. (1) Rather, eternity is a negative attribute that removes its opposite, as the scholars of this art express it, and this is the reason for the addition 'negation'.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents the second claim in a series of proofs: that the Maker of the world is eternal. It begins by stating the claim and then argues that if the cause of the world were itself temporal (hadith), it would require another cause, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Therefore, the chain must terminate in an eternal cause, which is what we call the Maker. The author then defines 'eternal' (qadim) as simply meaning that its existence is not preceded by non-existence. He emphasizes that 'eternity' is not an additional attribute superadded to the essence; rather, it is a negative attribute (sifah salbiyyah) that merely negates prior non-existence. He warns against the misconception that eternity is a positive attribute distinct from the essence, for that would lead to an infinite regress of attributing eternity to each successive attribute.

Key Terms
قديم
حادث
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maker of the world could be temporal (created) and still be the first cause.
Correction: If the Maker were temporal, it would require another cause, leading to an impossible infinite regress. Therefore, the Maker must be eternal.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what happens if the Maker of the world were temporal?
A) It would still be the first cause because nothing caused it
B) It would need another cause, leading to an infinite regress
C) It would be eternal by definition
D) It would be a self-caused being

Key Takeaway
The Maker of the world must be eternal because an infinite regress of temporal causes is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were not eternal, what would that mean for your certainty that He will never change His promises? How does this argument anchor your trust in His unchanging nature?
?
Reflect on a time you relied on something temporal (a person, status, or thing) as if it were permanent. How does the impossibility of infinite regress expose the futility of placing your ultimate hope in created things?
?
Ghazali says the chain must terminate in an eternal Being. Do you live as if your life's meaning terminates in something finite? What would change if you truly believed your Maker is the only necessary stop?

Defining 'Eternal' as a Negative Attribute
Ghazali defines 'eternal' as a negative attribute and refutes the notion of an added attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'eternal' as a negative attribute and why it cannot be a positive attribute superadded to the essence.

Core Idea
Eternal means simply 'not preceded by non-existence' – a negation, not a positive addition.

Having established that the Creator is eternal, Ghazali clarifies exactly what 'eternal' means. He defines it as existence not preceded by non-existence, and warns against thinking that eternity is an additional attribute. This clarification prevents a potential infinite regress in attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of 'eternal'
Read the passage: 'ولا نعني بقولنا (قديم) إلا أنَّ وجودَهُ غير مسبوق بعدم'. Notice the negation: 'not preceded by non-existence'.
The Arabic phrase 'غير مسبوق بعدم' is key.
2
Understand the negative attribute concept
Ghazali says 'القدم صفة سلبية' – eternity is a negative attribute. It does not add a positive quality but removes its opposite (non-existence).
A negative attribute only negates something; it does not affirm a separate quality.
3
Examine the warning against positive addition
Ghazali warns not to think eternity is an extra meaning added to the essence. If it were, that added meaning would also need to be eternal, leading to an infinite regress.
This is a common pitfall: reifying negative attributes as positive.
4
Reflect on the significance
By defining eternity purely negatively, Ghazali avoids multiplying attributes and keeps God's essence simple.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى الثانية: صانع العالم قديم. لا ندعي أنَّ السبب الذي أثبتناه لوجود العالم قديم؛ فإنه لو كان حادثاً لافتقر إلى سبب آخر، وكذا ذلك السببُ الآخرُ، ويتسلسل إما إلى غير أول له نهاية وهو محال، وإما أن ينتهي إلى قديم لا محالة يقف عنده، وهو الذي نطلبه ونسميه صانع العالم، ولا بدَّ من الاعتراف به بالضرورة. ولا نعني بقولنا (قديم) إلا أنَّ وجودَهُ غير مسبوق بعدم، فليس تحت لفظ (القديم) إلا إثبات موجود ونفي عدم سابق. القدم صفة سلبية، فلا تظننَّ أنَّ القدمَ معنى زائد على ذات القديم(١)، فيلزمَك أن تقول: ذلك المعنى أيضاً قديم بقدم زائد عليه، ويتسلسل إلى غير نهاية. (١) بل القدم صفة سلبية ترفع ضدَّها كما يعبر عنها علماء هذا الفن، وهذا هو سبب الزيادة. نفي.
The second claim: The maker of the world is eternal. We do not claim that the cause we have proven for the existence of the world is temporal, for if it were temporal, it would need another cause, and likewise that other cause, and so on, either ad infinitum which is impossible, or it must terminate in an eternal being necessarily, at which it stops, and that is what we seek and call the Maker of the world, and it is necessary to acknowledge it necessarily. And we do not mean by our saying 'eternal' except that its existence is not preceded by non-existence. Thus under the word 'the Eternal' there is nothing except affirming an existent and negating previous non-existence. Eternity is a negative attribute, so do not think that eternity is an additional meaning superadded to the essence of the Eternal, otherwise you would be compelled to say that that meaning is also eternal by an eternity superadded to it, and so on ad infinitum. (1) Rather, eternity is a negative attribute that removes its opposite, as the scholars of this art express it, and this is the reason for the addition 'negation'.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents the second claim in a series of proofs: that the Maker of the world is eternal. It begins by stating the claim and then argues that if the cause of the world were itself temporal (hadith), it would require another cause, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Therefore, the chain must terminate in an eternal cause, which is what we call the Maker. The author then defines 'eternal' (qadim) as simply meaning that its existence is not preceded by non-existence. He emphasizes that 'eternity' is not an additional attribute superadded to the essence; rather, it is a negative attribute (sifah salbiyyah) that merely negates prior non-existence. He warns against the misconception that eternity is a positive attribute distinct from the essence, for that would lead to an infinite regress of attributing eternity to each successive attribute.

Key Terms
قديم
قدم
صفة سلبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternity is a positive attribute distinct from God's essence.
Correction: Eternity is only a negation of prior non-existence; it does not add anything to the essence.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say is the meaning of 'eternal' (qadim)?
A) Existence that has no end
B) Existence not preceded by non-existence
C) Possessing an attribute of eternity
D) Being the first cause in a chain

Key Takeaway
Eternity is a negative attribute: it only negates prior non-existence, not a positive quality added to God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attribute of 'eternal', do you treat it as a mere concept or as a reality that means He has always existed and will always exist, with no beginning or end? How does this truth shape the urgency of your worship today?
?
Ghazali warns against imagining eternity as an added quality. In your own life, do you add qualities to God that He did not reveal, perhaps based on your own reasoning or cultural expectations? What step can you take this week to purify your understanding of God's attributes?
?
The infinite regress argument shows that if we do not stop with revelation, we end in absurdity. Where in your spiritual journey are you tempted to follow your own logic beyond what Allah has taught? Commit today to submit to the revealed definitions, even when they challenge your imagination.

The Creator Is Eternal in the Future
Ghazali proves the Creator's permanence and refutes the possibility of annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's proof that the Creator is eternal in the future (baqi) and why non-existence cannot be an effect of divine power.

Core Idea
If something is eternal in the past, it must be permanent in the future.

After establishing that the Creator is eternal in the past (qadim), Ghazali turns to the future: is He also eternal in the future? In this passage, he presents a rational proof that whatever is eternal cannot cease to exist, because its non-existence would require a cause—and such a cause cannot be divine power. This leads to a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position on the non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow. Notice the structure: a claim, then a proof, then an analogy, then an enumeration of possible causes, and finally a rejection of power as a cause.
Pay attention to the term 'طار' (new event) and the analogy with the preponderator.
2
Understand the main proof
Ghazali argues that if the Creator were to cease to exist, that non-existence would be a new event (طار) after eternal existence. Every new event needs a cause. Therefore, non-existence must have a cause.
This is based on the principle that everything that arises (hadith) requires a cause.
3
Analyze the analogy of preponderator
Just as the transition from non-existence to existence requires a preponderator (مرجح) for existence, the opposite transition requires a preponderator for non-existence. This establishes symmetry.
The preponderator for non-existence could be an agent, an opposite, or the interruption of a condition.
4
Examine why power cannot be the cause
Ghazali argues that non-existence is nothing (ليس بشيء), so it cannot be an effect of power. An agent who destroys would be doing something nonexistent, which is contradictory.
This refutes the idea that God could directly annihilate by His power.
5
Reflect on the Mu'tazilite objection
The Mu'tazilites claim that the non-existent (ma'dum) is a thing and an essence. Ghazali counters that even if so, that essence is not an effect of power, so the argument stands.
This shows Ghazali's engagement with opposing theological schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى الثالثة: ندعي أنَّ صانع العالم مع كونه موجوداً لم يزل فهو باقي لا يزال؛ لأن ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه. وإنما قلنا ذلك لأنه لو انعدم لافتقر عدمُهُ إلى سبب؛ فإنه طار (١) بعد استمرار الوجود في القدم، وقد ذكرنا أن كل طارٍ فلا بد له من سبب من حيث إنه طار، لا من حيث إنه موجود. وكما افتقر تبدُّلُ العدم بالوجود إلى مرجِّح للوجود على العدم.. فكذلك يفتقر تبدل الوجود بالعدم إلى مرجِّح للعدم على الوجود (٢). وذلك المرجِّح: إما فاعل يُعْدِمُ بالقدرة، أو ضدٌّ، أو انقطاع شرط من شرائط الوجود. ومحال أن يُحال على القدرة (٣)؛ إذ الوجود شيء ثابت يجوز أن يصدر عنه الإعدام بالقدرة، فيكون القادرُ باستعماله فَعَلَ شيئاً عدمياً، والعدم ليس بشيء.. فيستحيل أن يكون فعلاً واقعاً بأثر القدرة، فإنا نقول: فاعل العدم هل فعل شيئاً؟ فإن قيل: نعم.. كان محالاً؛ لأنَّ النفي ليس بشيء (٤). وإن قال المعتزلي: إن المعدوم شيء وذات.. فليس ذلك الذات من أثر القدرة. (١) أي: لم يكن فكان، وأصلها (طارى) سهلت الهمزة فانقلبت ياء، فعوملت الكلمة معاملة المنقوص. (٢) ولعل الفقرة: (وكما افتقر تبدل الوجود بالعدم إلى مرجح للوجود على العدم.. فكذلك يفتقر تبدلُ العدم بالوجود إلى مرجح للعدم على الوجود) وذلك لأن الباء إنما تدخل على المتروك الزائل. (٣) والمعنى: لا تصح إحالة المرجح للعدم على القدرة. (٤) في هامش (و): (اعلم: أن الوجود إثبات، والعدم نفي)
The third claim: We claim that the Maker of the world, while being existent without ceasing, is permanent without end; because whatever has been proven eternal, its non-existence is impossible. We only say this because if it were to cease to exist, its non-existence would need a cause; for it arises after the continuation of existence in eternity, and we have mentioned that everything that arises must have a cause insofar as it arises, not insofar as it exists. And just as the change from non-existence to existence needs a preponderator for existence over non-existence, so likewise the change from existence to non-existence needs a preponderator for non-existence over existence. That preponderator is either an agent who annihilates by power, or an opposite, or the interruption of a condition among the conditions of existence. And it is impossible to refer it to power (meaning it is invalid to attribute the preponderator of non-existence to power); for existence is a fixed thing from which annihilation by power may proceed, so the powerful one, by using it, would have done a non-existent thing, and non-existence is nothing—so it is impossible for it to be an actual deed that is an effect of power. For we say: Did the agent of non-existence do something? If it is said 'yes', it is impossible because negation is not a thing. And if the Mu'tazilite says that the non-existent is a thing and an essence, then that essence is not an effect of power. (1) i.e., it was not then it was; its original form is 'ṭārā', the hamza was softened and turned into a yā', so the word is treated as a defective noun. (2) Perhaps the sentence is: 'And just as the change from existence to non-existence needs a preponderator for existence over non-existence... so likewise the change from non-existence to existence needs a preponderator for non-existence over existence,' because the letter 'bā' only enters upon that which is left and vanishes. (3) Meaning: it is not valid to attribute the preponderator of non-existence to power. (4) In the margin of (w): 'Know that existence is affirmation, and non-existence is negation.'
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents the third claim: the Maker of the world is permanent (baqi) without end. Ghazali argues that whatever has been proven eternal (qadim) in the past must also be permanent in the future, because if it were to cease, its non-existence would be a new event (tari) requiring a cause. He draws an analogy: just as changing from non-existence to existence needs a preponderator (murajjih) for existence over non-existence, so changing from existence to non-existence needs a preponderator for non-existence over existence. He then lists three possible preponderators for non-existence: an agent acting through power (qudrah), an opposite (didd), or the interruption of a condition (shart). He focuses on the first: it is impossible to attribute the annihilation to divine power, because non-existence is nothing, and an effect must be something. If the agent of non-existence did something, that something would be an existent, contradicting non-existence. He then refutes the Mu'tazilite view that the non-existent (ma'dum) is a thing and an essence, arguing that even if so, that essence is not an effect of power. The footnotes clarify textual variants and provide additional commentary.

Key Terms
باق
عدم
مرجح
قدرة
طار
معتزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That non-existence ('adam) can be a direct effect of divine power.
Correction: Ghazali argues that non-existence is nothing, not a thing, so it cannot be produced by power. An effect of power must be something existent.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is it impossible for God to annihilate the world by His power?
A) Because God does not have the power to annihilate
B) Because non-existence is nothing and cannot be an effect
C) Because annihilation requires an opposite which does not exist
D) Because the world is eternal and cannot cease

Key Takeaway
The Creator's permanence in the future follows from His eternal past, because non-existence cannot be an effect of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Creator's existence is necessary and permanent, what does that mean for your own fleeting existence? How does this truth challenge your tendency to live as if this world is all there is? Will you commit today to realign your priorities toward the Permanent?
?
Ghazali argues that non-existence cannot be a direct act of power. In what areas of your life do you act as if you can 'annihilate' your responsibilities or sins by simply ignoring them? What does this lesson reveal about the nature of true power, and how will you stop pretending that ignoring something makes it vanish?
?
Consider the Mu'tazilite claim that the non-existent is a thing. Are there 'things' in your life—like your intentions, your future, your past—that you treat as real even though they are beyond your control? How does this lesson call you to submit to the One whose power is truly effective over existence—and to stop deluding yourself about what you can control? What concrete step will you take today to surrender that false notion of control?

The Eternal Cannot Cease to Exist
Ghazali states the third claim and proves the impossibility of the eternal's non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that what is eternal cannot cease to exist, based on the principle that every change requires a cause.

Core Idea
What is eternal cannot cease because non-existence would be a change needing a cause.

Ghazali continues his arguments for the necessary existence of the Creator. Here he addresses the third claim: that the eternal (al-Qadim) is also everlasting (baqi). The key is that non-existence would be a new occurrence requiring a cause, but nothing can cause the eternal to cease.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the concise style of Ghazali's argument.
The Arabic uses the term 'طار' (occurrence) to frame non-existence as an event.
2
Identify the claim and core principle
The claim is that the eternal cannot cease. The core principle is: every occurrence (طار) requires a cause.
This principle is foundational in kalam and is used to argue both for the existence of a Creator and for His permanence.
3
Follow the analogy
Notice the symmetry: just as coming into existence needs a preponderating factor, so does ceasing to exist.
This symmetry shows that non-existence is not a simple default but a change that requires explanation.
4
Examine the three possible causes
Ghazali lists three ways something could cease: an agent annihilating it, a contrary, or the cessation of a condition. He will later dismiss the first two.
Understanding these three categories is crucial for following his refutations in subsequent sections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى الثالثة
ندعي : أن صانع العالم مع كونه موجوداً لم يزل فهو باق لا يزال لأن ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه.
وإنما قلنا ذلك لأنه لو انعدم لافتقر عدمه إلى سبب؛ فإنه طار بعد استمرار الوجود في القدم، وقد ذكرنا أن كل طار فلا بد له من سبب من حيث إنه طار، لا من حيث إنه موجود.
وكما افتقر تبدل العدم بالوجود إلى مرجح للوجود على العدم، فكذلك يفتقر تبدل الوجود بالعدم إلى مرجح للعدم على الوجود.
وذلك المرجح: إما فاعل يعدم بالقدرة، أو ضد، أو انقطاع شرط من شرائط الوجود.
The third claim: We assert that the Maker of the world, being existent without beginning, is abiding without end, because what is proven to be eternal is impossible to cease. We only say this because if it were to cease, its ceasing would need a cause; for it is an occurrence after the continuation of existence in pre-eternity, and we have mentioned that every occurrence must have a cause inasmuch as it is an occurrence, not inasmuch as it is existent. And just as the change from non-existence to existence requires a preponderating factor for existence over non-existence, so likewise the change from existence to non-existence requires a preponderating factor for non-existence over existence. And that preponderating factor is either an agent that annihilates by power, or a contrary, or the cessation of a condition from among the conditions of existence.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is the statement of the third claim (الدعوى الثالثة) in Ghazali's argument. The claim is that the Maker of the world, who is eternal (لم يزل), is also abiding (باق لا يزال). The proof is based on the principle that what is proven eternal cannot cease to exist. Ghazali then elaborates: if it were to cease, its non-existence would be an occurrence (طار) after eternal existence, and every occurrence requires a cause. He draws an analogy: just as the transition from non-existence to existence requires a preponderating factor (مرجح) to tip the balance, so the transition from existence to non-existence requires a preponderating factor for non-existence. He then enumerates three possible causes for non-existence: an agent that annihilates by power (فاعل يعدم بالقدرة), a contrary (ضد), or the cessation of a condition of existence (انقطاع شرط من شرائط الوجود). This sets up the subsequent refutation of the first two.

Key Terms
الدعوى الثالثة
باق
طار
مرجح
فاعل يعدم بالقدرة
ضد
انقطاع شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The eternal simply cannot cease because it is eternal.
Correction: Ghazali's argument is not based on the mere fact of eternality but on the principle that non-existence would be a change requiring a cause, and no cause can affect the eternal.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does non-existence need a cause?
A) Because non-existence is an occurrence after existence.
B) Because everything needs a cause.
C) Because the Maker is eternal.
D) Because existence is better.

Key Takeaway
For Ghazali, the impossibility of the eternal's non-existence is derived from the need for a cause for any change, and the absence of any cause that could affect the eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's existence is so certain that it cannot change or cease, how does that anchor your trust in His promises? When was the last time your faith was shaken by a worldly change, and how does knowing that Allah is unchanging confront that doubt?
?
The argument says non-existence of the Eternal would require a cause—a cause that does not exist. Do you sometimes act as if your sins or weaknesses can diminish Allah's existence or power? What would it mean to truly believe that nothing—not even your worst failure—can affect Him?
?
If the eternal cannot cease, then everything you cling to in this world will eventually cease. Which attachment in your life are you treating as if it were eternal? What is one step you can take today to release that illusion and anchor your heart in the One who truly cannot cease?

Why Power and Contrary Cannot Cause Non-Existence
Ghazali refutes power and contrary as causes of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that neither power nor a contrary can cause non-existence for the eternal being.

Core Idea
Non-existence is not a thing, so power cannot produce it, and a contrary cannot overcome the eternal.

Ghazali tackles a crucial question: what could cause the non-existence of the eternal? He considers two possibilities—divine power and a contrary—and shows both are impossible. Let's follow his reasoning as he dismantles these alternatives step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: first the argument against power, then against a contrary.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the argument against power
Ghazali argues that non-existence is not a thing, so an act of power cannot produce it. Even if the non-existent is considered a thing (as Mu'tazilites claim), the agent only negates existence, which is not a thing either. So power is not used.
Notice how Ghazali addresses the Mu'tazilite objection.
3
Understand the argument against a contrary
Ghazali says a contrary cannot annihilate the eternal because if the contrary is originated, its existence would be repelled by opposition to the eternal, not cut off the eternal's existence.
This shows the eternal's supremacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومحال أن يحال على القدرة؛ إذ الوجود شيء ثابت يجوز أن يصدر دفع شبهة الإعدام عن القدرة، فيكون القادر باستعماله فعل شيئاً عدمياً، والعدم ليس بشيء فيستحيل أن يكون فعلاً واقعاً بأثر القدرة، فإنا نقول: فاعل العدم هل فعل شيئاً؟ فإن قيل: نعم كان محالاً؛ لأن النفي ليس بشيء.
وإن قال المعتزلي: إن المعدوم شيء وذات، فليس ذلك الذات من أثر القدرة، فلا يتصور أن يقول: الفعل الواقع بالقدرة فعل ذلك الذات؛ فإنها أزلية، وإنما فعله نفي وجود الذات، ونفي وجود الذات ليس شيئاً فإذن ما فعل شيئاً.
وإذا صدق قولنا: ما فعل شيئاً صدق قولنا: إنه لم يستعمل القدرة في أمر ألبتة، فبقي كما كان، ولم يفعل شيئاً.
وباطل أن يقال: إنه يعدمه ضده؛ لأن الضد إن فرض حادثاً اندفع وجوده بمضادة القديم، وكان ذلك أولى من أن ينقطع به وجود القديم.
And it is impossible to attribute [the cause of non-existence] to power; because existence is a fixed thing that can issue forth [as an act of power], to refute the doubt about annihilation by power. So the powerful agent, by using it, would do something non-existent, and non-existence is not a thing; so it is impossible for it to be an act produced by the effect of power. For we say: Did the agent of non-existence do something? If it is said: Yes, it is impossible, because negation is not a thing. And if the Mu'tazilite says: The non-existent is a thing and an essence, then that essence is not from the effect of power. So it is inconceivable that it be said: The act occurring by power is the act of that essence; for it is eternal, and what he did is the negation of the existence of the essence, and the negation of the existence of the essence is not a thing. So then he did not do anything. And if our statement 'He did not do anything' is true, then our statement 'He did not use power in any matter at all' is true, so he remained as he was, and did not do anything. And it is false to say that a contrary annihilates him; because if the contrary is assumed to be originated, its existence would be repelled by opposition to the eternal, and that is more fitting than that the existence of the eternal should be cut off by it.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the first two possible causes of non-existence for the eternal being. First, Ghazali argues that power (قدرة) cannot cause non-existence because non-existence (العدم) is not a thing (شيء). An act of power must produce something, but non-existence is nothing, so an agent cannot 'do' non-existence. He then addresses the Mu'tazilite objection that the non-existent is a thing and has an essence. Even if that were true, Ghazali says, that essence is not produced by power (because it is eternal), and what the agent actually does is negate the existence of that essence, which is still not a thing. So the agent does not do anything, and thus power is not used. Second, Ghazali refutes the possibility that a contrary (ضد) can cause non-existence. If the contrary were originated (حادث), its existence would be opposed to the eternal (القديم) and would be repelled by that opposition, rather than destroying the eternal. The eternal's existence would not be cut off by the contrary. Thus both power and the contrary fail as causes of non-existence for the eternal.

Key Terms
قدرة
عدم
شيء
فاعل
المعتزلي
المعدوم
ضد
قديم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ghazali denies God's power altogether, or that he says the eternal can be destroyed by a contrary.
Correction: Ghazali only denies that power can produce non-existence directly; God's power is over existence. He also affirms that the eternal's existence overpowers any originated contrary.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali say that a contrary cannot annihilate the eternal?
A) Because the eternal has no contrary.
B) Because if the contrary is originated, it would be repelled by opposition to the eternal, not destroy it.
C) Because the contrary is also eternal.
D) Because the contrary is weaker.

Key Takeaway
Neither power nor a contrary can cause the non-existence of the eternal because non-existence is not a thing and the eternal's existence overpowers any originated contrary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If non-existence is not a thing that can be 'done', then your own death is simply the cessation of God's sustaining your existence. Does that make you more aware that every moment you exist is a direct gift from the Eternal? How will you thank Him today for that sustained gift?
?
Ghazali proves that no originated contrary can overcome the eternal. What created 'contrary' are you afraid might destroy your connection with Allah—a sin, a trial, or a person? Will you trust that the Eternal's hold on you is stronger than any temporal opposite?
?
You have power over things that exist, but have you ever tried to 'produce' nothing—by ignoring, denying, or wishing away your responsibilities? How does Ghazali's argument expose the futility of such attempts? What is one area of your life where you need to stop trying to make something 'not exist' and instead submit to the Real?

Refutation of Annihilation by an Opposite
Ghazali refutes annihilation by opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for an eternal being to be annihilated by an opposite, whether temporal or eternal.

Core Idea
An opposite cannot annihilate the Eternal; if temporal it would be repelled, if eternal it would have always coexisted.

Ghazali addresses a common doubt: could something be annihilated by its opposite? For the Eternal, this is impossible. This passage presents two decisive arguments, one for a temporal opposite and one for an eternal opposite. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the translation to ensure comprehension.
The passage begins with 'دفع شبهة الإعدام وباطل أن يقال...'
2
Identify the two scenarios
Notice that Ghazali splits the objection into two cases: the opposite being temporal (حادث) and the opposite being eternal (قديم). Understand why each fails.
The first case uses the principle that the Eternal repels what opposes it.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
For a temporal opposite, it is more likely that the opposite itself is repelled. For an eternal opposite, it would have always existed without annihilating the Eternal, so it cannot do so now.
This shows the impossibility of annihilation by an opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دفع شبهة الإعدام وباطل أن يقال : إنَّه يعدمُهُ ضدُّهُ ; لأنَّ الضدَّ إِن فُرضَ حادثاً .. اندفع بالضد وجوده بمضادة القديم، وكان ذلك أولى من أن ينقطعَ به وجودُ القديم (۳)
ومحال أن يكون له ضد قديم كان موجوداً معه في القدم ولم يعدمه وقد أعدمَهُ الآن (۱) .
وباطل أن يقال : انعدم لانعدام شرط وجوده ; فإن الشرط إن كان دفع شبهة الإعدام بانقطاع أحد شرائط حادثاً.. استحال أن يكون وجود القديم مشروطاً بحادث ، وإن كان قديماً .. فالكلام في استحالة عدم الشرط كالكلام في استحالة عدم عدمه (۲) المشروط ، فلا يتصور
Refutation of the doubt about annihilation. And it is false to say that its opposite annihilates it; because if the opposite is assumed to be temporal, then by the opposite its existence is repelled by the opposition to the eternal, and that would be more appropriate than that the existence of the eternal should be cut off by it (3). And it is impossible that it has an eternal opposite that existed with it in eternity and did not annihilate it, but now annihilated it (1). And it is false to say: it ceased to exist due to the cessation of a condition of its existence; for if the condition is temporal, then it is impossible that the existence of the eternal be conditioned by a temporal thing; and if it is eternal, then the discussion about the impossibility of the condition's non-existence is like the discussion about the impossibility of the non-existence of the conditioned (2), so it is not conceivable.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage refutes two possible explanations for the annihilation of the eternal (i.e., God). First, the claim that an opposite (didd) causes annihilation: if the opposite is temporal, it would be repelled by opposing the eternal, and the eternal would be more likely to persist; if the opposite is eternal, it would have always coexisted and not caused annihilation now. Second, the claim that annihilation occurs due to the loss of a condition for existence: if the condition is temporal, the eternal cannot depend on it; if the condition is eternal, then its non-existence is as impossible as the non-existence of the conditioned. Thus, neither explanation works.

Key Terms
ضد (didd, opposite)
قديم (qadim, eternal)
حادث (hadith, temporal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think annihilation by opposite is a natural process that could affect even the Eternal.
Correction: Ghazali shows that both temporal and eternal opposites fail to cause annihilation, because the Eternal is not subject to change from external forces.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what happens if the opposite is temporal?
A) The opposite annihilates the eternal.
B) The eternal repels the opposite, so the opposite ceases.
C) Both the eternal and opposite cease.
D) The opposite becomes eternal.

Key Takeaway
The Eternal cannot be annihilated by an opposite, whether temporal or eternal, because the logic leads to contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial that feels like it could 'annihilate' your trust in Allah, how does knowing that no temporal opposite can overcome the Eternal change your attitude toward that trial? What specific action can you take today to rely on Allah's permanence?
?
If an eternal opposite is impossible, what does that imply about any force in your life that seems to oppose Allah's will? How does this truth challenge you to submit completely to His decree, especially in moments of doubt?
?
The argument shows that an opposite cannot suddenly act after being inactive. How does this apply to your relationship with Allah? Are there any areas where you treat Allah's mercy as if it could suddenly 'annihilate' due to your sins? What does this passage teach you about the constancy of His attributes?

Refutation of Annihilation by Loss of Condition
Ghazali refutes annihilation by loss of condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Eternal's existence cannot depend on any condition that could cease, thereby making annihilation impossible.

Core Idea
If the Eternal's existence were conditional, the condition must itself be eternal, making its non-existence impossible.

Another possible objection is that the Eternal could cease to exist if a condition for its existence is removed. Ghazali shows this leads to a contradiction: either the condition is temporal (impossible for the Eternal) or eternal (impossible to cease).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on condition
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Notice that the argument divides into two possibilities for the condition.
The passage begins with 'وباطل أن يقال : انعدم لانعدام شرط وجوده'
2
Analyze the temporal condition case
If the condition is temporal, then the Eternal's existence would depend on something temporal, which is impossible because the Eternal is independent.
This shows a direct contradiction.
3
Analyze the eternal condition case
If the condition is eternal, then its non-existence is as impossible as the non-existence of the Eternal itself. So the condition cannot cease.
This makes annihilation through condition loss impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دفع شبهة الإعدام وباطل أن يقال : إنَّه يعدمُهُ ضدُّهُ ; لأنَّ الضدَّ إِن فُرضَ حادثاً .. اندفع بالضد وجوده بمضادة القديم، وكان ذلك أولى من أن ينقطعَ به وجودُ القديم (۳)
ومحال أن يكون له ضد قديم كان موجوداً معه في القدم ولم يعدمه وقد أعدمَهُ الآن (۱) .
وباطل أن يقال : انعدم لانعدام شرط وجوده ; فإن الشرط إن كان دفع شبهة الإعدام بانقطاع أحد شرائط حادثاً.. استحال أن يكون وجود القديم مشروطاً بحادث ، وإن كان قديماً .. فالكلام في استحالة عدم الشرط كالكلام في استحالة عدم عدمه (۲) المشروط ، فلا يتصور
Refutation of the doubt about annihilation. And it is false to say that its opposite annihilates it; because if the opposite is assumed to be temporal, then by the opposite its existence is repelled by the opposition to the eternal, and that would be more appropriate than that the existence of the eternal should be cut off by it (3). And it is impossible that it has an eternal opposite that existed with it in eternity and did not annihilate it, but now annihilated it (1). And it is false to say: it ceased to exist due to the cessation of a condition of its existence; for if the condition is temporal, then it is impossible that the existence of the eternal be conditioned by a temporal thing; and if it is eternal, then the discussion about the impossibility of the condition's non-existence is like the discussion about the impossibility of the non-existence of the conditioned (2), so it is not conceivable.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage refutes two possible explanations for the annihilation of the eternal (i.e., God). First, the claim that an opposite (didd) causes annihilation: if the opposite is temporal, it would be repelled by opposing the eternal, and the eternal would be more likely to persist; if the opposite is eternal, it would have always coexisted and not caused annihilation now. Second, the claim that annihilation occurs due to the loss of a condition for existence: if the condition is temporal, the eternal cannot depend on it; if the condition is eternal, then its non-existence is as impossible as the non-existence of the conditioned. Thus, neither explanation works.

Key Terms
شرط (shart, condition)
قديم (qadim, eternal)
حادث (hadith, temporal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Eternal's existence might be contingent on conditions that could be removed, leading to annihilation.
Correction: Ghazali shows that any condition must be either temporal (impossible) or eternal (cannot cease), so annihilation by loss of condition is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, if the condition for the Eternal's existence is eternal, what follows?
A) The condition can cease, causing annihilation.
B) The condition's non-existence is as impossible as the Eternal's non-existence.
C) The condition must be temporal.
D) The Eternal can exist without any condition.

Key Takeaway
The Eternal cannot be annihilated by loss of a condition, because any condition for its existence must be eternal and thus cannot cease.

?

?
When you rely on temporal conditions for your security—wealth, health, relationships—are you implicitly denying the eternity of the One who sustains them? What would it look like to trust only the Unconditional today?
?
The Eternal cannot be annihilated by loss of any condition. Does your heart truly rest in that permanence, or do you still behave as if God's support could be withdrawn? What must change in your daily thoughts to align with this truth?

The Impossibility of an Eternal Opposite to God
Ghazali refutes the possibility of a co-eternal opposite and invalidates the condition excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that God cannot have a co-eternal opposite and why the excuse of a conditional existence fails.

Core Idea
An eternal opposite to God is impossible, and the condition excuse is self-defeating.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles a fundamental theological issue: can there be any entity co-eternal with God? He then addresses a potential objection that God's existence might depend on a condition. This lesson walks through his concise refutation, which hinges on the nature of eternity and conditionality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: two distinct refutations.
2
Identify the two claims
The passage refutes two claims: (1) that God could have an eternal opposite, and (2) that God could cease to exist due to a missing condition. Find where each is addressed.
3
Understand the first refutation
Why is an eternal opposite impossible? Because if it were truly co-eternal, it could not be annihilated—its existence would be necessary. Ghazali states this in the first sentence.
4
Analyze the condition argument
The condition excuse is examined in two cases: if the condition is temporal (created), an eternal being cannot depend on it; if the condition is eternal, then its non-existence is as impossible as God's non-existence, so the argument is circular.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters: God's existence is absolute and unconditional. No entity can rival His eternity, and no external condition can affect His necessary existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومحال أن يكون له ضد قديم كان موجوداً معه في القدم ولم يعدمه وقد أعدمَهُ الآن. وباطل أن يقال : انعدم لانعدام شرط وجوده ؛ فإن الشرط إن كان [؟] حادثاً.. استحال أن يكون وجود القديم مشروطاً بحادث ، وإن كان قديماً .. فالكلام في استحالة عدم الشرط كالكلام في استحالة عدم المشروط ، فلا يتصور
It is impossible that He has an eternal opposite that existed with Him in pre-eternity and He did not annihilate it and now He has annihilated it. And it is false to say: 'It ceased to exist due to the cessation of a condition of its existence'; for the condition, if it is [?] temporal, it is impossible that the existence of the eternal is conditioned by a temporal thing; and if it is eternal, then the discussion about the impossibility of the non-existence of the condition is like the discussion about the impossibility of the non-existence of the conditioned, so it cannot be conceived.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument refuting two claims: first, that God could have a co-eternal opposite, and second, that God's existence could cease due to the removal of a condition. The author first states that it is impossible for God to have an eternal opposite that existed with Him from eternity and then was destroyed, because if it were truly co-eternal, its non-existence would be impossible. Then he addresses the excuse that God ceased to exist because the condition for His existence ceased. He examines two possibilities: if the condition is temporal (created), then it is impossible for an eternal being to depend on a temporal thing; if the condition is eternal, then the impossibility of its non-existence is exactly the same as the impossibility of the non-existence of the conditioned (God), so the argument is self-defeating. Thus the claim is inconceivable.

Key Terms
ضد
قديم
شرط
وجود
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's existence is conditioned upon something else and could cease if that condition is removed.
Correction: Ghazali shows that any condition would either be temporal (impossible for an eternal being) or eternal (its non-existence is as impossible as God's), so the excuse is invalid.

Checkpoint

A) Conditions are always eternal.
B) If the condition is temporal, the eternal cannot depend on it; if eternal, its non-existence is as impossible as the non-existence of the conditioned.
C) God has no conditions at all.
D) Conditions are irrelevant to God's existence.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's argument demonstrates that God's existence is absolute, not dependent on any condition, and that no co-eternal entity is possible.

Proof that the Creator is Not a Body
Ghazali presents the fifth claim that God is not a body and gives two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments proving that God cannot be a body: the argument from composition and the argument from the need for a determiner.

Core Idea
God cannot be a body because bodies are composed of atoms and need an external cause for their specific size.

Imam al-Ghazali now addresses a foundational claim of Islamic theology: that God is not a body. This lesson presents two clear rational proofs from his work 'al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad'. The first proof relies on the definition of a body as composed of atoms, while the second shows that a bodily God would require a determiner, contradicting His nature as the Creator. Let's examine these arguments closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice how Ghazali connects the two proofs with 'wa li-annahu' (and because).
2
Understand the first proof: body = composed of atoms
Ghazali defines a body as composed of two atoms (jawhar) that occupy space. Since God cannot be an atom (as atoms are contingent), He cannot be a body.
This argument hinges on a specific technical definition of 'body' in kalam.
3
Understand the second proof: the need for a determiner
If God were a body, He would have a specific size. He could be smaller or larger, but no size is inherently preferable. Therefore, an external determiner (mukhassis) would be needed to assign His size, making Him dependent and created.
This is an application of the principle of preponderance (tarjih) without a preponderator.
4
Reflect on the implication
Both proofs show that a bodily God would be contingent and caused. Hence, the Creator cannot be a body.
This conclusion is essential for the Islamic concept of tanzih (divine transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى الخامسة : أنَّ صانع العالم ليس بجسم ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ جسم فهو مؤلف من جوهرين متحيزين، وإذا استحال أن يكون جوهراً . . استحال أن يكون جسماً. ونحن لا نعني بالجسم إلا هذا، فإن سمّاه مسم جسماً ولم يرد هذا المعنى.. كانت المضايقة معه لحق اللغة، أو لحق الشرع، لا لحق العقل؛ فإنَّ العقل لا يحكمُ في إطلاق الألفاظ ونظم الحروف والأصوات التي هي اصطلاحات. ولأنه لو كان جسماً.. لكان مقدَّراً بمقدار مخصوص، يجوز أن يكون أصغر منه أو أكبر، ولا يترجَحُ أحد الجائزين على الآخر إلا بمخصص ومرجّح كما سبق، فيفتقر إلى مخصص يتصرف فيه، فيقدرُهُ بمقدار مخصوص، فيكون مصنوعاً لا صانعاً، ومخلوقاً لا خالقاً
The fifth claim: that the Maker of the world is not a body; because every body is composed of two atoms that occupy space, and if it is impossible for Him to be an atom, it is impossible for Him to be a body. And we mean by 'body' nothing other than this. So if someone calls Him a body and does not intend this meaning, the contention with him is on linguistic grounds or on legal grounds, not on rational grounds; for reason does not rule over the utterance of words and the arrangement of letters and sounds which are conventions. And because if He were a body, He would be quantified by a specific quantity, it would be possible for Him to be smaller or larger, and neither of the two possibilities would be preferred over the other except by a determiner and preponderator, as previously mentioned; thus He would need a determiner to act upon Him and quantify Him with a specific quantity, so He would be made, not a maker, and created, not a creator.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) that God is not a body. It is presented as the fifth claim (al-da'wa al-khamisa). Ghazali gives two arguments. First, every body is composed of two atoms (jawhar) that occupy space. Since God cannot be an atom (because atoms are contingent and created), He cannot be composed of atoms and thus cannot be a body. Ghazali clarifies that 'body' is defined technically as that which is composed of atoms. If someone uses 'body' in a different sense, the disagreement becomes linguistic or legal, not rational. The second argument: if God were a body, He would have a specific size. He could be smaller or larger, and neither possibility is inherently preferable. Therefore, an external determinator (mukhassis) would be needed to assign His actual size. That would make God contingent and caused, not the Creator. Hence, God cannot be a body.

Key Terms
جسم
جوهر
متحيز
مخصص
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God is not a body, He is nothing or imperceptible.
Correction: Ghazali shows that being a body entails composition and contingency, which are incompatible with divinity. God's non-bodily nature does not mean He is non-existent; rather, He is transcendent beyond physical categories.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali argue that God cannot be a body?
A) Because bodies are always visible, which would contradict His hiddenness
B) Because every body is composed of atoms and would need a determiner for its size, implying createdness
C) Because the word 'body' is never used for God in the Quran
D) Because reason cannot judge whether God is a body or not

Key Takeaway
God is not a body because bodies are composed and contingent, whereas God is the necessary Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were a body, He would need a determiner to choose His size. Who determines your life—your desires, your plans—if not the transcendent God who needs no determiner? Are you truly surrendering to the One who is absolutely independent, or are you trying to be your own determiner? What would change if you fully trusted His determination over your own?
?
Ghazali points out that disagreements about calling God a 'body' might be merely linguistic. Is your knowledge of God merely mental assent to correct words, or does it transform how you live? Examine your heart: do you treat God as if He were limited, needing your approval or fitting into your categories? Repent of any subtle attempt to reduce the Creator to something manageable.
?
The proof shows that if God had a specific size, He would be contingent and dependent on a creator. How often do you depend on created things—health, wealth, relationships—as if they were ultimate? Are you seeking your security from the One who is not a body, who transcends all limitations, or are you relying on fragile bodies like your own? Today, commit to placing your trust solely in the Determiner who needs no determiner.

The Impossibility of Direction: The Argument from Contingency
Ghazali's first proof against direction: from contingency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first proof that attributing a direction to God implies contingency and thus cannot apply to the Eternal.

Core Idea
Direction implies contingency; the Eternal cannot be contingent.

Ghazali begins a key argument against those who claim God has a direction. He shows that if God were in a direction, that direction would be a possible attribute requiring a specifier, which introduces composition and contingency. Since the Eternal must be necessary in every respect, direction is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the argument's own sequence come through before you interpret.
2
Identify key terms
Note the terms jawāz (possibility), mukhassis (specifier), qadīm (eternal), wājib al-wujūd (necessary being). These are central to the proof.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the steps: all directions equal → particularization is not necessary → it is possible → needs a specifier → specifier adds extra meaning → contingency → impossibility for the eternal.
Notice how Ghazali moves from the premise to the conclusion.
4
Reflect on the foundation
Consider why this argument is foundational: it links attributes to necessity and shows that any possible attribute in God would undermine His eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدلُّ أيضاً على بطلان القول بالجهة : أن ذلك يطرق الجواز إليه ويُحوجه إلى مخصص يخصُصُهُ بأحد وجوه الجواز ، وذلك محال من وجهين : أحدهما : أنَّ الجهة التي تختص به لا تختص به لذاته ؛ فإن سائر الجهات متساوية بالإضافة إلى قابل الجهةِ ، فاختصاصه ببعض الجهات المعينة ليس بواجب لذاته ، بل هو جائز ، فيحتاج إلى مخصص يخصصه ويكون الاختصاصُ فيه معنى زائداً على ذاته ، وما تطرق الجواز إليه . استحال قدمُهُ ، بل القديم عبارة عما هو واجب الوجود من جميع الجهات
It also indicates the invalidity of the statement that He is in a direction: that it introduces possibility (jawāz) to Him and necessitates a specifier (mukhassis) to specify Him to one of the possible ways, and that is impossible. This has two aspects: One of them: that the direction which He is particularized by is not particularized to Him by His essence; for all directions are equal with respect to the recipient of direction, so His being particularized to some specific directions is not necessary by His essence, but rather possible (jā'iz), so He needs a specifier to specify Him, and the particularization becomes an extra meaning over His essence. And whatever possibility enters upon, its eternity becomes impossible. Rather, the eternal (qadīm) is that which is necessary of existence from all aspects.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two proofs that direction attribution to God is invalid. Ghazali argues that if God were in a direction, that direction would be a possible (jā'iz) attribute because all directions are equivalent with respect to God's essence—none is inherently necessary. Therefore, being in a specific direction requires a specifier (mukhassis) external to His essence, which introduces composition (ziyāda) and contingency. Anything that is contingent cannot be eternal (qadīm), because the eternal must be necessary from every aspect. Thus, direction attribution contradicts the very definition of eternity.

Key Terms
جَواز (jawāz)
مُخَصِّص (mukhassis)
قَدِيم (qadīm)
واجب الوجود (wājib al-wujūd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's direction could be an inherent attribute that does not require a cause.
Correction: Ghazali shows that since all directions are equal with respect to essence, any particular direction is possible (not necessary) and thus requires an external specifier, which introduces contingency.

Checkpoint

C) Because God chose a direction freely.
D) Because the direction is a body.

Key Takeaway
Attributing a direction to God implies contingency, which contradicts His eternal necessity; therefore, direction is impossible.

Commit to one specific action this week that realigns your ultimate reliance on the Necessarily Existent alone.

Direction Implies Measurement and Composition
Ghazali proves direction implies spatial opposition and contingent measurement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing direction to Allah leads to the absurdity of measurement and composition, based on Ghazali's rational proof.

Core Idea
If God were in a direction, He would need a determiner—contradicting His necessity.

Ghazali now offers a second, more direct argument: spatial opposition forces a size comparison. Any size (smaller, larger, or equal) is a contingent measurement that could be otherwise, requiring an external causer. This introduces need and composition, incompatible with the Eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the logical flow from 'direction' to 'opposite' to 'measurement'.
Pay attention to the four key terms highlighted.
2
Understand Key Terms
Learn the meanings of muḥādhī (opposite), taqdīr (measurement), muqaddir (determiner), and mukhassis (specifier). These are central to the argument.
These terms appear in many kalam arguments.
3
Follow the Logical Chain
Trace the argument: direction → spatial opposition → size comparison (smaller, larger, equal) → contingent measurement → need for a determiner → absurdity for God.
The conclusion is that direction would make God dependent on a cause.
4
Reflect on the Absurdity
Consider why this proof is decisive: God cannot be subject to measurement because that implies composition and contingency, which are impossible for the Necessary Being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : أنَّه لو كان بجهة .. لكان محاذياً لجسمِ العالم ، وكلُّ محاذ : فإما أن يكون أصغر منه ، وإما أكبر ، وإما مساوياً ، وكلُّ ذلك يوجب التقدير بمقدار ، وذلك المقدار يجوز في العقل أن يفرض أصغر منه أو أكبر ؛ فيحتاج إلى مقدر ومخصص
The second aspect: that if He were in a direction, He would be opposite to the body of the world. And everything opposite is either smaller than it, or larger, or equal. All of that necessitates being determined by a measure (taqdīr). And that measure can, in the intellect, be supposed to be smaller or larger; so it needs a determiner (muqaddir) and a specifier (mukhassis).
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage gives the second of two proofs that direction attribution is invalid. If God were in a direction, He would be spatially opposite (muḥādhī) to the world. That would imply a relationship of size: He would be either smaller, larger, or equal to the world. Any such determination of size is a contingent measurement, which in the intellect could be conceived as smaller or larger. Therefore, it requires an external determiner (muqaddir) and specifier (mukhassis). This introduces contingency and need for a cause, incompatible with God's necessity and eternity.

Key Terms
مُحَاذِي (muḥādhī)
تَقْدِير (taqdīr)
مُقَدِّر (muqaddir)
مُخَصِّص (mukhassis)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God is in a direction, He can simply be immense without measurement.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that any spatial position implies a relationship of size (smaller, larger, or equal) with the world. Even immense implies a measure that could be otherwise, requiring a cause. Thus, direction necessarily introduces contingency.

Checkpoint
What does the relation of 'being opposite' to the world entail according to Ghazali?
A) It makes God part of the world.
B) It implies a need for a determiner.
C) It proves God's greatness.
D) It shows God's omnipresence.

Key Takeaway
Attributing direction to Allah leads to the absurd conclusion that He requires a determiner, proving His transcendence beyond spatial relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Proof of a Non-spatial Existent from Two Premises
Ghazali presents a two-premise proof for a non-spatial existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali proves the existence of a non-spatial existent through two necessary premises and why the conclusion is unavoidable once the premises are accepted.

Core Idea
Two premises force the existence of a non-spatial Creator.

In this passage, Ghazali distills the proof for a non-spatial existent into a crisp conditional argument. He assumes his opponent denies the possibility, then shows that two already-established premises lead necessarily to the desired conclusion. This is a model of efficient theological reasoning, building on prior demonstrations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If the opponent claims... then we have proven it by...'
The proof is presented as a response to a denial.
2
Identify the two premises
Ghazali states two premises: (1) every spatial being is originated, and (2) every originated thing needs a non-originated agent. Pause and think: do you accept these?
These are not proven here; they are assumed from earlier arguments.
3
Follow the logical necessity
From the two premises, Ghazali claims the conclusion follows necessarily (bi l-ḍarūra). Consider why: if every spatial is originated and needs a non-originated maker, then that maker must be non-spatial (since if it were spatial, it would be originated and need another, leading to infinite regress).
4
Reflect on the argument's force
Ghazali asserts that acknowledging the premises forces one to accept the conclusion—the existence of a non-spatial existent. Contemplate why this argument is foundational for Islamic theology.
This argument is a concise form of the cosmological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن زعم الخصمُ أنَّ ذلك محال وجوده .. فقد دللنا عليه بأنه : مهما بان أنَّ كلَّ متحيّر حادث ، وأنَّ كلَّ حادث يفتقر إلى فاعل ليس بحادث .. فقد لزم بالضرورة من هاتين المقدمتين ثبوت موجود ليس بمتحيز . وأما الأصلان فقد أثبتناهُما ، وأما الدعوى اللازمة منهما .. فلا سبيل إلى جحدها مع الإقرار بالأصلين .
If the opponent claims that such [an existent] is impossible in its existence, then we have proven it by [saying]: Whenever it is evident that every spatial being (mutaḥayyiz) is originated (ḥādith), and that every originated thing is in need of an agent (fā‘il) that is not originated, then necessarily from these two premises follows the existence of an existent that is not spatial (mutamayyiz). As for the two premises, we have established them. And as for the claim that follows from them, there is no way to deny it while admitting the two premises.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This is a compact proof (burhān) for the existence of a non-spatial existent. The author presents a conditional argument: if the opponent denies the possibility of a non-spatial existent, we can prove it by two established premises: (1) every spatial body is originated, and (2) every originated thing requires a non-originated maker. The conclusion that there exists a non-spatial existent (i.e., the Creator) follows necessarily. The author asserts that the premises have already been proven, and anyone who accepts them cannot consistently deny the conclusion.

Key Terms
متحيز
حادث
فاعل
مقدمتان
ضرورة
موجود ليس بمتحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what necessarily follows from the two premises that every spatial body is originated and every originated thing needs a non-originated maker?
A) The world is eternal
B) There exists a non-spatial existent
C) Spatial bodies are eternal
D) The maker is a body

Key Takeaway
Once you accept that every spatial thing is originated and every originated thing needs a non-originated maker, you cannot deny the existence of a non-spatial Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that every spatial thing is temporary and depends on a non-spatial Creator. But when you feel anxious, do you run to spatial comforts (food, entertainment, people) rather than to the non-spatial Creator? What does that reveal about your true trust?
?
If the ultimate reality is non-spatial, why do you invest so much energy in acquiring and maintaining physical objects? What would it look like to live as if the non-spatial is more real than the spatial?
?
The proof shows that you cannot logically deny a non-spatial existent. But does your daily schedule reflect that you live for that existent? What would you change if you truly believed that the non-spatial is the only permanent reality?

God is Not on the Throne: Ghazali's Proof
Ghazali asserts and proves God's transcendence above physical settlement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that God cannot be described as physically settling on the Throne, based on the implications of quantification and encompassment.

Core Idea
God's transcendence negates any physical settlement on the Throne.

Ghazali continues his series of negative claims about God. In this eighth claim, he addresses a common anthropomorphic interpretation of God's relationship to the Throne. The passage presents two rational arguments and a general principle to show that describing God as physically settled on the Throne is impossible. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the main claim and arguments
The passage has a clear claim: God is not settled on the Throne. It then gives two arguments and a general principle. Find each one.
3
Understand the first argument: quantification
Anything that settles on a body is quantified (larger, smaller, or equal). This implies estimation, which is impossible for God.
Think about why estimation is incompatible with God's perfection.
4
Understand the second argument: encompassment
If God touched the Throne from above, He could be touched from all sides, becoming encompassed. The opponent denies this but it follows from his view.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
5
Reflect on the general principle and conclusion
Only bodies settle on bodies; only accidents inhere in bodies. Since God is neither, no separate proof is needed.
This principle ties back to earlier lessons on God's nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى الثامنة: ندعي: أنَّ الله تعالى منزَّةٌ عن أن يوصف بالاستقرار على العرش؛ فإن الله تعالى غير مستقر، كلُّ مُتمكِّن على جسم ومستقر عليه مُقدَّر لا محالة فإنَّه إما أن يكون أكبر منه، أو أصغر، أو مساوياً، وكلُّ ذلك لا يخلو عن التقدير، ولأنه لو جاز أن يماسَّهُ جسم العرش من هذه الجهة التي هي العليا لجاز أن يماسه من سائر الجهات، فيصير محاطاً به، والخصم لا يعتقد ذلك بحال وهو لازم على مذهبه بالضرورة. وعلى الجملة: لا يستقر على الجسم إلا جسم، ولا يحلُّ فيه إلا عرض، وقد بان أنه تعالى وتقدَّسَ ليس بجسم ولا عرض، فلا يُحتاج إلى إفراد هذه الدعوى بإقامة البرهان.
The eighth claim: We assert that God, exalted, is free from being described as settling on the Throne; for God, exalted, is not settled. Every thing that is settled on a body and resting upon it is necessarily quantified; for it is either larger than it, or smaller, or equal, and all of that is not free from estimation. And because if it were permissible for the body of the Throne to touch Him from that direction which is the upper, then it would be permissible for it to touch Him from all other directions, so He would become encompassed by it, and the opponent does not believe that at all, yet it is necessarily entailed by his doctrine. In summary: nothing settles on a body except a body, and nothing inheres in it except an accident; and it has become clear that He, exalted and sanctified, is not a body nor an accident, so there is no need to single out this claim with a proof.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal claim and its proof. Ghazali states the eighth claim: God cannot be described as 'mustaqirr' (settled) on the Throne. He gives two reasons: (1) Anything that settles on a body is quantified—it must be bigger, smaller, or equal to the body, which implies composition and estimation, impossible for God. (2) If God were touching the Throne from one direction (the top), then He could be touched from all directions, making Him enclosed, which the opponent himself denies but his position entails. Then he gives a general principle: only a body settles on a body, and only an accident inheres in a body. Since God is neither body nor accident, He is not on the Throne in a physical sense. Thus no separate proof is needed.

Key Terms
استقرار (settling)
تمكن (being established)
تقدير (estimation/quantification)
محاط (encompassed)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that God literally sits on the Throne like a body on a throne.
Correction: Ghazali argues that such a notion entails quantification and encompassment, which are impossible for God.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali say God cannot be described as 'mustaqirr' (settled) on the Throne?
A) Because the Throne is not a body.
B) Because settling on a body entails being a body, and God is not a body.
C) Because the Quran says God is above all direction.
D) Because the Prophet forbade describing God with location.

Key Takeaway
God's transcendence means He is not subject to physical attributes like settlement, which would imply quantification and limitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever mentally imagine God as being 'up there' or in a specific direction? How does Ghazali's argument challenge that tendency in your own heart, and what will you do today to purify your conception of Him?
?
If you believe God is not limited by space or time, why do you sometimes act as if He is far away or confined to a particular place (e.g., only in the mosque)? What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized His absolute transcendence?
?
The opponent's view logically leads to God being encompassed. In what ways do you try to 'contain' God — through your own expectations, limited understanding, or demands? Will you repent from that limitation and submit to His unfathomable majesty right now?
