The Three Meanings of Oneness
Ghazali defines the oneness of God in three senses and begins a proof against a partner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three meanings of 'one' as applied to God and Ghazali's initial argument against the possibility of a partner.

Core Idea
God's oneness is a negation of all forms of otherness, not a positive attribute.

Ghazali begins the tenth claim by clarifying that God's oneness is not an additional attribute but a negation—affirming His essence while denying any equal, peer, or opposite. He then explains three distinct senses in which Allah is called 'One' and initiates a rational proof against the existence of a partner.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Notice that Ghazali places this discussion under the 'qutb' (pole) of negation, indicating that oneness is a negation of otherness, not a positive attribute.
2
Learn the three meanings of 'one'
Identify the three meanings: (1) not divisible, (2) no peer in rank, (3) no opposite. Also note the clarification about 'no equal' meaning everything else is His creation.
3
Connect meanings to denial of partner
See how each meaning supports the denial of a partner: indivisibility negates composition, no peer negates equality, no opposite negates rivalry.
4
Observe the beginning of the proof
Ghazali starts a proof: if a partner existed, it would be either like God or superior. The passage cuts off, but the implication is that both are impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى العاشرة : أنَّ الله تعالى واحد ، فإن كونه واحداً يرجع إلى ثبوت ذاته ونفي ذاته وصفاته وأفعاله غيره فليس هو نظراً في صفة زائدة على الذات فوجب ذكره في هذا القطب فنقول : الواحد قد يطلق ويراد به أنه لا يقبل الانقسام أي لا كمية له ولا حدَّ ولا مقدار والباري سبحانه وتعالى واحد بمعنى سلب الكمية المصححة للقسمة عنه فإنه غير قابل للانقسام إذ الانقسام فيما له كمية والتقسيم تصرف في كمية بالتفريق والتصغير وما لا كمية له لا يتصور انقسامه وقد يطلق والمراد به أنه لا نظير له في رتبته كما تقول الشمس واحدة والباري تعالى أيضاً بهذا المعنى واحد فإنه لا ند له فأما أنه لا ضد له فظاهر إذ المفهوم من الضد هو الذي يتعاقب الشيء على محل واحد ولا يجامعه وما لا محل له فلا ضد له والباري تعالى لا محل له فلا ضد له وأما قولنا لا ند له فنعني به أن ما سواه هو خالقه لا غير نفي الشريك عن وبرهانه أنه لو قدر له شريك لكان مثله من كل الوجوه أو أرفع
The Tenth Claim: That Allah Almighty is One (wahid). For His being One goes back to the affirmation of His essence and the negation of anything other than His essence, attributes, and acts from Him; so it is not a consideration of an attribute additional to the essence. Hence it must be mentioned in this section (al-qutb). So we say: 'One' may be used and intended by it that He does not accept division; i.e., He has no quantity (kamiyya), no limit, and no magnitude. The Creator, exalted is He, is One in the sense of negating the quantity that would make division possible from Him, for He is not divisible, since division is only for that which has quantity, and division is an operation on quantity by separation and diminution; and what has no quantity cannot be conceived to be divided. And it may be used, and the intended meaning is that He has no peer (nazir) in His rank, just as you say 'the sun is one.' The Creator, exalted is He, is also One in this sense: He has no equal (nidd). As for that He has no opposite (didd), that is obvious, for the understanding of 'opposite' is that which alternates with a thing upon a single locus and does not coexist with it; and what has no locus has no opposite. The Creator, exalted is He, has no locus, so He has no opposite. And as for our saying 'He has no equal,' we mean by it that whatever is other than Him is His creation, nothing else. The negation of a partner from Him, and its proof: that if a partner were supposed for Him, that partner would be like Him in every respect or higher.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a definition and partial proof of God's oneness (tenth claim). Ghazali begins by explaining that the oneness of God is not an additional attribute but rather a negation: it affirms His essence while negating any other essence, attributes, or acts equal to Him. Therefore it is placed in this section (the qutb on negation). He then enumerates three meanings of 'one' (al-wahid): (1) not divisible—God has no quantity, no limit, no magnitude, so He cannot be divided; (2) having no peer (nazir) in His rank—meaning no equal or counterpart; (3) having no opposite (didd)—since opposite requires a locus for alternation, and God has no locus; and (4) having no equal (nidd)—meaning all else is His creation. The passage then begins a proof for the negation of a partner: if there were a partner, that partner would either be exactly like God or superior. The text cuts off, but the implication is that both are absurd. Ghazali follows the order: first establishing the meaning of oneness, then offering a rational proof against polytheism.

Key Terms
الواحد
الانقسام
الكمية
النظير
الند
الضد
الشريك
القطب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's oneness is a positive attribute added to His essence.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that oneness is a negation: it affirms His essence and denies anything else from Him.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the meanings of 'one' (al-wahid) as applied to God in this passage?
A) He does not accept division.
B) He has no peer in His rank.
C) He has no opposite.
D) He is one in number like the number one.

Key Takeaway
God's oneness means He is indivisible, has no equal or opposite, and all else is His creation—any supposed partner would be either like Him or less, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You recite 'La ilaha illa Allah' daily. But are there moments when you treat something else as an equal—your money, your reputation, your desires? What would it cost you to truly negate every rival in your heart?
?
The passage says God has no opposite because He has no locus. Where in your life do you still imagine God is confined—by your understanding, your circumstances, or your failures? How would your prayer change if you truly believed nothing limits Him?
?
If you fully internalized that everything besides God is His creation—including your own existence, your abilities, and your future—what would you stop worrying about? What would you start doing differently today?

Proof That Allah Cannot Have a Partner
Ghazali presents a proof that Allah cannot have a partner by eliminating three possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's logical proof that Allah cannot have a partner by eliminating the three possible scenarios: equality, superiority, or inferiority.

Core Idea
A partner for Allah is impossible because it would need to be equal, higher, or lower—each absurd.

Ghazali presents a rigorous logical argument based on the definition of divinity. He systematically eliminates every conceivable scenario in which a partner could exist, using the very concept of 'two distinct things' to show the impossibility. This passage lays out the argument with careful reasoning about identity, difference, and the transcendence of Allah beyond space and time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument, then read the translation. Notice how Ghazali structures the proof around three possibilities.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three possibilities
Find where Ghazali lists the three possibilities for a supposed partner: equal in every respect, higher in rank, or lower. Note that he declares all three impossible from the start.
3
Understand the equality argument
Focus on the reasoning for why equality fails. Ghazali argues that for two things to be distinct, there must be a difference in locus, time, or definition. Since Allah is eternal and not in place or time, no distinction is possible, so number is impossible.
The example of two blacks illustrates the principle.
4
Reflect on higher and lower
Consider why a partner cannot be higher (then that being would be the true deity) or lower (then it would be deficient and not divine). This reinforces the uniqueness of Allah as the Most High.
Notice how Ghazali anchors the proof in the definition of 'deity'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفي الشريك عن وبرهانه: أنه لو قُدْرَ له شريك .. لكان مثله من كل الوجوه، أو أرفع رتبة منه، أو كان دونه، وكل ذلك محال، فالمفضي إليه محال. ووجه استحالة كونه مثلَهُ من كل وجه: أنَّ كلَّ اثنين هما متغايران، فإنْ لم يكن تغاير .. لم تكن الاثنينية معقولة، فإنا لا نعقل سوادين إلا في محلين، أو في محلّ واحد في وقتين، فيكون أحدهما مفارقاً للآخر ومبايناً له ومغايراً؛ إما في المحل، وإما في الوقت، والشيئان يتغايران تارةً بتغاير الحد والحقيقة؛ كتغاير الحركة واللون؛ فإنهما وإن اجتمعا في محل واحد في وقت واحد .. فهما اثنان؛ إذ أحدهما مغاير للآخر بحقيقته فإن استوى اثنان في الحقيقة والحد؛ كالسوادين .. فيكون الفرقُ بينهما: إما في المحل، أو في الزمان، فإن فُرضَ سوادان مثلان في جوهر واحد في حال واحد .. كان محالاً؛ إذ لم تعرف الاثنينية. ولو جاز أن يقال: هما اثنان ولا مغايرة.. لجاز أن يشار إلى إنسان واحد ويقال: إنه إنسانان، بل عشرة، ولكنهم متساوون متماثلون في العوارض واللوازم من غير فُرقان! وذلك محال بالضرورة. والمكان، وجميع فإن كان ند الله تعالى مساوياً له في الحقيقة والصفات.. استحال وجوده؛ إذ ليس يغايره بمكان؛ إذ لا مكانَ، ولا بزمان؛ إذ لا زمان، فإنهما قديمان، فإذن .. لا فرقان، وإذا ارتفع كلُّ فرق .. ارتفع العدد بالضرورة، ولزمت الوحدة. ومحال أن يقال: يخالفه بكونه أرفع منه؛ فإنَّ الأرفع هو الإله، إذ الإله عبارة عن أجل الموجودات وأرفعها، والآخر المقدر ناقص ليس بإلله، ونحن إنما نمنع العدد في الإلهية، والإله هو الذي يقال فيه بالقول المطلق: إنه أرفع الموجودات وأجلها. وإن كان أدنى منه كان محالاً؛ لأنه ناقص، ونحن نعبر بالإله عن أجل الموجودات، فلا يكون الأجل إلا واحداً، وهو الإله.
The negation of the partner and its proof: that if a partner were supposed for Him, it would be like Him in every respect, or higher in rank than Him, or below Him, and all of that is impossible, so what leads to it is impossible. The aspect of the impossibility of it being like Him in every respect: that every two things are mutually distinct; if there were no distinction, duality would not be intelligible. For we do not conceive two blacks except in two loci, or in one locus at two times; then one of them would be separate from the other, distinct from it, and different—either in locus or in time. And two things are sometimes distinct by difference in definition and reality, like the difference between motion and color; even if they coexist in one locus at one time, they are two, since one is distinct from the other in its reality. If two things are equal in reality and definition, like two blacks, then the difference between them is either in locus or in time. If two similar blacks were supposed in one substance at one moment, it would be impossible, for duality is not known. And if it were permissible to say that they are two with no distinction, it would be permissible to point to one man and say he is two men, indeed ten, but they are equal, similar in accidents and concomitants without any distinction—and that is necessarily impossible. And [considering] place, and all [that]—if the equivalent of Allah Most High were equal to Him in reality and attributes, its existence would be impossible; for it would not differ from Him by place, since there is no place, nor by time, since there is no time, for they would both be eternal. Then there would be no distinction, and when every distinction is removed, number is necessarily removed and unity necessarily follows. And it is impossible to say that it differs from Him by being higher than Him; for the higher is the deity, since 'deity' is an expression for the most exalted and highest of existents, and the other supposed is deficient, not a deity. And we only forbid number in divinity, and the deity is that of which it is said in absolute terms: it is the highest and most glorious of existents. And if it were below Him, it would be impossible because it is deficient, and we use 'deity' to mean the most glorious of existents; thus the most glorious can only be one, and He is the deity.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhān) demonstrating that Allah cannot have a partner (sharīk). Al-Ghazali begins by stating the argument: if a partner were possible, it would have to be exactly equal to Allah, or higher, or lower; all three are impossible, so the premise is impossible. He then explains in detail why each possibility fails. First, if the partner were equal in every respect, it would have to be distinct from Allah by some factor (locus, time, or definition). But since Allah is not in a place or time (both are eternal), no distinction is possible; thus number is impossible and unity is necessary. He illustrates with the example of two identical black objects requiring either different places or times to be two. Second, if the partner were higher, then that higher being would be the true deity, contradicting the definition of 'deity' as the highest. Third, if the partner were lower, it would be deficient and not divine, since divinity implies perfection. Therefore, no partner exists.

Key Terms
شريك
مثل
أرفع
أدنى
إله
عدد
مغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that a partner could be inferior to Allah and still be a partner.
Correction: If inferior, the being would be deficient and not divine, since divinity implies the highest and most glorious. Thus it fails as a partner in divinity.

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Allah cannot have a partner because any candidate would either be identical (impossible without distinction), superior (contradicts His being the highest), or inferior (not divine).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if Allah alone is the Most Exalted, or have you placed something else—status, wealth, or people—at a level that rivals Him in your heart? What would it take to dethrone those rivals today?
?
When you feel helpless, who or what do you instinctively turn to for help? Does your reliance match your confession that no partner exists?
?
If you really believed that no being can be higher or equal to Allah, how would your fear of people and desire for their approval change? Will you act on that belief this week in a specific situation?

The Impossibility of Two Equal Gods
Ghazali proves the impossibility of multiple gods through the impossibility of differentiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of two equal gods is impossible according to Ghazali's rational argument.

Core Idea
Two gods would need a basis for distinction, but the divine has neither place nor time.

Imam al-Ghazali presents a crisp logical proof that the claim of a second god equal to Allah leads to contradictions. He examines the three possible relationships between two beings claiming divinity and shows each one is impossible. This passage establishes the rational necessity of divine unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the full argument.
2
Understand the principle of distinction
Pay attention to the example of two blacks: they must differ in place or time. This illustrates that any two distinct things require a basis of differentiation.
This principle is key to the proof.
3
Apply to the divine case
Consider two gods equal in essence and attributes. Since they are eternal (no time) and not located (no place), they cannot be distinguished. Therefore they are identical, not two.
Absence of space and time removes the only possible differentiators.
4
Examine the other possibilities
If one god is superior, the superior is the true god; the inferior is not divine. If one is inferior, the same applies. Thus all three scenarios are impossible.
The term 'god' implies the highest and most majestic.
5
Conclude the proof
Since every possible relationship between two claimants to divinity leads to impossibility, the very claim of a partner is rationally impossible.
Unity is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجه استحالة كونه مثلَهُ من كل وجه : أنَّ كلَّ اثنين هما متغايران، فإنْ لم يكن تغاير .. لم تكن الاثنينية معقولة، فإنا لا نعقل سوادين إلا في محلين، أو في محلّ واحد في وقتين، فيكون أحدهما مفارقاً للآخر ومبايناً له ومغايراً؛ إما في المحل، وإما في الوقت، والشيئان يتغايران تارةً بتغاير الحد والحقيقة؛ كتغاير الحركة واللون؛ فإنهما وإن اجتمعا في محل واحد في وقت واحد .. فهما اثنان؛ إذ أحدهما مغاير للآخر بحقيقته فإن استوى اثنان في الحقيقة والحد؛ كالسوادين .. فيكون الفرقُ بينهما : إما في المحل، أو في الزمان، فإن فُرضَ سوادان مثلان في جوهر واحد في حال واحد .. كان محالاً؛ إذ لم تعرف الاثنينية ولو جاز أن يقال : هما اثنان ولا مغايرة.. لجاز أن يشار إلى إنسان واحد ويقال : إنه إنسانان، بل عشرة، ولكنهم متساوون متماثلون في العوارض واللوازم من غير فُرقان! وذلك محال بالضرورة. فإن كان ند الله تعالى مساوياً له في الحقيقة والصفات.. استحال وجوده؛ إذ ليس يغايره بمكان؛ إذ لا مكانَ، ولا بزمان؛ إذ لا زمان، فإنهما قديمان، فإذن .. لا فرقان، وإذا ارتفع كلُّ فرق .. ارتفع العدد بالضرورة، ولزمت الوحدة ومحال أن يقال : يخالفه بكونه أرفع منه؛ فإنَّ الأرفع هو الإله، إذ الإله عبارة عن أجل الموجودات وأرفعها، والآخر المقدر ناقص ليس بإلله، ونحن إنما نمنع العدد في الإلهية، والإله هو الذي يقال فيه بالقول المطلق : إنه أرفع الموجودات وأجلها وإن كان أدنى منه كان محالاً؛ لأنه ناقص، ونحن نعبر بالإله عن أجل الموجودات، فلا يكون الأجل إلا واحداً، وهو الإله والمفضي إلى هذه الاحتمالات الثلاث هو دعوى وجود الشريك، وهي المحال
The proof of the impossibility of Him being equal from every aspect: that every two [things] are distinct; if there is no distinction, then duality is not conceivable. For we do not conceive two blacks except in two places, or in one place at two times, so that one of them is separate from the other, distinct and different; either in place or in time. And two things sometimes differ by difference of definition and essence, like the difference between motion and color; for even if they coexist in one place at one time, they are two, since one differs from the other in its essence. If two are equal in essence and definition, like two blacks, then the difference between them is either in place or in time. If two blacks exactly alike are supposed in one substance at one instant, that is impossible, for duality is not known. And if it were permissible to say: they are two without distinction, then it would be permissible to point to one individual and say: he is two individuals, nay ten, but they are equal and alike in accidents and concomitants without any distinction! That is necessarily impossible. If a rival to Allah the Exalted were equal to Him in essence and attributes, His existence would be impossible, for He is not distinguished by place since there is no place, nor by time since there is no time, for they are eternal. Thus there is no distinction, and if every distinction is removed, number is necessarily removed, and unity follows. And it is impossible to say: He differs from Him by being higher, for the higher is the god, since 'god' means the most majestic and highest of existents, and the other supposed [being] is deficient, not a god. We only deny number in divinity, and the god is the one of whom it is said absolutely: He is the highest and most majestic of existents. And if He were lower than Him, it would be impossible because He is deficient, and we refer by 'god' to the most majestic of existents, so the most majestic is only one, and He is God. And what leads to these three possibilities is the claim of the existence of a partner, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the impossibility of multiple gods by examining the three possible relationships between two claimants to divinity: equality, superiority, and inferiority. Ghazali first establishes a general principle: two distinct things must have some basis of differentiation (al-taghayur). He uses the example of two blacks: they are distinguished either by location or by time. If both are exactly alike in essence and exist in the same substance at the same time, they are actually one, not two. Applying this to the divine, if there were a god equal to Allah in essence and attributes, they would have no differentiation since they are both eternal (no time) and not in place (no space). Thus they would be identical, not two. If one were superior, the superior would be the true god, and the inferior would not be a god. If one were inferior, the inferior would be deficient, not a god. Thus all three possibilities are impossible, so the very claim of a partner is impossible.

المغايرة
المثل
الإله
الأرفع
الأجل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could coexist if one is slightly higher in rank, e.g., a greater god and a lesser god.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the term 'god' (al-ilah) denotes the most majestic of existents. Therefore any being that is inferior or equal cannot be called 'god' in the absolute sense. The concept of 'god' inherently implies supremacy, so only one can be supreme.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is it impossible for two gods to be equal in every respect?
A) Because they would need to be in different places or times, but God is not limited by space or time.
B) Because they would both be eternal, so they would be the same being.
C) Because equality is impossible in existence.
D) Because the Quran explicitly says there is only one God.

Key Takeaway
The existence of two equal gods is rationally impossible because they would lack any basis for distinction, making them identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even for a moment, feared the opinion of a creature more than the judgment of Allah? What does this show about your belief in His exclusive supremacy?
?
If you truly internalized that no rival to Allah can exist—not in power, nor in influence, nor in provision—what would you change about how you spend your time and energy today?
?
When you catch yourself thinking 'If only I had this person's support' or 'I am afraid of what they might do,' are you not, in practice, treating them as a rival to Allah's control? How will you remind yourself of Ghazali's proof next time?

Refutation of Dividing Creation Among Multiple Gods
Ghazali refutes the objection that creation could be divided among several creators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the idea that multiple gods could divide creation among themselves is rationally impossible, based on Ghazali's argument that any distribution leads to conflict of powers.

Core Idea
Multiple gods sharing creation inevitably fall into conflict, making the division impossible.

In this passage, Ghazali responds to an objection that acknowledges a single most majestic being but suggests creation could be distributed among multiple creators. The objector demands a proof. Ghazali provides one by analyzing possible distributions and showing each leads to contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the passage all the way through. Notice how the objector concedes the name 'God' is for the most majestic being, but still proposes dividing creation among multiple creators.
The objector is not denying God's existence but only the oneness of the act of creation.
2
Identify the two categories of division
Ghazali states that the proposed distributions fall into two types: either a complete division (one creates all bodies, the other all accidents) or a partial division (each creates some bodies and accidents). Recognize these two.
The complete division is mentioned as an alternative, but Ghazali focuses on disproving the partial division.
3
Understand the proof against partial division
Focus on Ghazali's argument: if the creator of some part (e.g., heaven) is also able to create another part (earth), then both creators share equal power over all things, leading to conflict. This makes partial division impossible.
The impossibility stems from the principle that two equally powerful agents cannot both be the exclusive cause of one effect.
4
Conclude the overall impossibility
Having shown partial division impossible, and implying total division is also untenable (since bodies and accidents are interdependent), Ghazali concludes that any distribution among multiple creators is impossible.
The overall argument reinforces tawheed by showing that only one Creator can exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: بِمَ تنكرون على مَنْ لا ينازعُكُم في اتحاد من يُطْلَقُ عليه اسم الإله، مهما كان الإله عبارة عن أجل الموجودات، ولكنه يقول : العالم كلُّه ليس بمخلوقِ خالق واحد، بل هو مخلوق خالقين؛ أحدهما مثلاً : خالقُ السماء، والآخرُ : خالق الأرض، أو أحدهما : خالق الجمادات، والآخرُ : خالق الحيوانات وخالق النبات، فما المحيلُ لهذا ؟ فإن لم يكن على استحالة هذا دليل .. فمِنْ أين ينفعُكُم قولكم : إن اسم الإله لا يطلق على هؤلاء ؟ فإن هذا القائل يعبر بالإله عن الخالق، أو يقول : أحدهما : خالقُ الخير، والآخرُ : خالقُ الشر، أو أحدهما : خالقُ الجواهر، والآخرُ : خالقُ الأعراض؛ فلا بدَّ من دليل على استحالة ذلك ؟ فنقول : يدلُّ على استحالة ذلك : أن هذه التوزيعات للمخلوقات على الخالقين في تقدير هذا السائل . . لا تعدو قسمين : إما أن يقتضي تقسم الجواهر والأعراض جميعاً؛ حتى يخلق أحدهما بعض الأجسام والأعراض دون البعض أو يقال : كلُّ الأجسام من واحد، وكل الأعراض من الآخر. وباطل أن يقال : إن بعض الأجسام يخلقه واحدٌ؛ كالسماء مثلاً دون الأرض فإنا نقول : خالق السماء هل هو قادر على خلق الأرض أم لا ؟ فإن كان قادراً كقدرته .. لم يتميز أحدهما في القدرة عن الآخر؛ فلا يتميز في المقدور عن الآخر، فيكون المقدورُ بين قادرين، ولا يكون نسبته إلى أحدهما بأولى من الآخر، وترجع الاستحالة إلى ما ذكرناه من تقدير تزاحم متماثلين من غير فرق، وهو محال .
If it is said: How do you reject someone who does not dispute with you regarding the oneness of the one to whom the name 'God' is applied, as long as 'God' means the most majestic of existents, but he says: The entire world is not the creation of one Creator, but rather the creation of two creators; for example, one is the Creator of the heavens, the other the Creator of the earth, or one is the Creator of inanimate objects, the other the Creator of animals and plants. So what is the impossible about this? If there is no proof of its impossibility, then how does your statement benefit you that the name 'God' is not applied to these [multiple]? For this speaker calls 'God' the creator, or he says: one is the Creator of good, the other the Creator of evil, or one is the Creator of substances, the other the Creator of accidents. So there must be a proof of its impossibility. So we say: The proof of its impossibility is that these distributions of creatures among creators in this questioner's estimation do not go beyond two divisions: either it entails the division of substances and accidents entirely, so that one creates some bodies and accidents to the exclusion of others, or it is said: all bodies are from one, and all accidents from the other. And it is false to say that some bodies are created by one, like the heavens without the earth, for we say: Is the Creator of the heavens able to create the earth or not? If He is able, like His power, then one is not distinguished from the other in power, so they are not distinguished in what is created from the other, so the created thing is between two powerful beings, and its relation to one is not more appropriate than to the other, and the impossibility returns to what we mentioned of supposing the conflict of two similars without distinction, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the possibility of multiple gods dividing creation. The objector concedes that the term 'God' refers to the most majestic being, but suggests that creation could be allocated among multiple creators—e.g., one creates heavens, another earth; or one good, another evil; or one substances, another accidents. The objector demands a direct proof of impossibility. Ghazali responds by dividing the proposed distributions into two categories: either the division is partial (some bodies by one, some by another) or total (all bodies by one, all accidents by another). The second alternative is clearly false because substances and accidents are interdependent. For the first, Ghazali argues that if the creator of the heavens is also able to create the earth, then he shares the same power as the other, so neither has exclusive control; the created thing would be subject to both equally, leading to conflict. If he is unable, then he is deficient and not divine. Thus all divisions lead to contradiction, reverting to the earlier argument against equal rivals.

المقدور
التزاحم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think multiple gods could coexist by each having a distinct domain, like one creating good and another evil, without conflict.
Correction: Ghazali shows that any division of creation among multiple gods leads to logical impossibility because each would have equal power over all things, causing inevitable conflict.

A) One god creates all substances, the other all accidents.
B) One god creates good, the other evil.
C) One god creates some bodies and accidents, the other the rest.
D) All gods share equally in creating everything.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that any division of creation among multiple gods leads to logical impossibility due to conflict of powers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in one God, but do you ever live as though different areas of your life—your career, your health, your relationships—are controlled by separate 'creators'? Reflect on Ghazali's argument: if you truly believed that only Allah has absolute power over everything, what aspect of your life would you stop worrying about today? Can you identify one specific thing you are struggling to trust Allah with, and commit to actively reminding yourself of His sole control whenever that worry arises?
?
Ghazali says that if two creators had equal power, no created thing could exclusively belong to one. How does this expose the futility of dividing your own loyalty between Allah and something else—like wealth, status, or people? Think of a moment this week when you acted more to please someone else than to please Allah. What would it look like to act on the truth that only Allah is the Creator and Sustainer of all, and that no one else holds any real power?
?
If you truly internalized that creation cannot be divided among gods, would you still feel comfortable partially obeying Allah while partially following your desires? Identify a specific area where you are giving yourself 'permission' to disobey (e.g., lying, procrastinating prayer, consuming haram). What step will you take right after this reflection to align that area with the single Creator's command?

Refuting the First Division: Two Gods Creating Similar Things
Ghazali presents the first division and refutes it with detailed proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that two gods cannot divide the creation of similar beings (like substances) without leading to logical impossibility.

Core Idea
If two gods can create one similar thing, they can create all, leading to conflict.

Ghazali begins by considering an objection: why can't multiple gods share creation? He presents a first possibility where each creates a portion of similar things. He then shows this leads to either equal power without distinction or arbitrary limitation, both impossible for a necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Ghazali's opening response
Read the passage from the beginning through the statement that distributions are of two types. Note how the objector suggests dividing creation by types (heaven vs. earth, etc.).
2
Focus on the first division
Identify that the first division is where both gods create similar beings (e.g., both create some bodies). Ghazali then asks whether one god can create what the other creates.
This is about similarity of substances and accidents.
3
Trace the two sub-cases
Follow Ghazali's reasoning: if the god can create the other's portion, then both have equal power leading to conflict; if not, his power is arbitrarily limited, which is impossible because all substances are similar.
Notice how similarity plays a key role.

المخلوق؟ ليس بمخلوقِ خالق واحد ، بل هو مخلوق خالقين ؛ أحدهما مثلاً : خالقُ السماء ، والآخرُ : خالق الأرض ، أو أحدهما : خالق الجمادات ، والآخرُ : خالق الحيوانات وخالق النبات ، فما المحيلُ لهذا ؟ فإن لم الجواهر ، والآخرُ : خالقُ الأعراض ؛ فلا بدَّ من دليل على استحالة ذلك ؟ فنقول : يدلُّ على استحالة ذلك : أن هذه التوزيعات للمخلوقات على الخالقين في تقدير هذا السائل . . لا تعدو قسمين : إما أن يقتضي تقسم والقادرُ على الشيء قادر على مثله التي هي إذا كانت قدرته قديمةً ، بحيث يجوز أن تتعلق بمقدورين ، وقدرة كل واحد منهما تتعلق بعدة من الأجسام والجواهر ، فلم تتقيد بمقدور واحد (۱) ، وإذا جاوز المقدور الواحد على خلاف القدرة الحادثة.. لم يكن بعض الأعداد بأولى من بعض، بل يجب الحكم بنفي النهاية عن مقدوراته ؛ فيدخل كلُّ جوهر ممكن وجوده في قدرته (۲) والقسم الثاني (٢) : أن يقال : أحدهما يقدر على الجواهر ، والآخر على الأعراض ، وهما مختلفان ، فلا يجب من القدرة على أحدهما القدرة على الآخر . وهذا محال ؛ لأن العرض لا يستغني عن الجوهر ، والجوهر لا يستغني عن العرض ؛ فيكون فعل كل واحد منهما موقوفاً على الآخر ، فإذا أراد خالق العرض خلق عرض : فكيف يخلقه وربما لا يساعده خالق الجوهر على خلق الجوهر عند إرادته لخلق العرض ؟! فيبقى عاجزاً متحيراً ، والعاجز لا يكون قادراً ، وكذا خالق الجوهر ؛ إن أراد خلق الجوهر ربما خالفه خالقُ العرض ؛ فيمتنع على الآخر خلق الجوهر ، فيؤدي إلى التمانع فإن قيل : مهما أراد أحدهما خلق جوهر . . ساعده الآخر على العرض تحريجة: نفرض Y ؟ قلنا : هذه المساعدة : هل هي واجبة لا يتصور في العقل خلافها ، أم اتفاقهما على الخلق
Problematization: What is the response? For if it is said: By what do you reject those who do not dispute with you the oneness of the one to whom the name 'god' is applied, concerning the number of gods, as long as 'god' means the most majestic of existents, but he says: The entire world, due to the multiplicity of creatures, is not created by one creator, rather it is created by two creators; one, for example, is the creator of heaven, and the other is the creator of earth, or one is the creator of inanimate objects, and the other is the creator of animals and the creator of plants. So what is the impossibility of this? For if there is no proof of the impossibility of this... then how does your statement benefit you: that the name 'god' is not applied to these? For this speaker uses 'god' to mean creator, or he says: one is the creator of good, and the other is the creator of evil, or one is the creator of substances (jawahir), and the other is the creator of accidents (a‘rad). So there must be a proof of the impossibility of that. So we say: The proof of the impossibility of that is that these distributions of creatures among creators in the estimation of this questioner... do not exceed two divisions: Either it necessitates the division of substances and accidents entirely, so that one creates some bodies and accidents but not others, or it is said: all bodies are from one, and all accidents are from the other. And it is false to say that some bodies are created by one, like heaven for example but not earth. For we say: Is the creator of heaven able to create earth or not? If he is able, as is his power... then one is not distinguished from the other in power, so he is not distinguished from the other in what is capable of being brought into existence (maqdur). Thus the maqdur would be between two capable beings, and its relationship to one is not more appropriate than to the other, and the impossibility returns to what we mentioned of the estimation of conflict (tazahum) between two similar beings without distinction, and that is impossible. And if he is not able to do it... then it is impossible because substances (jawahir) are similar, and their modes of being (akwan) which are specializations in locations are similar, and the one able to do a thing is able to do its like. Since his power is eternal, such that it may attach to two maqdurs, and the power of each one attaches to a number of bodies and substances, so it is not restricted to one maqdur (1). And if it exceeds the one maqdur, unlike temporal power, then some numbers are not more appropriate than others, rather it is necessary to judge the negation of limit for his maqdurat; thus every possible substance enters into his power (2). And the second division: that it be said: one has power over substances, and the other over accidents, and they are different, so power over one does not necessitate power over the other. And this is impossible because accident is not independent of substance, and substance is not independent of accident; thus the action of each one is dependent on the other. So if the creator of accident wants to create an accident, how can he create it when perhaps the creator of substance does not assist him in creating the substance at the moment of his desire to create the accident? Thus he remains incapable and perplexed, and the incapable is not powerful. Similarly for the creator of substance: if he wants to create a substance, perhaps the creator of accident opposes him, so the other is prevented from creating the substance, leading to mutual prevention (tamana‘). For if it is said: Whenever one of them wants to create a substance, the other assists him in creating the accident, problematization: we suppose Y? We say: This assistance: is it obligatory, not conceivable in reason to differ from it, or is it their agreement on creation?
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that multiple gods could divide the creation of the world among themselves. Ghazali presents an objection (qila) and then responds (fa-naqulu).

The objection states: Why do you reject someone who agrees that 'god' means the most majestic being, but denies that there is only one creator? He proposes that the world could be created by two creators—for instance, one creates heaven, another earth; or one creates inanimates, another animals and plants; or one creates good, another evil; or one creates substances, another accidents. He demands a proof of impossibility.

Ghazali's response: The distribution of creatures among creators falls into two divisions: either (1) both gods share in creating both substances and accidents, but each creates a portion, or (2) one creates all substances, the other all accidents.

He rejects the first possibility by examining whether one god (e.g., creator of heaven) is able to create what the other is said to create (e.g., earth). If he is able, then both have equal power over the same type of things, so there is no distinction. Since the things are similar (all substances are similar, all accidents are similar), the power of each would apply equally to all, leading to conflict (tazahum) because two equally powerful beings cannot both bring about the same thing without distinction—this is impossible. If he is not able to create the other's portion, then his power is limited to one type, which is also impossible because all substances are similar; if his power is eternal and can attach to multiple things, it cannot arbitrarily be limited to one without a balancing factor, so it must extend to all similar substances.

He then rejects the second division: one creates substances, the other accidents. This is impossible because substances and accidents are interdependent—accidents cannot exist without substances, and substances cannot exist without accidents. Thus each creator's action depends on the other. If the creator of accident wants to create an accident, he must wait for the creator of substance to create a substance; if the latter does not cooperate, the former is helpless. Similarly, the creator of substance needs the creator of accident. This leads to mutual prevention (tamana‘), where each can be thwarted by the other, meaning neither is truly powerful—contradicting their divinity.

Finally, a further objection: What if they always cooperate, so that whenever one wants to create, the other assists? Ghazali asks whether this assistance is necessary by nature (obligatory) or accidental agreement. If it is necessary, then they are not independent agents. If it is accidental, it is not guaranteed, so the possibility of disagreement remains, proving the impossibility.

Thus, Ghazali concludes that any distribution of creation among multiple gods leads to logical impossibility, affirming that there must be one unique creator.

Key Terms
توزيع
متماثل
جوهر
قدرة
مقدور
تزاحم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that two gods could cooperate by each specializing in a different subset of similar creation (e.g., one creates all heavens, the other all earths).
Correction: Ghazali shows that since all substances are similar, the power of each god cannot be limited to a subset without a distinguishing factor, leading either to conflict or arbitrary limitation.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali argue that if the creator of heaven is able to create earth, then the first division is impossible?
A) Because the creator of heaven would then have no need for another god.
B) Because both would have equal power over the same type of thing, leading to conflict.
C) Because the creator of heaven would be limited in power.
D) Because substances are not similar.

Key Takeaway
Similarity of all substances forces that any god able to create one could create all, making any division arbitrary and impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems beyond your control, do you ever mentally assign independent power to people, nature, or chance—as if they were ‘co-creators’ with Allah? How does Ghazali's proof of the impossibility of sharing power expose the futility of that assumption?
?
Think of an area in your life where you tend to rely on your own strength or someone else's rather than Allah alone. Reflect deeply: are you, in that moment, functionally believing in a ‘second creator’ for that domain? What step can you take today to return to absolute tawheed in that area?
?
Have you ever felt that Allah 'specializes' in spiritual matters while worldly affairs are left to human effort? How does Ghazali's argument that all substances are similar force you to reconsider the reach of Allah's power over every particle of your life?

Refuting the Second Division: Substances and Accidents
Ghazali refutes the second division and addresses a cooperation objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that two gods cannot divide creation into one creating substances and the other creating accidents, and why cooperation between them is impossible.

Core Idea
Substances and accidents depend on each other, so separate creators would be helpless without the other's cooperation.

Ghazali now turns to the second possible division: one god creates all substances, the other all accidents. He argues that this is impossible because substances and accidents are mutually interdependent—an accident cannot exist without a substance, and a substance cannot exist without an accident. This leads to each creator being dependent on the other, resulting in mutual prevention (tamana‘) and thus inability.

Guided Path
1
Read the second division and its argument
Read from 'والقسم الثاني' to the end of the passage. Notice how Ghazali describes the interdependence and the resulting dependence of actions.
2
Understand the dependence
Accidents require substances as substrates, and substances require accidents for differentiation. So each creator's act requires the other's act.
3
Follow the objection and response about cooperation
A further objection suggests they could cooperate, but Ghazali asks whether this cooperation is necessary or accidental. If necessary, they are not independent; if accidental, it is not guaranteed, so the possibility of disagreement remains.

فإنا نقول : خالق السماء هل هو قادر على خلق الأرض أم لا ؟ فإن كان قادراً كقدرته .. لم يتميز أحدهما في القدرة عن الآخر ؛ فلا يتميز في المقدور عن الآخر ، فيكون المقدورُ بين قادرين ، ولا يكون نسبته إلى أحدهما بأولى من الآخر ، وترجع الاستحالة إلى ما ذكرناه من تقدير تزاحم متماثلين من غير فرق ، وهو محال . وإن لم يكن قادراً عليه .. فهو محال ؛ لأن الجواهر متماثلة ، وأكوانها اختصاصات بالأحياز متماثلة ، والقادرُ على الشيء قادر على مثله التي هي إذا كانت قدرته قديمةً ، بحيث يجوز أن تتعلق بمقدورين ، وقدرة كل واحد منهما تتعلق بعدة من الأجسام والجواهر ، فلم تتقيد بمقدور واحد (۱) ، وإذا جاوز المقدور الواحد على خلاف القدرة الحادثة.. لم يكن بعض الأعداد بأولى من بعض، بل يجب الحكم بنفي النهاية عن مقدوراته ؛ فيدخل كلُّ جوهر ممكن وجوده في قدرته (۲) والقسم الثاني (٢) : أن يقال : أحدهما يقدر على الجواهر ، والآخر على الأعراض ، وهما مختلفان ، فلا يجب من القدرة على أحدهما القدرة على الآخر . وهذا محال ؛ لأن العرض لا يستغني عن الجوهر ، والجوهر لا يستغني عن العرض ؛ فيكون فعل كل واحد منهما موقوفاً على الآخر ، فإذا أراد خالق العرض خلق عرض : فكيف يخلقه وربما لا يساعده خالق الجوهر على خلق الجوهر عند إرادته لخلق العرض ؟! فيبقى عاجزاً متحيراً ، والعاجز لا يكون قادراً ، وكذا خالق الجوهر ؛ إن أراد خلق الجوهر ربما خالفه خالقُ العرض ؛ فيمتنع على الآخر خلق الجوهر ، فيؤدي إلى التمانع فإن قيل : مهما أراد أحدهما خلق جوهر . . ساعده الآخر على العرض تحريجة: نفرض Y ؟ قلنا : هذه المساعدة : هل هي واجبة لا يتصور في العقل خلافها ، أم اتفاقهما على الخلق
Problematization: What is the response? For if it is said: By what do you reject those who do not dispute with you the oneness of the one to whom the name 'god' is applied, concerning the number of gods, as long as 'god' means the most majestic of existents, but he says: The entire world, due to the multiplicity of creatures, is not created by one creator, rather it is created by two creators; one, for example, is the creator of heaven, and the other is the creator of earth, or one is the creator of inanimate objects, and the other is the creator of animals and the creator of plants. So what is the impossibility of this? For if there is no proof of the impossibility of this... then how does your statement benefit you: that the name 'god' is not applied to these? For this speaker uses 'god' to mean creator, or he says: one is the creator of good, and the other is the creator of evil, or one is the creator of substances (jawahir), and the other is the creator of accidents (a‘rad). So there must be a proof of the impossibility of that. So we say: The proof of the impossibility of that is that these distributions of creatures among creators in the estimation of this questioner... do not exceed two divisions: Either it necessitates the division of substances and accidents entirely, so that one creates some bodies and accidents but not others, or it is said: all bodies are from one, and all accidents are from the other. And it is false to say that some bodies are created by one, like heaven for example but not earth. For we say: Is the creator of heaven able to create earth or not? If he is able, as is his power... then one is not distinguished from the other in power, so he is not distinguished from the other in what is capable of being brought into existence (maqdur). Thus the maqdur would be between two capable beings, and its relationship to one is not more appropriate than to the other, and the impossibility returns to what we mentioned of the estimation of conflict (tazahum) between two similar beings without distinction, and that is impossible. And if he is not able to do it... then it is impossible because substances (jawahir) are similar, and their modes of being (akwan) which are specializations in locations are similar, and the one able to do a thing is able to do its like. Since his power is eternal, such that it may attach to two maqdurs, and the power of each one attaches to a number of bodies and substances, so it is not restricted to one maqdur (1). And if it exceeds the one maqdur, unlike temporal power, then some numbers are not more appropriate than others, rather it is necessary to judge the negation of limit for his maqdurat; thus every possible substance enters into his power (2). And the second division: that it be said: one has power over substances, and the other over accidents, and they are different, so power over one does not necessitate power over the other. And this is impossible because accident is not independent of substance, and substance is not independent of accident; thus the action of each one is dependent on the other. So if the creator of accident wants to create an accident, how can he create it when perhaps the creator of substance does not assist him in creating the substance at the moment of his desire to create the accident? Thus he remains incapable and perplexed, and the incapable is not powerful. Similarly for the creator of substance: if he wants to create a substance, perhaps the creator of accident opposes him, so the other is prevented from creating the substance, leading to mutual prevention (tamana‘). For if it is said: Whenever one of them wants to create a substance, the other assists him in creating the accident, problematization: we suppose Y? We say: This assistance: is it obligatory, not conceivable in reason to differ from it, or is it their agreement on creation?
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that multiple gods could divide the creation of the world among themselves. Ghazali presents an objection (qila) and then responds (fa-naqulu).

The objection states: Why do you reject someone who agrees that 'god' means the most majestic being, but denies that there is only one creator? He proposes that the world could be created by two creators—for instance, one creates heaven, another earth; or one creates inanimates, another animals and plants; or one creates good, another evil; or one creates substances, another accidents. He demands a proof of impossibility.

Ghazali's response: The distribution of creatures among creators falls into two divisions: either (1) both gods share in creating both substances and accidents, but each creates a portion, or (2) one creates all substances, the other all accidents.

He rejects the first possibility by examining whether one god (e.g., creator of heaven) is able to create what the other is said to create (e.g., earth). If he is able, then both have equal power over the same type of things, so there is no distinction. Since the things are similar (all substances are similar, all accidents are similar), the power of each would apply equally to all, leading to conflict (tazahum) because two equally powerful beings cannot both bring about the same thing without distinction—this is impossible. If he is not able to create the other's portion, then his power is limited to one type, which is also impossible because all substances are similar; if his power is eternal and can attach to multiple things, it cannot arbitrarily be limited to one without a balancing factor, so it must extend to all similar substances.

He then rejects the second division: one creates substances, the other accidents. This is impossible because substances and accidents are interdependent—accidents cannot exist without substances, and substances cannot exist without accidents. Thus each creator's action depends on the other. If the creator of accident wants to create an accident, he must wait for the creator of substance to create a substance; if the latter does not cooperate, the former is helpless. Similarly, the creator of substance needs the creator of accident. This leads to mutual prevention (tamana‘), where each can be thwarted by the other, meaning neither is truly powerful—contradicting their divinity.

Finally, a further objection: What if they always cooperate, so that whenever one wants to create, the other assists? Ghazali asks whether this assistance is necessary by nature (obligatory) or accidental agreement. If it is necessary, then they are not independent agents. If it is accidental, it is not guaranteed, so the possibility of disagreement remains, proving the impossibility.

Thus, Ghazali concludes that any distribution of creation among multiple gods leads to logical impossibility, affirming that there must be one unique creator.

جوهر
قدرة
إله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that two gods could be specialists—one for substances, one for accidents—and cooperate harmoniously.
Correction: Ghazali shows that interdependence makes them helpless without the other, so they cannot be truly powerful. Even if they always cooperate, that cooperation is either necessary (negating free will) or accidental (not guaranteed).

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali argue that the second division (one god for substances, another for accidents) leads to impossibility?
A) Because substances and accidents are independent of each other.
B) Because each god's action requires the other's cooperation, leading to potential helplessness.
C) Because accidents are inferior to substances.
D) Because the gods would disagree on which accidents to create.

Key Takeaway
Mutual dependence of substances and accidents makes separate creation impossible, because each creator would be helpless without the other's cooperation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you feel helpless because you are waiting for someone else to act first? How does this lesson challenge you to rely only on Allah, who is completely independent and self-sufficient?

If you truly believed that only Allah has absolute power independent of any other being, what would change about the way you seek help from others? Would your dua become more urgent?
?
The argument shows that any supposed partner would be dependent and thus not divine. Are there 'partners' in your heart—people, possessions, or status—that you treat as if they give you power or security? How will you dismantle that reliance today?

Refuting the Dualistic Claim: Evil is Not Essential
Ghazali refutes the objection that two gods could be assigned to good and evil respectively.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is absurd to assign one god to create good and another to create evil, based on Ghazali's argument that evil is relative, not essential.

Core Idea
Evil's relativity destroys the case for two gods.

Some might think that since evil and good seem opposite, they could be created by separate gods. Ghazali dismantles this by showing that evil is not an intrinsic quality but depends on context. This lesson walks through his logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Ghazali's response
Read the Arabic passage and translation to see the flow of argument.
Pay attention to the initial objection and how Ghazali labels it 'nonsense'.
2
Understand the key premise
Notice that Ghazali says evil is not evil in its essence. This is the linchpin of his argument.
3
Analyze the burning example
See how the same act of burning can be good or evil depending on the person's faith. This demonstrates relativity.
Observe that the power to burn does not change with context.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Because the power to burn is the same, a single god must be able to do both, leading to conflict if there were two.
The conclusion is mutual prevention (tamānuʿ) and conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فيكونُ أحدهما خالق الشر ، والآخر خالق الخير . أحدهما يخلق الخير قلنا : هذا هوس ؛ لأن الشرَّ ليس شراً لذاته ، بل هو من حيث مساو للخير ومماثل له ، والقدرة على الشيء قدرة على مثله ؛ فإنَّ إحراق بدن المسلم بالنار شر وإحراق بدن الكافر خيرٌ ودفع شر ، والشخص الواحد إذا تكلم بكلمة الإسلام .. انقلب الإحراق في حقه شراً ؛ فالقادر على إحراق لحمِهِ بالنار عند سكوته عن كلمة الإيمان .. لا بدَّ وأن يقدر على إحراقه عند النطق بها ؛ لأن نطقه بها صوت ينقضي ، لا يغيرُ ذات اللحم ، ولا ذاتَ النار ، ولا ذات الإحراق ، ولا يقلبُ جنساً ؛ فتكون الإحراقات متماثلة فيجب تعلق القدرة بالكل ؛ ويقتضي ذلك تمانعاً وتزاحماً
For if it is said: Then one of them would be the creator of evil, and the other the creator of good. One of them creates good. We say: This is nonsense; because evil is not evil in its essence, but rather it is from the aspect that it is equal and similar to good, and power over a thing is power over its like. For burning the body of a Muslim with fire is evil, and burning the body of an unbeliever is good and repelling evil. And the same person, if he speaks the word of Islam, the burning becomes evil in his case. So the one who has power to burn his flesh with fire when he is silent about the word of faith must necessarily have power to burn it when he utters it; because his utterance of it is a sound that passes away, it does not change the substance of the flesh, nor the substance of the fire, nor the substance of the burning, nor transform its kind. Thus the burnings are similar, so power must be related to all of them, and that necessitates mutual prevention and conflict.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection claims that if there were two gods, they could be assigned to good and evil respectively. Ghazali rejects this as nonsense (هوس). His argument: evil is not evil in its essence (لذاته) but is relative. For example, burning a Muslim's body is evil, but burning an unbeliever's body is good. The same burning changes in judgment depending on the person's state. The ability to burn does not change with the person's utterance of faith; it is the same power. Therefore, if one god could burn in one case, he must be able to burn in the other. This leads to mutual conflict (تمانع وتزاحم) between two gods, which is absurd. The key point is that good and evil are not essential attributes of actions but are relational, so dividing creation between two gods is impossible.

شر
خير
ذات
قدرة
تمانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think good and evil are intrinsic properties of actions.
Correction: Ghazali shows they are relative to context, so the same action can be good or evil, making a dualistic creator division untenable.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's argument, why is it absurd to assign one god to create good and another to create evil?
A) Because good and evil are essential properties that cannot be separated.
B) Because evil is relative and not essential, so the same action can be good or evil depending on context.
C) Because a single god must create both good and evil to maintain balance.
D) Because the objection is based on a misunderstanding of divine power.

Key Takeaway
Evil is not an intrinsic quality; its relativity undermines any attempt to attribute its creation to a separate god.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial you label as 'evil,' do you ever stop to ask whether its harm is absolute or relative? How might this lesson change the way you react to difficulty?
?
Think of a person you consider a 'bad' person. Can you see any of their actions as good in a different context? What does that reveal about your own judgments?
?
If the same act can be good for one person and evil for another, then your own sins are not inherently evil—they become evil due to divine command. Does this truth make you feel more accountable or more lenient? How will you respond?

Concluding Argument and the Tenth Claim
Ghazali summarizes the proof against two gods and announces the tenth claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali summarizes the proof against multiple gods using the Quran and identifies the remaining topic of the first pole.

Core Idea
The Quran itself proves that multiple gods lead to disorder, and the only remaining matter is God's immunity from temporality.

Having established the impossibility of two gods through rational arguments, Ghazali now turns to the Quranic affirmation of that proof. He then announces the tenth and final claim of this pole: that God cannot be a locus for temporal events. This bridges the discussion of existence to that of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the Quranic verse is cited as a proof text.
2
Observe the Quranic proof
Ghazali uses the Quranic verse (21:22) as a concise summary: multiple gods lead to corruption. See how he says 'no need to add to the clarification of the Quran.'
3
Identify the tenth claim
Locate the statement: 'the impossibility of His being a locus for temporal events.' That is the tenth claim that concludes the first pole.
4
Note the connection to attributes
Ghazali mentions this claim will be discussed later in the context of attributes, as a refutation of those who say attributes like knowledge are temporal.
This shows the systematic flow of the book.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى الجملة : كيفما فُرضَ الأمرُ .. تولَّدَ منه اضطراب وفساد ، وهو الذي أراده الله تعالى بقوله : ( لَوْ كَانَ فِيهِمَا الهَهُ إِلَّا اللَّهُ لَفَسَدَنَا ) فلا مزيد على بیان القرآن ولنختم هذا القطبَ بالدعوى العاشرة ، فلم يبق مما يليق بهذا الفن إلا بيان استحالة كونه محلاً للحوادث ، وسنشير إليه في أثناء الكلام في الصفات ؛ رداً على من قال بحدوث العلم والإرادة وغيرهما
And in summary: However the matter is supposed, disorder and corruption arise from it. And that is what Allah Almighty intended by His saying: 'If there were other gods besides Allah, both would have been ruined.' So there is no need to add to the clarification of the Quran. And let us conclude this pole with the tenth claim. Nothing remains that is suitable for this art except the explanation of the impossibility of His being a locus for temporal events, and we will refer to it in the course of discussion on the attributes, as a refutation of those who say that knowledge, will, and others are temporal.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a summary and transition. Ghazali concludes that any supposition of two gods leads to disorder, as the Quran says. Then he announces the tenth and final claim of the first pole: the impossibility of God being a locus for temporal events (محل للحوادث). He notes this will be discussed later in the section on attributes, as a refutation of those who claim that God's knowledge and will are temporal. This shows the interconnectedness of the topics.

Key Terms
اضطراب
فساد
محل للحوادث
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The tenth claim is an isolated topic unrelated to divine attributes.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly says he will refer to it in the discussion on attributes, as a refutation of those who claim attributes are temporal.

Checkpoint
What is the tenth claim mentioned by Ghazali at the end of the first pole?
A) The impossibility of two gods.
B) The impossibility of God being a locus for temporal events.
C) The necessity of God's knowledge being eternal.
D) The distinction between good and evil.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic proof against multiple gods is decisive, and the final claim of the first pole prepares the ground for the discussion of divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Quranic verse 'If there were other gods besides Allah, both would have been ruined' is presented as decisive. Do you truly believe that any 'god' competing for your heart—be it wealth, status, or comfort—will lead to ruin in your life? What specific false god are you tolerating right now, and what will you do this week to dethrone it by affirming Allah's sole lordship?
?
Ghazali concludes that the only remaining discussion is about God's immunity from temporal events. This means your concept of Allah must be free from any suggestion of change or need. Have you ever caught yourself thinking of Allah as if He changes His mind or is affected by your actions? How can you train your heart to see Him as the Perfect, Unchanging One, and what verse or name of Allah will you meditate on today to reinforce that?
?
The passage bridges the proof of God's existence to the discussion of His attributes. This implies that knowing Allah is not complete with just belief in His existence—you must know His names and attributes. Have you been lazy in learning about Allah's attributes? What is one attribute you will study deeply this week, and how will you let it transform your worship?

Allah's Existence

4 min
The Impossibility of an Eternal Act
Al-Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim of the world's eternity using the definition of act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Ghazali argues that an act of God cannot be eternal and why the philosophers' claim leads to absurd infinite cycles.

Core Idea
An act that never begins is not an act.

Al-Ghazali opens his refutation by attacking the very definition of an 'act'. The philosophers call the world an act of God yet claim it is eternal. Al-Ghazali shows this is a contradiction: an act must come into existence after non-existence. This lesson examines that foundational argument and its consequence of infinite cycles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Al-Ghazali defines 'act' (fi‘l) and why he says it cannot be eternal.
The term 'qadim' (eternal) means without beginning.
2
Understand the definition of act
Al-Ghazali says the meaning of an act is 'it was not and then it was'. Relate this to the concept of creation from nothing.
3
Reflect on the implication of infinite cycles
Al-Ghazali asserts that an eternal world would require infinite cycles, which is impossible. Think about why infinite regress is problematic in kalam.
This is a standard argument against the eternity of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على الفلاسفة : أما الفلاسفة : فقد قالوا بقدم العالم ، وهو محال ؛ لأن الفعل يستحيل أن يكون قديماً ؛ إذ معنى كونه فعلاً : أنه لم يكن ثم كان ، فإن كان موجوداً أبداً فكيف يكون فعلاً ؟! بل يلزم من ذلك تقدير دوراتٍ لا نهاية لها على ما سبق ، وهو محال من وجوه . ثم إنهم مع اقتحام هذا الإشكالِ لم يتخلَّصوا من أصل السؤال ؛ وهو أن الإرادة لم تعلَّقتْ بالحدوث في وقت مخصوص لا قبله ولا بعده مع تساوي نسب الأوقات إلى الإرادة ؟ فإنهم إن تخلصوا عن خصوص الوقت .. لم يتخلصوا عن خصوص الصفات ؛ إذ العالم مخصوص بمقدار مخصوص ووضع مخصوص وكانت نقائضها ممكنة في العقل ، والذات القديمة بالصفات لا تناسب بعض الممكنات دون بعض ، ومن أعظم ما يلزمهم فيه ـ ولا عذر عنه - أمران أوردناهما في كتاب ( تهافت الفلاسفة ) ، ولا محيص لهم عنهما ألبتة : أحدهما : أنَّ حركاتِ الأفلاك بعضُها مشرقية ؛ أي : من المشرق إلى المغرب ، وبعضُها مغربيَّةٌ ؛ أي: من مغرب الشمس إلى المشرق ، وكان عكس ذلك في الإمكان مساوياً له ؛ إذ الجهات في الحركات متساوية ، فكيف لزم من الذات القديمة أو من ذواتِ الملائكة ـ وهي قديمة عندهم ـ أن تعين جهة عن جهة تقابلها وتساويها من كل وجه ؟! وهذا لا جواب عنه . الثاني : أن الفلك الأقصى الذي هو الفلك التاسع عندهم ، المحرَّكُ لجميع السماوات بطريق القهر في اليوم والليلة مرة واحدة .. يتحرَّك على قطبين ؛ شمالي وجنوبي ، والقطبُ : عبارةٌ عن النقطتين المتقابلتين على الكرة ، الثابتتين عند حركة الكرة على نفسها ، والمنطقة : عبارة عن دائرة عظيمة على وسط الكرة بعدها من القطبين واحد . فنقول : جرم الفلك الأقصى متشابه ، وما من نقطة إلا ويتصوَّرُ أن تكونَ قطباً ؛ فما الذي أوجب تعيين نقطتين من بين سائر النقط التي لا نهاية لها ؟
The refutation of the philosophers: As for the philosophers, they have asserted the eternity of the world, but this is impossible because an act cannot be eternal; for the meaning of its being an act is that it was not and then it was. If it existed always, how could it be an act? Rather, that would entail positing infinite cycles, as previously mentioned, and it is impossible on multiple counts. Then, even as they plunge into this difficulty, they do not escape from the original question: why did the will attach to occurrence at a particular time and not before or after, given that the relations of times to the will are equivalent? For if they escape the particularity of time, they do not escape the particularity of attributes, since the world is specified by a specific quantity and a specific configuration, and their opposites are possible in the intellect, and the eternal essence with [its] attributes does not render some possibilities more appropriate than others. Among the greatest problems that follow for them—and from which there is no excuse—are two matters that we have presented in the book 'The Incoherence of the Philosophers', and they have absolutely no escape from them: First: that some of the movements of the spheres are easterly, i.e., from east to west, and some are westerly, i.e., from the west of the sun to the east, and the opposite was equally possible, since directions in movements are equivalent. So how did it follow from the eternal essence or from the essences of the angels—which are eternal according to them—to specify one direction over another that is symmetrical and equivalent in every respect? This has no answer. Second: that the outermost sphere, which is the ninth sphere according to them, the mover of all the heavens by compulsion once every day and night, moves on two poles: a northern and a southern. The pole is the two opposite points on the sphere that are stationary during the sphere's rotation on itself, and the equinoctial line is a great circle on the middle of the sphere, its distance from the two poles being equal. So we say: the body of the outermost sphere is homogeneous, and there is no point that could not be imagined to be a pole. So what necessitated the specification of two points out of the infinite points?
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' position that the world is eternal. Al-Ghazali presents a series of arguments. First, he states that the very concept of an 'act' implies temporal origination (it was not, then it was); an eternal act is a contradiction. Moreover, positing an eternal world leads to infinite cycles, which is absurd. Second, even granting their eternity, they cannot answer the problem of specification: why did the world begin at a particular time? If they evade that by denying temporal beginning, they still face the problem of why the world has its specific size and shape rather than alternatives, given that all possibilities are equally possible. The eternal essence alone cannot provide a sufficient reason for such specification. Third, Al-Ghazali brings two specific arguments from his Tahafut al-Falasifa. The first is about the directions of celestial motions: some spheres move eastward, some westward, yet the opposite directions are equally conceivable. No necessary cause from an eternal essence or eternal angels can explain why one direction was chosen over its symmetric opposite. The second argument concerns the poles of the outermost sphere: since the sphere is homogeneous, any pair of points could serve as poles. Why were just these two points specified? The philosophers have no rational explanation, so they must resort to an arbitrary divine choice—which is exactly the Sunni position of an eternal will that specifies without cause. Al-Ghazali thus turns their own inability to explain into a proof for the necessity of an uncreated, specifying will.

Key Terms
قدم العالم
الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' belief that an act can be eternal without temporal origination.
Correction: An act, by definition, must have a beginning. An eternal act is not an act at all.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Ghazali, why is an eternal act impossible?
A) Because God cannot act eternally
B) Because the meaning of 'act' implies it was not then was
C) Because the philosophers deny it
D) Because it would require infinite cycles

Key Takeaway
An act that never begins is not an act; therefore, the world's claim to be both an act of God and eternal is self-contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship—like prayer or charity—do you sense it as a genuine beginning in that moment, or do you perform it on autopilot? What would change if you deliberately started each act as if it were your first?
?
Al-Ghazali says an eternal act is a contradiction. Are there areas of your life where you claim consistency yet hold contradictory beliefs? Examine one such inconsistency and commit to resolving it today.
?
The philosophers assumed an eternal world could still be an act. How often do you assume things about God or His creation without checking whether your definitions align? What step can you take this week to let truth, not assumption, define your belief?

The Problem of Specification: Time and Attributes
Al-Ghazali presents the problem of specification regarding the time and attributes of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers cannot explain the specific time and attributes of the world without invoking a divine will that specifies without cause.

Core Idea
Why this world, at this time, with this shape? Reason alone cannot answer.

Even if the philosophers evade the problem of temporal origination, Al-Ghazali presents them with another dilemma: the world has a particular quantity, configuration, and—if they admit a beginning—a specific moment of creation. The eternal essence and attributes are indifferent to possibilities, so how can one possibility be actualized? This lesson examines that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on specification
Read the Arabic and translation. Al-Ghazali first points to the problem of time specification, then to attribute specification.
The term 'takhsis' means specification.
2
Identify the two levels of specification
First: why the world began at a particular time. Second: why it has its specific size and configuration. Both are problematic for the philosophers.
If they deny temporal beginning, only the second applies.
3
Understand the reasoning
Al-Ghazali argues that the eternal essence and attributes are not sufficient to render one possibility more appropriate than another, so a specifying will is needed.

Two Objections from Astronomy: Directions and Poles
Al-Ghazali presents two specific objections from astronomy against the philosophers' eternity claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ghazali's two astronomical arguments that force the philosophers to accept a divine will that specifies without cause.

Core Idea
Why do spheres move one way and not the other? Why are the poles here and not there? Reason has no answer.

Al-Ghazali presents two devastating objections from the philosophers' own astronomical framework. The first concerns the differing directions of celestial motions, the second the specification of the poles of the outermost sphere. Both arguments show that the philosophers cannot explain these features without invoking an arbitrary divine choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the two objections
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the first objection about the directions (easterly/westerly) and the second about the poles of the ninth sphere.
These are from Al-Ghazali's Tahafut al-Falasifa.
2
Understand the symmetry principle
In both cases, Al-Ghazali points out that the alternatives (opposite direction, different poles) are equally possible given the symmetry of the heavens.
3
Realize the conclusion
Since reason cannot account for the specification, the philosophers must admit a divine will that arbitrarily chooses one possibility over another.
This aligns with the Sunni position on divine will.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the night sky and see the stars moving in fixed patterns, do you ever wonder why they go this way and not that? Al-Ghazali says reason has no answer. Does that humble your intellect? In what area of your life are you secretly trusting your own reasoning over Allah's will, even when you cannot see the reason?
?
The philosophers argued that the universe follows necessary laws. Al-Ghazali shows a crack: the arbitrary directions and poles. If you truly believed that every detail of the cosmos—including your own existence—is not necessary but freely chosen by Allah, would you live differently tonight? What would change?
?
Al-Ghazali says there is 'no escape' for the philosophers. But what about you? Do you have an area where you insist on a rational explanation before submitting to Allah's command? Can you think of one thing you are resisting simply because you cannot see the wisdom in it? Will you surrender it to the Will that specified the poles?
