Ghazali's Refutation of the Analogy of Non-Existence
Ghazali refutes an analogy between silence and non-existence of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the non-existence of the world is not analogous to God's attribute of silence, because the world lacked a prior essence while God's essence is eternal.

Core Idea
The world had no essence before existence; God's essence is continuous.

In this passage, Ghazali responds to a clever objection: if silence is a positive attribute of God before He speaks, then why isn't the non-existence of the world a positive attribute? He shows the key difference lies in the subject. The world had no prior essence, while God's essence is eternal and known in different states.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ghazali uses a very condensed style.
2
Identify the objection
The opponent claimed that if silence is a positive attribute, then by analogy the non-existence of the world before creation would also be a positive attribute. Find where Ghazali states this.
3
Notice Ghazali's key point
Ghazali points out a crucial difference: the world had no essence at all before its existence, so there is no continuous subject. In contrast, God has an eternal essence that is known in different modes (silence and speech).
This is the heart of the refutation.
4
Understand the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that the analogy fails because the two cases are not parallel. He emphasizes that this balancing is exact with no escape.
Reflect on why the analogy is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يلزم على هذا الفرق بين وجود العالم وعدمه ؛ فإن ذلك يوجب ذاتين ، فإنه لم تُدرك في الحالتين ذات واحدةٌ يطرأ عليها الوجود ، بل لا ذات للعالم قبل الحدوث ، وللقديم ذات قبل حدوثِ الكلام ، عُلِمَ على وجه مخالف للوجه الذي عُلِمَ عليه بعد حدوث الكلام ، يعبر عن الوجه بالسكوت وعن هذا بالكلام ، فهما وجهان مختلفان أُدركت عليهما ذات مستمرة الوجودِ في الحالتين ، وللذات هيئة وصفة وحالة بكونه ساكتاً كما أنَّ له هيئة بكونه متكلماً ، وكما له هيئة بكونه ساكناً ومتحركا وأبيض وأسود ، وهذه الموازنة مطابقة لا مخرج منها ألبتة
And this [objection] does not necessitate [accepting] a distinction between the existence of the world and its non-existence. For that would require two essences, because a single essence that existence befalls it was not perceived in the two states. Rather, the world has no essence before its coming-to-be, while the Eternal [God] has an essence before the occurrence of speech, known in a manner different from the manner in which it is known after the occurrence of speech. The former manner is expressed as 'silence' and the latter as 'speech'. They are two different modes in which an essence that continues to exist in both states is perceived. And for the essence there is a form, description, and state of being silent just as it has a form of being speaking, and just as it has a form of being still, moving, white, and black. And this balancing is exact with no way out of it at all.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection. The opponent had argued that if silence is a positive attribute, then by analogy, the non-existence of the world before creation would also be a positive attribute, which is absurd. Ghazali replies by pointing out a crucial difference: the world had no essence at all before its creation, so there is no continuous subject that changes. In contrast, God has a continuous essence that is perceived in two different modes: as silent and as speaking. These are two distinct states of a single enduring essence. Therefore, the analogy fails. He emphasizes that this is an exact match with no escape.

Key Terms
الوجود
العدم
الذات
السكوت
الكلام
الهيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence of the world is analogous to silence; both are positive attributes.
Correction: Ghazali shows the difference: the world had no prior essence, while God has a continuous essence. The analogy is false.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali reject the analogy between silence and non-existence of the world?
A) Because the world had no essence before existence, while God has continuous essence.
B) Because silence is a positive attribute but non-existence is not.
C) Because the world is eternal.
D) Because God cannot be described as silent.

Key Takeaway
The non-existence of the world is not analogous to God's attribute of silence because the world had no essence before existence, while God's essence is eternal and perceived in different modes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may have thought of God's silence as a period of absence or inactivity. After reading this lesson, do you realize that God's essence was always fully present and His silence was a positive mode? How does this challenge your assumptions about when God is 'speaking' in your life? Will you now look for His constant presence even when He seems silent?
?
The world had no essence before God created it. Your own existence is entirely from Him. Does your daily life reflect that you are a contingent being, or do you act as if you have an independent essence? What would change if you truly lived with the constant awareness that your existence is borrowed?
?
Ghazali teaches that God's essence remains the same while being perceived in different modes. Have you ever thought that God's favor or anger changes His essence? How does this understanding of God's unchanging essence affect your confidence in His reliability? Will you trust Him more knowing that His essence never wavers?

Imagination and Illusion as Drivers of Action
Ghazali illustrates the power of imagination over action with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's explanation of how imagination and illusion can override rational judgment in driving human actions, using examples of eating while fasting and sexual arousal.

Core Idea
Imagination moves the body even when reason says no.

Ghazali begins by giving concrete examples of how a person can be driven by imagination despite rational knowledge: imagining food while fasting leads to salivation, and sexual imagery triggers physical arousal. He then reveals that God has created our internal forces to be habitually obedient to imagination and illusion, not just reason. This psychological foundation is crucial for his later argument about coerced speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on imagination and action
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the two examples Ghazali provides.
Notice that the person knows rationally but is still moved.
2
Identify the two examples
Find the example of eating despite fasting and the example of sexual arousal. Note how Ghazali describes the physical reactions.
In the second example, he lists specific physiological details.
3
Understand the key principle
Ghazali states that God created these forces to be obedient to imagination and illusion, whether reason assists or not. This is the main point.
This principle explains why actions can occur against rational judgment.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this principle applies to free will and moral responsibility. Why do you think Ghazali emphasizes this?
This foundation is needed for the next lesson on coerced speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحسب التخيل وإن كان الشخص عالماً بأنه ليس يريد الإقدام على الأكل بصوم أو بسبب آخر. وكذلك يتخيَّلُ الصورة الجميلة التي يشتهي مجامعتها، فإذا ثبت ذلك في الخيال انبعثت القوَّةُ الناشرة لآلة الفعل، وساقت الرياح إلى تجاويف الأعصاب وملأتها، وثارت القوَّةُ المأمورة بصب المذي المرطب المعين على الوقاع. وذلك كله مع التحقق بحكم العقل لا للامتناع عن الفعل في ذلك الوقت، ولكن خلق الله تعالى هذه القوى بحكم جري العادة مطيعة مسخرة تحت حكم الخيال والوهم، ساعد العقلُ الوهم أو لم يساعد، فهذا وأمثالُهُ ترجيح أحد جانبي الفعل على الآخر، وكل ذلك راجع إلى الأغراض. فأما النطق بكلمة الكفر وإن كان كذلك فلا يستقبحه العاقل تحت السيف ألبتة، بل ربما يستقبحُ الإصرار، فإن استحسن الإصرار فله سببان: أحدهما: اعتقاده أن الثوابَ على الصبر والاستسلام أكثر. والآخر: ما ينتظرُ من الثناء عليه بصلابته في الدين؛ فكم من شجاع يمتطي متن الخطر ويتهجم على عدد جَم يعلم أنه لا يطيقُهُمْ ويستحقرُ ما ينالُهُ بما يعتاضُهُ عنه من لذَّةِ الثناء والحمدِ بعد موته. وكذلك الامتناع عن نقض العهد سببه ثناءُ الخلق على من يفي بالعهود وتواصيهم به على مرّ الأوقات لما فيها من مصالح الناس، فإن قدر حيث لا ينتظر ثناء فسببه حكم الوهم من حيث إنه لم يزل مقروناً بالثناء الذي لذيذ، والمقرون باللذيذ لذيذ؛ كما أن المقرون بالمكروه مكروه كما سبق من الأمثلة. فهذا ما يحتمله هذا المختصرُ من بثّ أسرار هذا الفصل، وإنما يعرفُ قدرَهُ مَنْ طَالَ في المعقولات نظره، وقد استفدنا بهذه المقدمة إنجاز الكلام في الدعاوى؛ فلنرجع إليها.
According to imagination, even if the person knows that he does not intend to proceed with eating due to fasting or another reason. And similarly, he imagines the beautiful image that he desires to copulate with. When that is established in the imagination, the force that spreads the instrument of action is aroused, and it drives winds into the cavities of the nerves and fills them, and the force commanded to discharge the lubricating fluid that aids intercourse arises. And all that is while being certain of the judgment of reason, not due to abstaining from the act at that time, but God Most High created these forces by the rule of habit, obedient and subjugated under the rule of imagination and illusion, whether reason assisted illusion or not. This and its like are a preponderance of one side of the action over the other, and all that goes back to purposes. As for uttering the word of disbelief, even if it is [considered] ugly, the rational person does not find it ugly under the sword at all; rather, he might find persistence ugly. If he finds persistence good, he has two reasons: one is his belief that the reward for patience and submission is greater. The other is what he expects of praise for his firmness in religion. For how many a brave person rides the back of danger and attacks a large group while knowing he cannot overpower them, and he scorns what befalls him for what he receives in exchange of the pleasure of praise and commendation after his death. Likewise, refraining from breaking a covenant is caused by the praise of people for those who fulfill covenants and their continuous exhortation to it over time, because of the interests of the people in it. If it happens where no praise is expected, its cause is the judgment of illusion because it has never ceased to be associated with praise, which is pleasurable, and what is associated with the pleasurable is pleasurable; just as what is associated with the abominable is abominable, as examples have preceded. This is what this summary can contain of divulging the secrets of this chapter. Only he whose reflection in intellectual matters is prolonged knows its value. We have benefited from this introduction in completing the discourse on the claims, so let us return to them.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument explaining the psychological mechanisms behind human actions, particularly how imagination and illusion can override rational judgment, and applying this to the specific case of uttering words of disbelief under duress. The author, Imam al-Ghazali, begins by describing how a person can imagine eating even when knowing he is fasting, or imagine a beautiful image leading to sexual arousal and physical reactions, all while reason is aware but does not prevent the action. He states that God created these forces to be habitually obedient to imagination and illusion, whether reason supports them or not. This shows that actions can be driven by these subrational forces, leading to a preponderance of one side of an action over another, all based on purposes. Then he turns to the case of speaking the word of disbelief under the sword: a rational person does not consider it ugly at all; rather, he might consider persistence in refusal ugly. If a person does choose to persist, there are two reasons: first, the belief that the reward for patience and submission to death is greater; second, the expectation of praise for steadfastness in religion, similar to a brave warrior who risks his life for the pleasure of praise after death. Ghazali then extends this to breaking covenants: refraining from breaking is due to the praise of people for faithfulness over time, and when no praise is expected, the cause is the illusion that the act is always associated with pleasure, and what is associated with the pleasurable becomes pleasurable itself. He concludes that this summary has disclosed the secrets of the chapter, and that only he who has deep reflection in intellectual matters appreciates its value, and he now returns to the main claims.

Key Terms
تخيل (imagination)
وهم (illusion)
قوى (forces)
مذي (lubricating fluid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason always controls our actions.
Correction: Ghazali shows that imagination and illusion can override rational judgment, causing physical actions even when reason knows better.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what drives the physical reactions in the example of sexual arousal?
A) Rational decision to engage in intercourse
B) The imagination of a beautiful image
C) The command of reason
D) Fear of social consequences

Key Takeaway
Imagination and illusion can compel physical actions even when reason knows better, revealing a fundamental aspect of human psychology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time your imagination of a pleasure led you to act against what your reason clearly knew was better? How does this expose your reliance on a power beyond reason? What will you do today to train your imagination to submit to your faith?
?
You cannot deny that your body reacts automatically to certain images—whether of food or of people. Does this reality frighten you or humble you? How will you guard your eyes and thoughts to prevent your imagination from enslaving you?
?
Ghazali says these forces are created obedient to habit. What one habit of imagination do you need to break—not just avoid, but actively replace—so that your reason regains its rightful authority? What concrete step will you take this week?

Uttering Disbelief Under Duress: Rational and Motivational Factors
Ghazali applies the psychological mechanism to coerced speech and examines reasons for persistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that uttering the word of disbelief under threat is not inherently ugly, and the two motivations for choosing to persist in refusal: greater reward and expectation of praise.

Core Idea
Under the sword, speaking kufr is not blameworthy; persistence has reasons.

Having established the power of imagination over action, Ghazali now applies this to the case of being forced to utter disbelief. He argues that a rational person does not find such speech ugly when under the sword; rather, persistence without necessity is considered ugly. If one chooses to persist, it is due to either a belief in greater reward or the desire for posthumous praise. He extends this reasoning to covenant-breaking, showing how praise and association shape decisions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on coerced speech
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice Ghazali's opening claim about the rational person.
He contrasts the ugliness of the act itself with ugliness of persistence.
2
Identify the two reasons for persistence
Find the two reasons Ghazali lists for choosing to persist in refusing to utter kufr: greater reward and expectation of praise.
The second reason is illustrated with the example of a brave warrior.
3
Understand the extension to covenants
Ghazali explains that refraining from breaking a covenant is due to praise or association with pleasure.
This shows the general principle of motivation by praise and association.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this passage completes the psychological framework: actions under duress are evaluated based on motivations, not just the act itself.
Ghazali ends by noting the value of this analysis for the broader discussion.

God's Actions Are Not Obligatory
Ghazali presents the first claim that God's creating and obligating are not obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that God's creating and obligating are not obligatory in the ordinary sense, his definition of wājib, and how he responds to the Mu'tazila objection that such actions are required for the benefit of creation.

Core Idea
God's actions are not obligatory because obligation implies harm or impossibility, neither of which applies to Allah.

Ghazali opens his discussion of divine actions by tackling a foundational claim: that God is not obligated to create or to impose obligations. To defend this, he clarifies what 'obligatory' actually means, showing that it cannot apply to God in any meaningful way—except perhaps in reference to His eternal will and knowledge. He then anticipates and rebuts a Mu'tazila objection that God acts for the benefit of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it, then read the English translation. Notice the flow: claim, opposing view, definition of wājib, proof, objection, response.
This passage is dense; give yourself time to absorb each part.
2
Identify the claim and the opposing view
Locate the first sentence where Ghazali states the claim that God may not create and is not obliged. Then find where he reports the Mu'tazila position. Contrast the two.
The Mu'tazila say creating and obligating are obligatory upon God.
3
Understand Ghazali's definition of wājib
Focus on the sentence beginning with 'فإنا بيَّنَّا أن المفهوم عندنا من لفظ الواجب'. Note the two criteria: harm to the abandoner, or impossibility of the opposite. Reflect on why neither applies to God.
Harm is impossible for Allah, and abandoning creation/obligation does not entail any impossibility unless tied to His eternal will.
4
Follow the objection and Ghazali's response
After the proof, Ghazali introduces an objection that God is obligated for the benefit of creation. In his response, he argues that the objection merely gives a cause without clarifying the meaning of 'obligation' itself. Ask yourself: why does Ghazali insist the objection fails?
The objection does not define wājib in a way that is intelligible for God.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى الأولى
أنه يجوز الله تعالى ألا يخلُقَ، وإذا خلَقَ فلم يكن ذلك واجباً عليه، لا يجب على الله تعالى الخلق، وإذا خلقَهُمْ فله ألا يكلّفَهم، وإذا كلَّفَهُمْ فلم يكن ذلك واجباً عليه.
وقالت طائفة من المعتزلة : يجب عليه الخلق، والتكليف بعد الخلق.
وبرهانُ الحقِّ فيه أن نقول : قول القائل: الخلقُ والتكليف واجب.. غيرُ مفهوم؛ فإنا بيَّنَّا أن المفهوم عندنا من لفظ الواجب: ما ينال تاركه ضررٌ إما عاجلاً وإما أجلاً، أو ما يكون نقيضُه محالاً، والضرر محال في حق الله سبحانه، وليس في ترك التكليف وترْكِ الخلق لزومُ محال إلا أن يقال: كان ذلك إلى خلاف ما سبق به العلم في الأزل، وما سبقت به المشيئة في الأزل، فهذا حقٌّ، وهو بهذا التأويل واجبٌ؛ فإن الإرادة إذا فرضت موجودةً، أو العلم إذا فرض متعلقاً بالشيء.. كان حصول المراد والمعلوم واجباً لا محالة.
فإن قيل: إنما يجب عليه ذلك لفائدة الخلق لا لفائدة ترجع إلى الخالق.
قلنا: الكلامُ في قولكم: ( لفائدة الخلق ).. للتعليل، والحكم المعلَّلُ هو الوجوبُ، ونحن نطالبُكُمْ بتفهيم الحكم، فلا يغنيكُمْ ذكرُ العلَّةِ، فما معنى قولكم: إنه يجب لفائدة الخلق ؟ وما معنى الوجوب ونحن لا نفهم.
The first claim: That Allah the Exalted may not create, and if He creates, then that is not obligatory upon Him. Creating is not obligatory upon Allah the Exalted, and if He creates them, then He may not obligate them, and if He obligates them, then that is not obligatory upon Him. A group of the Mu‘tazila said: Creating is obligatory upon Him, and obligation after creation. The proof of the truth in this is that we say: The statement of the speaker: 'Creating and obligating are obligatory' is not understood; for we have explained that what is understood by us from the word 'obligatory' (wājib) is: that whose abandoner incurs harm either immediately or eventually, or that whose opposite is impossible. Harm is impossible with respect to Allah the Exalted, and there is not in abandoning obligation and abandoning creating any occurrence of impossibility, unless it is said: That would be contrary to what was previously known by knowledge in eternity and what was previously willed by will in eternity. This is true, and in this interpretation it is obligatory; for if will is assumed to exist, or knowledge is assumed to be related to a thing, then the attainment of the willed and the known is necessarily obligatory. If it is said: He is only obligated to do that for the benefit of creation, not for a benefit returning to the Creator. We say: The discussion concerning your statement 'for the benefit of creation' is for causal explanation, and the judgment being explained is obligation, and we demand of you an understanding of the judgment, so mentioning the cause does not suffice you. So what is the meaning of your statement: 'He is obligated for the benefit of creation'? And what is the meaning of obligation, while we do not understand?
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the first claim in Ghazali's discussion of divine actions, addressing whether God is obligated to create and impose obligations. Ghazali begins by stating the claim: God may not create, and if He creates, He may not impose obligations; none of these are obligatory upon Him. He then reports the opposing view of a group of Mu‘tazila, who assert that creating and imposing obligations after creation are obligatory upon God. The proof of the truth follows: the word 'obligatory' (wājib) is not properly understood when applied to God. Ghazali defines 'obligatory' as that whose abandonment results in harm (immediate or eventual) or that whose opposite is impossible. Harm is impossible for God, and abandoning creation or obligation does not entail an impossibility—unless it would contradict God's eternal knowledge and will. If God's will or knowledge is already fixed to something, then the occurrence of that thing is indeed necessary, and in that sense, it can be called 'obligatory' per this interpretation. Ghazali then addresses an objection: that God is obligated for the benefit of creation, not for His own benefit. In reply, he argues that the objection merely states a cause ('for the benefit of creation') without explaining what 'obligation' means here. The cause is offered to explain the judgment, but the judgment itself (obligation) is undefined. Without a clear meaning of 'obligation' as applied to God, the objection fails to be intelligible. Ghazali thus upholds the original claim that God's actions are not subject to obligation in the ordinary sense.

Key Terms
وجوب (wājib)
تكليف (taklīf)
علة (ʿilla)
ضرر (ḍarar)
محال (muḥāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the meaning of 'obligatory' (wājib)?
A) That which reason deems good
B) That whose abandoner incurs harm or whose opposite is impossible
C) That which benefits creation
D) That which God wills

Key Takeaway
Ghazali establishes that God's actions are not obligatory in the human sense, because obligation implies harm or impossibility, which cannot be attributed to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that God is not obliged to create you or command you. Have you ever lived as if God owes you something—health, guidance, or ease? What would change if you fully internalized that even your existence is a pure gift, not an obligation?
?
Ghazali defines obligation as that whose neglect brings harm or impossibility. Since harm is impossible for God, any thought that God 'should' act differently reflects a human standard. Where in your life have you felt God 'should' have done something for you? What does it mean to surrender that expectation while still trusting His wisdom?
?
The Mu'tazila said God must act for the benefit of creation. Have you ever assumed God's actions must make sense to you or serve your benefit? How does this lesson challenge you to worship God as He is, not as you think He ought to be?

God May Burden Beyond Capacity
Ghazali states the second claim and notes Mu'tazila opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari claim that God may burden His servants with what they cannot bear and the Mu'tazila denial of this.

Core Idea
God's power is absolute; He can oblige the impossible.

Imam al-Ghazali presents the second of his claims in the debate on divine power and human obligation. The issue is whether God’s command is limited by human capacity. The Mu'tazila argued that God cannot command what is impossible, while the Ash'aris affirm His unrestricted authority. This short passage marks the fault line between the two schools.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Ghazali introduces the claim and then specifies its scope.
The phrase 'ما يطيقونه وما لا يطيقونه' is key.
2
Identify the opposing position
Note that the Mu'tazila denied this. Ask yourself why they would reject it.
Consider their views on divine justice.
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about how this claim relates to God's power and human responsibility. Does it contradict justice?
This will be explored in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى الثانية له سبحانه أن يكلف ندعي : أن الله تعالى أن يكلف عبادَهُ ما يطيقونه وما لا يطيقونه وذهبت المعتزلة إلى إنكار ذلك
The second claim: He, glorified, may burden. We claim: That Allah may burden His servants with what they can bear and what they cannot bear. And the Mu'tazila went to denial of that.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a statement of a doctrinal claim. Ghazali presents the second claim in his argument: that Allah has the power to impose obligations upon His servants that they are capable of fulfilling and also obligations that they are not capable of fulfilling. He then notes that the Mu'tazila denied this, holding that God cannot burden anyone with what they cannot do. This sets up the position of the Ash'ari school, which affirms the absolute power of God, though later discussions (not in this passage) distinguish between logical impossibility and actual non-occurrence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God can command the impossible, He would be unjust or arbitrary.
Correction: Ash'aris hold that justice is not a standard independent of God; rather, whatever God does is just by definition.

Checkpoint

A) They affirm it is possible
B) They deny it
C) They remain silent
D) They say it is obligatory

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school affirms that God may command what is beyond human ability, while the Mu'tazila reject this to safeguard divine justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command from Allah that feels impossible to fulfill—like abandoning a deep-rooted sin or praying through exhaustion—do you trust that He knows your limits better than you do? Or do you let your own estimation of inability become an excuse? What would full submission look like in that moment?
?
The Mu'tazila denied burden beyond capacity because they thought it unjust. Have you ever resisted a difficult obligation—in your deen or in life—because you believed it was unfair or beyond you? How does the Ash'ari position challenge that attitude and call you to humble reliance on Allah's wisdom?
?
If Allah can indeed command the impossible, then no perceived incapacity is a valid escape from obedience. Is there an area of your life where you have been hiding behind 'I can't' instead of saying 'I will try, and rely on Allah'? What step will you take today to act on this truth?

Allah's Power to Inflict Pain on the Innocent
Ghazali states the third claim and refutes Mu'tazila and reincarnation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's affirmation that Allah can inflict pain on innocent beings without obligation of reward, and how this refutes Mu'tazili and transmigration views.

Core Idea
Allah's power is absolute; suffering in creation does not constrain Him.

Ghazali tackles a challenging question: can Allah cause pain to innocent animals, children, or the insane without being required to compensate them? The Mu'tazila argued this would be evil, so Allah must provide reward. Others resorted to reincarnation. In this lesson, we see Ghazali anchor the discussion in observed reality and divine sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to absorb the flow. Notice the three distinct positions: the claim, the Mu'tazili view, the transmigration view, and Ghazali's own conclusion.
The Arabic has a bracketed unclear word '[تعليب؟]' — do not let that distract.
2
Identify the rival views
Locate the Mu'tazili argument: they say inflicting pain on the innocent is impossible because it's evil, so Allah must resurrect and reward every harmed creature. Then find the transmigration view: souls return to new bodies to receive pleasure. Understand why Ghazali rejects both.
The Mu'tazili position leads to absurd obligations on Allah.
3
Grasp Ghazali's conclusion
Ghazali states that inflicting pain on the innocent is possible and indeed witnessed. Note that he does not say it always happens, but that it is within Allah's power and occurs in reality.
This is an empirical argument: we see suffering, so it cannot be impossible.
4
Reflect on implications
Ask yourself: why does this matter for understanding Allah's attributes? What does it say about divine justice and power? This lesson defends Allah's absolute sovereignty and freedom from external obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى الثالثة: أن الله تعالى قادر على إيلام الحيوان البريء عن الجنايات له سبحانه [تعليب؟] ولا يلزمه عليه ثواب. وقالت المعتزلة: إن ذلك محال؛ لأنه قبيح؛ ولذلك لزمهم المصير إلى أن كل بق وبرغوث إذا أوذي بفرك أو صدمة فإن الله تعالى يجب عليه أن يحشره ويثيبه عليه بثواب. وذهب ذاهبون إلى أن أرواحها تعود بالتناسخ إلى أبدان أخر، وينالها من اللذة ما يقابل تعبها، وهذا مذهب لا يخفى فساده. ولكنا نقول: أما إيلام البريء عن الجناية من الحيوان والأطفال والمجانين فمقدور، بل هو مشاهد محسوس.
The third claim: that Allah Most High is capable of inflicting pain on the animal that is innocent of crimes — to Him belongs [?] — and He is not obligated to give it reward. And the Mu'tazila said: that is impossible, because it is evil; therefore they were compelled to hold that every flea and louse, if harmed by rubbing or a blow, Allah Most High must resurrect it and give it reward for it. And some people held that their souls return through transmigration to other bodies, and they attain pleasure commensurate with their suffering; and this school is of no hidden corruption. But we say: as for inflicting pain on the innocent — animals, children, and the insane — it is possible, indeed it is witnessed and perceived.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents the third doctrinal claim (al-da'wa al-thalitha) in Ghazali's argument: that Allah is capable of inflicting pain on innocent animals without being obliged to reward them. It first states the claim, then responds to the Mu'tazila, who argue that this is impossible because it would be evil (qabih), and therefore they are compelled to hold that if any small creature like a flea or louse is harmed, Allah must resurrect and reward it. Then it mentions a deviant view (transmigration) that seeks to compensate the suffering through rebirth, which Ghazali rejects as plainly corrupt. Finally, Ghazali affirms the Ash'ari position: such infliction of pain is possible, and in fact it is observed and witnessed — i.e., it occurs in reality, so it cannot be impossible.

Key Terms
إيلام
بريء
ثواب
قبيح
تناسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that if Allah inflicts pain on the innocent, He must compensate them with reward, otherwise it would be unjust.
Correction: Ghazali shows that Allah is not obligated by any external standard; His actions are not subject to human notions of good and evil. The Mu'tazila view leads to absurdities like requiring resurrection and reward for every flea.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the Mu'tazili view on inflicting pain on animals?
A) It is possible but not required.
B) It is impossible because it is evil, so Allah must reward them.
C) It is possible but Allah must not do it.
D) It is impossible because animals have no souls.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali affirms that Allah can inflict pain on the innocent without obligation, as observed in reality, refuting both Mu'tazili and transmigration views.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a child suffering from illness or an animal in pain, do you ever feel anger toward Allah? This lesson teaches that such suffering is within His power and He is not obligated to reward. Will you now submit to His absolute sovereignty, or will you let your emotions lead you to question His wisdom?
?
If you have ever believed that God must compensate every innocent suffering with reward, how does Ghazali's claim challenge that assumption? Examine your heart: are you ready to accept that Allah may allow pain without any perceived justice in this world? What would it take for you to trust Him completely, even when you do not understand?
?
The Mu'tazila tried to reason that inflicting pain on the innocent is evil, so Allah cannot do it. Yet you see suffering every day. Will you insist on your own reasoning and fall into arguments like the Mu'tazila, or will you humble yourself and accept that Allah's actions are not bound by human notions of fairness? What change in your daily life must follow from this truth?

God's Power to Harm the Innocent Without Reward
Ghazali states the third claim and refutes the Mu'tazila on reward obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that God is not obligated to reward innocents He harms, and why the Mu'tazila view is untenable.

Core Idea
God's actions are not bound by human concepts of obligation.

This passage presents the third key claim in Ghazali's discussion of divine attributes: God can inflict pain on innocent beings without being required to compensate them. The Mu'tazila object, but Ghazali shows their view leads to absurdities like resurrecting every flea. He then dismantles their notion of obligation, reaffirming God's absolute sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the flow: claim, objection, consequence, refutation.
Pay attention to the mention of reincarnation as a rejected alternative.
2
Identify the key groups
Spot the Mu'tazila position, the reincarnationists, and Ghazali's own stance. How does each view the issue of reward?
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila argument
The Mu'tazila say harming innocents is 'ugly' and thus God must reward them. What absurdity does Ghazali derive from this?
Think about the implication for every flea or bug.
4
Examine Ghazali's refutation
Ghazali returns to the concept of 'obligatory' (wājib). Why can no meaning of obligation apply to God? Refer to earlier discussions if needed.
This is the core of the argument: God is not subject to external obligations.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why Ghazali includes this claim in his theological work. How does it defend divine omnipotence and justice from a different angle?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى الثالثة: أن الله تعالى قادر على إيلام الحيوان البريء عن الجنايات له سبحانه تعالى ولا يلزمه عليه ثواب. وقالت المعتزلة: إن ذلك محال؛ لأنه قبيح؛ ولذلك لزمهم المصير إلى أن كل بقة وبرغوث إذا أوذي بفرك أو صدمة.. فإن الله تعالى يجب عليه أن يحشره ويثيبه عليه بثواب. وذهب ذاهبون إلى أن أرواحها تعود بالتناسخ إلى أبدان أخر، وينالها من اللذة ما يقابل تعبها، وهذا مذهب لا يخفى فساده. ولكنا نقول: أما إيلام البريء عن الجناية من الحيوان والأطفال والمجانين.. فمقدور، بل هو مشاهد محسوس. فيبقى قول الخصم: إن ذلك يوجب عليه الحشر والثواب بعد ذلك. فنعود إلى معاني الواجب، وقد بان استحالته في حق الله تعالى، وإن فسروه بمعنى رابع فهو غير مفهوم.
The third claim: that God Almighty is able to inflict pain on the innocent animal, free from crimes, for His sake, and He is not obligated to reward it. The Mu'tazila said: that is impossible because it is ugly; therefore they are forced to hold that every flea and bug, if harmed by rubbing or impact, God must resurrect it and reward it with a reward. And some people went to the view that their souls return through reincarnation to other bodies, and they receive pleasure corresponding to their suffering, and this doctrine is not hidden in its corruption. But we say: as for inflicting pain on the innocent from animals, children, and the insane, it is possible, indeed it is witnessed and perceived. Then remains the opponent's statement: that obligates resurrection and reward after that. So we return to the meanings of the obligatory (wājib), and its impossibility with respect to God Almighty has become clear, and if they interpret it with a fourth meaning, it is unintelligible.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the third claim in Ghazali's discussion: God is able to inflict pain on innocent beings (animals, children, insane) without being obligated to reward them. The Mu'tazila object that this is impossible because it is 'ugly' (qabīḥ), and thus they are forced to absurd conclusions—such as God having to resurrect every flea to reward it for its suffering. Some have resorted to reincarnation to explain compensation, which Ghazali dismisses as clearly corrupt. Ghazali then affirms the possibility of such pain, as it is observed. He returns to the opponent's argument that this obligates reward and then refers to the earlier discussion of the meanings of 'obligatory' (wājib), concluding that no meaning of obligation applies to God, and any fourth meaning they propose is unintelligible.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this claim implies God is unjust or arbitrary.
Correction: Ghazali is not denying God's justice; he is showing that God is not bound by human notions of obligation. God's actions are just by definition, but 'obligation' as a concept cannot be applied to the Creator.

The Fifth Claim: Reward and Punishment Are Not Obligatory Upon God
Ghazali states the fifth claim and defends it against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that God is not obligated to reward obedience or punish disobedience, and the exception based on God's promise.

Core Idea
God’s reward is a gift, not a debt; only His promise makes it obligatory.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a key theological question: Is God required to reward those who obey Him or punish those who disobey? He argues that such obligations would limit God's sovereignty. However, he makes an important exception based on God's truthful promise. This discussion highlights the Ash'ari stance on divine justice and the nature of good and evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Let Ghazali's own argument come through before analysis.
2
Identify the two key arguments
Look for how Ghazali supports his claim: (a) taklif is an act of disposal over slaves, and (b) reward is a separate gratuitous act. Understand why these make reward non-obligatory.
3
Examine the objection and response
Notice the objection that withholding reward is 'evil' (qabih). Pay attention to Ghazali's two-part response: distinguishing between contradicting God's purpose vs. the servant's purpose.
4
Note the exception due to God's promise
Ghazali concedes that breaking a promise is impossible for God, so reward becomes obligatory in that sense. This is the only basis for obligation.
5
Reflect on divine justice
Think about how this view preserves God's absolute sovereignty while still affirming His truthfulness. Consider how this differs from anthropomorphic notions of justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يجب عليه سبحانه ثواب ولا عقاب الدعوى الخامسة ندعي : أن الله تعالى إذا كلَّفَ العباد فأطاعوه .. لم يجب عليه الثوابُ ، بل إن شاء .. أثابَهُم ، وإن شاء .. عاقبهم ، وإن شاء .. أعدمهم ولم يحشرهم ، ولا يبالي لو غفر لجميع الكافرين وعاقب جميع المؤمنين ، ولا يستحيل ذلك في نفسه ، ولا يناقض صفة من صفات الإلهية وهذا ؛ لأنَّ التكليف تصرُّفٌ منه في عبيده ومماليكه ، وأما الثواب .. ففعل آخر على سبيل الابتداء ، وكونه واجباً بالمعاني الثلاثة (1) غير مفهوم ، ولا معنى للحسن والقبح ، وإن أريد معنى آخر .. فليس بمفهوم ، إلا أن يقال : إنه يُصيرُ وعده كذباً ، وهو محال ، ونحن نعتقد الوجوب بهذا المعنى ولا ننكره . تحريجة : التكليف فإن قيل : التكليف مع القدرة على الثواب وتركُ الثواب قبيح ؟ مع القدرة على قلنا : إن عنيتُم بالقبيح أنه مخالف غرضَ المكلف .. فقد تعالى المكلف الثواب وتركه قبيح وتقدَّس عن الأغراض ، وإن عنيتُمْ به أنه مخالف غرض المكلف .. فهو مسلَّم ، ولكن ما هو قبيح عند المكلَّف لم يمتنع عليه (۲) فعله ؛ إذ كان القبيحُ والحسن عنده وفي حقه بمثابة واحدة (۳)
It is not obligatory upon Him, Exalted is He, reward or punishment. The Fifth Claim: We claim that if God Almighty imposes obligations upon servants and they obey Him, reward is not obligatory upon Him. Rather, if He wills, He rewards them; if He wills, He punishes them; if He wills, He annihilates them and does not resurrect them. He does not mind if He forgives all disbelievers and punishes all believers. That is not impossible in itself, nor does it contradict any attribute of divinity. This is because the imposition of obligation (taklif) is an act of disposal by Him over His slaves and properties. As for reward, it is another act done as a gratuitous beginning (ibtida'). Its being obligatory in the three meanings is not understood. There is no meaning for 'good' (husn) and 'evil' (qubh) [in this context]. And if another meaning is intended, it is not understood—except that it could be said that it would make His promise a lie, which is impossible. And we believe in obligation in this sense and do not deny it. Query: Concerning imposition of obligation. For if it is said: 'Imposition of obligation while being able to reward, and then withholding reward, is evil?' We say: If you mean by 'evil' that it contradicts the purpose of the one obligated (the servant), then—exalted is the One who imposes obligation—He is far above purposes. And if you mean by it that it contradicts the purpose of the one obligated (the servant), then that is conceded. But what is 'evil' according to the servant is not impossible for Him (God) to do, since 'evil' and 'good' in His regard and concerning His right are equivalent.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth claim of the book: that God is not obliged to reward obedient servants or punish disobedient ones. The author argues that taklif (imposing obligations) is an act of God's sovereignty over His creatures. Reward is a separate act of gratuitous favor, not a necessity. He then refutes the objection that withholding reward is 'evil' (qabih) by distinguishing two meanings of 'evil': (1) contradicting the purpose of the agent—God has no purposes; (2) contradicting the purpose of the servant—this is conceded, but it does not prevent God from doing it, because in God's right, good and evil are equivalent (i.e., not constrained by human ethical categories). However, the author notes that we do affirm obligation in the sense that God's promise (of reward for obedience) must be truthful—otherwise His promise would be false, which is impossible. So the obligation is based on God's promise, not on any inherent necessity.

Key Terms
ثواب (reward)
عقاب (punishment)
التكليف (imposition of obligation)
الحسن والقبح (good and evil)
الوجوب بالوعد (obligation due to promise)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying obligatory reward/punishment implies God is unjust or capricious.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that God's justice is defined by His will and promise; He is not bound by human notions of good and evil.

Checkpoint
What is the exception that makes reward obligatory upon God according to this passage?
A) The inherent goodness of obedience.
B) The need to maintain justice.
C) God's promise, because breaking it would be impossible.
D) The servant's right to reward.

Key Takeaway
God is not obligated by human notions of right and wrong; only His own promise creates a duty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own obedience to God, do you secretly feel that He owes you reward? What would it mean for your heart to truly believe that your good deeds are only possible by His grace and that any reward is a gift, not a wage you deserve?
?
If God were to punish all believers and forgive all disbelievers, would you still submit to Him as the Sovereign? Does your worship depend on the expectation of reward, or is it rooted in pure servitude?
?
How does the truth that your own human sense of 'fairness' does not bind God—because His actions are beyond good and evil as you define them—change the way you approach His commands and His decree in your life?

The Mu'tazila Theory of Sarf and Its Weakness
Ghazali presents and critiques the Mu'tazila theory of sarf regarding the Quran's inimitability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali rejects the Mu'tazila claim that the Quran's miraculousness is due to God's prevention of imitation, and how this view weakens the concept of i'jaz.

Core Idea
The Quran's miracle is not in God's prevention but in its own inimitable nature.

Ghazali here takes on a key Mu'tazila argument about the Quran's inimitability. The Mu'tazila held that the Quran is not inherently miraculous; rather, God simply turned people away from attempting to imitate it. Ghazali finds this unsatisfactory because it makes the miracle dependent on an external act rather than the text itself. This lesson examines his critique and the analogy he uses to expose the flaw.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The passage begins by stating the Mu'tazila view: the Quran is not miraculous in itself; God prevents imitation. Read carefully to grasp this.
2
Analyze the Abu Lahab analogy
Ghazali compares this to God turning Abu Lahab away from faith. Why does he use this example? Consider the implications.
Abu Lahab was challenged but God prevented his belief.
3
Identify Ghazali's critique
Ghazali says this path weakens the miracle (توهين الإعجاز). Find the sentence that states this and reflect on why.
4
Contrast the two views
Notice the contrast between the first view (inherent i'jaz) and the second (sarf). Why does the first view make the miracle clearer?
The first view requires no ongoing prevention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير معجز في نفسه كما يرى ذلك المعتزلة ، والإعجاز أصبح أمراً خارجاً ، مع وجود القدرة على الإتيان بمثله ، فلو لم يصرفهم عن الإتيان بمثله .. لفعلوا ، فصار الأمر كصرف إيمان أبي لهب عنه مع تحدي القرآن له وإخباره بأنه لم يؤمن ؛ فإنه لو آمن ... لأدمغت حجته ، ولكن الله أخبر وصرفه عن الإيمان، وهذا وإن كان ملزماً للنصارى إلا أنه غير مرضي ؛ لما في هذا الطريق من توهين الإعجاز ، فصار الترك إعجازاً ، فلا محاولة أصلاً ، ولا ريب أن خلود إعجازه على الأول أجلى ؛ لأن وجود القرآن وحده كاف ، فهو معجز في نفسه ، أما على الثاني .. فلا بد من وجود صرف وسلب عند كل محاولة معارضة متوهمة. ثم إن للقرآن الكريم أوجهاً كثيرة من الإعجاز ، تناسب أحوال العقول ومداركها.
It is not miraculous in itself, as the Mu'tazila see it. Rather, the miraculousness becomes an external matter, while the ability to produce its like exists. Had He not turned them away from producing its like, they would have done so. So the matter becomes like the turning away of Abu Lahab's faith from him, despite the Quran's challenge to him and its informing that he would not believe. For if he had believed, his argument would have been demolished. But God informed and turned him away from faith. This, although it is binding upon the Christians, is not satisfactory because this path weakens the concept of miracle. So the lack of attempt becomes a miracle, and there is no attempt at all. There is no doubt that the perpetuity of its miracle according to the first view is clearer, because the existence of the Quran alone is sufficient—it is miraculous in itself. As for the second view, there must be a turning away and negation at every supposed attempt at opposition. Moreover, the Noble Quran has many aspects of inimitability that suit the states of minds and their perceptions.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage critiques the Mu'tazila theory of sarf (turning away) regarding the Quran's miraculousness. The Mu'tazila argue that the Quran is not inherently inimitable; rather, God prevented people from imitating it despite their ability to do so. Ghazali rejects this view because it weakens the concept of i'jaz (miracle). He compares it to God's turning Abu Lahab away from believing despite the Quran's challenge and prediction of his disbelief—a point that, while effective against Christians, is unsatisfactory for Muslims. According to the Mu'tazila, the mere absence of any attempt at imitation becomes the miracle, but Ghazali argues that the Quran's ongoing existence as a challenge is sufficient proof of its inherent miraculousness, without needing to posit a constant act of turning away. He then adds that the Quran has many aspects of i'jaz, suited to different intellects.

Key Terms
معجز
المعتزلة
صرف
إعجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran is miraculous only because God prevents people from imitating it; otherwise they could.
Correction: Ghazali argues that this weakens the miracle; the Quran is inherently inimitable, and its existence alone is sufficient proof.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali reject the Mu'tazila view that the Quran's i'jaz is due to God's prevention (sarf)?
A) It contradicts the Quran's challenge.
B) It weakens the concept of miracle by making the lack of attempt the miracle.
C) It implies people could imitate the Quran if God allowed.
D) It was only a view of the Christians.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability is inherent; making it dependent on God's prevention weakens the very concept of a miracle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever truly felt the Quran's inherent miracle in your heart, or have you treated it as just another book whose power depends on your mood or circumstances? What would change if you read it today with the conviction that it is miraculous in itself?
?
Ghazali says the Quran's existence alone is sufficient as a perpetual miracle. Does your life reflect that belief, or do you seek other signs to confirm your faith? Will you commit to meditating on a single verse until its miracle moves you?
?
The Quran challenged Abu Lahab, but he was turned away by his own arrogance. Are there areas in your life where you are turned away from responding to the Quran's challenge—by pride, laziness, or distraction? What will you do differently today to answer that challenge?

The Quran's Inherent Miraculousness and Its Many Facets
Ghazali presents the Quran as inherently miraculous and possessing multiple facets of i'jaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali establishes the Quran's inherent miraculousness and why he emphasizes the Quran's multiple aspects of i'jaz suited to different minds.

Core Idea
The Quran alone is sufficient proof of its divine origin, and its miracle has countless aspects.

After refuting the Mu'tazila theory of sarf, Ghazali asserts the Quran's self-sufficient miraculousness. He argues that the Quran's existence and its ongoing challenge are enough to prove its inimitability. Moreover, he points out that the Quran contains many layers of i'jaz, addressing various levels of understanding. This lesson explores that positive case.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's positive assertion
The passage begins by stating that the perpetuation of the miracle according to the first view is clearer because the Quran alone is sufficient. Pay attention to the phrase 'خلود إعجازه على الأول أجلى'.
2
Understand the implication
Why is the first view clearer? Because it does not require an external act of prevention. Reflect on the logical simplicity.
3
Note the multiple aspects
Ghazali then adds that the Quran has many aspects of i'jaz (وجهاً كثيرة من الإعجاز) suited to different intellects. What does this suggest about the Quran's appeal?
This answers potential objections that not everyone can perceive the miracle.

The Threefold Classification of Religious Knowledge
Ghazali introduces the threefold division of unknown matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's threefold classification of what is not known by necessity: by reason alone, by scripture alone, and by both.

Core Idea
Religious knowledge has three sources: reason, scripture, and both together.

In the introduction to Chapter Two of his work, Ghazali lays out a fundamental classification that structures all subsequent discussion. He teaches that matters not known by direct intuition fall into three categories based on their epistemological source. This lesson unpacks that tripartite division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three categories
Read the Arabic passage carefully and note the three types Ghazali enumerates. Then read the translation.
The categories are clearly marked by 'إلى ما يعلم... وإلى ما يعلم... وإلى ما يعلم'.
2
Notice the order of presentation
Observe that Ghazali first mentions reason alone, then scripture alone, then both. This order reflects the epistemological priority he defends.
3
Reflect on why this classification matters
Consider how this division sets the stage for the rest of the chapter, where each category will be explained in detail.
This classification is foundational for understanding Ghazali's method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني في بيانِ وُجُوبِ التصديق بأمورٍ وَرَدَ بها الشمع وقضى بجوازها العقل وفيه مقدمة وفصلان : أما المقدمة : فهو أنَّ ما لا يعلم بالضرورة ينقسم : إلى ما يعلم بدليل المعلوم بالضرورة العقل دون الشرع ، وإلى ما يعلمُ بالشرع دون العقل ، وإلى ما يعلم بهما أما المعلوم بدليل العقل دون الشرع .. فهو حدث العالم ، ووجود المحدث وقدرته وعلمه وإرادته ، فإنَّ كلَّ ذلك ما لم يثبت .. لا يثبت الشرع ؛ إذ الشرع ينبني على الكلام ، فإن لم يثبت كلام النفس .. لم يثبت الشرع ، فكل ما يتقدم في الرتبة على كلام النفس يستحيل إثباته بكلام النفس وما يستند إليه ، ونفس الكلام أيضاً فيما اخترناه لا يمكن إثباتُهُ بالشرع . ومن المحققين من تكلَّفَ ذلك وادّعاه كما سبقت الإشارة إليه (1) وأما المعلوم بمجرد السمع .. فيخصِّصُ أحد الجائزين بالوقوع ؛ فإن ذلك من مواقف العقول، وإنما يعرفُ من الله تعالى بوحي وإلهام ، ونحن نعلمه من المُوحَى إليه بسماع ؛ كالحشر والنشر والثواب والعقاب وأمثالها وأما المعلوم بهما .. فكلُّ ما هو واقع في مجال العقل ومتأخر في الرتبة عن إثبات كلام الله تعالى ؛ كمسألة الرؤية ، وانفراد الله تعالى بخلق الحركات والأعراض كلّها وما يجري هذا المجرى ، ثم كلُّ ما ورد السمعُ به ينظرُ : فإن كان العقل مجوزاً له .. وجب التصديقُ به قطعاً إن كانت الأدلة السمعية قاطعة في متنها وسندها لا يتطرق إليها احتمال (1) لأن الكلام إذا ثبت بالشرع .. لزم الدور ؛ لأن الشرع كلامه أيضاً انظر ( ص ۱۸۲ ) . ثلاثة
Chapter Two: On explaining the necessity of believing matters that come from scripture (sham‘) and reason (ʿaql) judges their permissibility. It contains an introduction and two sections. As for the introduction: what is not known by necessity is divided into: what is known by a rational proof without scripture, what is known by scripture without reason, and what is known by both. As for what is known by rational proof without scripture, it is the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the world, the existence of the temporal originator (muḥdith), His power, knowledge, and will. For all of that, if it is not established, scripture is not established; because scripture is built upon speech (kalām). If the speech of the self (kalām al-nafs) is not established, scripture is not established. So everything that precedes in rank the speech of the self cannot be established by the speech of the self or what is based on it. And the speech itself, in our chosen view, cannot be established by scripture. Some investigators have taken trouble over that and claimed it, as previously indicated. (1) As for what is known by mere hearing/scripture, it specifies one of the two equally possible things as occurring; for that is beyond the scope of intellects, and it is only known from God through revelation and inspiration. We know it from the one inspired by hearing: such as resurrection, scattering, reward, punishment, and the like. As for what is known by both, it is everything that falls within the scope of reason and is posterior in rank to the establishment of God's speech; such as the issue of vision (ru’ya), and God's exclusive creation of all movements and accidents, and what proceeds in that manner. Then every matter that scripture brings is considered: if reason deems it possible, then belief in it is absolutely obligatory if the scriptural proofs are decisive in text and chain, with no possibility of interpretation. (1) Because if speech is established by scripture, circularity would occur; because scripture is also His speech. See p. 182. Three.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a classification (muqaddimah) introducing Chapter Two. Ghazali divides what is not known by necessity into three types based on their source of knowledge: (1) known by rational proof alone without scripture, (2) known by scripture alone without reason, and (3) known by both. For the first type, he lists the temporal origination of the world, the existence of the Creator, and His attributes (power, knowledge, will). These must be established by reason before scripture can be accepted, because scripture is built on the concept of divine speech (kalām). If divine speech (as an attribute) is not proven, scripture as revelation cannot be proven. He notes a minority view that claims scripture can prove these, but rejects it. For the second type, known by scripture alone, it concerns specifying which of two equally possible events actually occurs (e.g., details of the afterlife). Reason alone cannot determine these; they require revelation. For the third type, known by both reason and scripture, these are matters that reason can grasp but are posterior to the establishment of God's speech (e.g., the beatific vision, God's exclusive creation of motions and accidents). He further notes that if scripture brings something that reason deems possible, then belief is obligatory if the scriptural evidence is decisive (qāṭiʿa) in both text (matn) and chain (sanad) with no possibility of interpretation. The footnote explains a circularity problem if divine speech is proven by scripture. The passage ends abruptly with 'ثلاثة' possibly indicating the start of the next section or page.

Key Terms
ضرورة
سمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All religious knowledge comes from scripture alone.
Correction: Ghazali shows that some foundational truths must be established by reason before scripture can be accepted.

Checkpoint
How many categories does Ghazali divide unknown matters into?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four

Key Takeaway
Ghazali classifies matters not known by necessity into three types based on their source of knowledge: reason, scripture, or both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are uncertain about a religious matter, do you first turn to your own reasoning, or do you immediately search for a verse or hadith? What does this reveal about your trust in the intellect that Allah gave you? Will you commit to engaging both reason and scripture in your next theological doubt?
?
Think of a belief you hold without ever having questioned it. Which of Ghazali's three categories does it fall into? If it belongs to 'known by reason alone,' have you ever actually examined the rational proof for it? If not, what would it take for you to seek that proof?
?
Have you ever dismissed a rational argument about Islam because you felt it was 'just philosophy' and not 'real religion'? How does Ghazali's willingness to include reason as a source of certain knowledge challenge that attitude? What will you change about how you evaluate arguments from reason from now on?

The Primacy of Reason: Foundations of Scripture
Ghazali argues that certain truths must be proven by reason because scripture depends on them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali insists that certain foundational beliefs (like the existence of God) must be proven by reason before scripture can be accepted.

Core Idea
Reason is the foundation; scripture depends on it.

After establishing the three categories, Ghazali explains the first category in detail. He argues that the temporal origination of the world, the existence of the Creator, and His attributes must be known by reason alone. Why? Because scripture itself is built on the concept of divine speech, which must first be proven. This lesson explores that crucial argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first category
Read the Arabic passage that begins 'أما المعلوم بدليل العقل دون الشرع'. Note the specific items listed.
The list includes 'حدث العالم' and 'وجود المحدث' etc.
2
Follow the argument for priority
Ghazali explains that these items must be established first; otherwise scripture cannot be established, because scripture depends on the concept of divine speech (kalām al-nafs).
Understand the logical dependency.
3
Consider the alternative view
Ghazali mentions that some scholars claimed these could be proven from scripture, but he rejects that.
This reinforces his position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني في بيانِ وُجُوبِ التصديق بأمورٍ وَرَدَ بها الشمع وقضى بجوازها العقل وفيه مقدمة وفصلان : أما المقدمة : فهو أنَّ ما لا يعلم بالضرورة ينقسم : إلى ما يعلم بدليل المعلوم بالضرورة العقل دون الشرع ، وإلى ما يعلمُ بالشرع دون العقل ، وإلى ما يعلم بهما أما المعلوم بدليل العقل دون الشرع .. فهو حدث العالم ، ووجود المحدث وقدرته وعلمه وإرادته ، فإنَّ كلَّ ذلك ما لم يثبت .. لا يثبت الشرع ؛ إذ الشرع ينبني على الكلام ، فإن لم يثبت كلام النفس .. لم يثبت الشرع ، فكل ما يتقدم في الرتبة على كلام النفس يستحيل إثباته بكلام النفس وما يستند إليه ، ونفس الكلام أيضاً فيما اخترناه لا يمكن إثباتُهُ بالشرع . ومن المحققين من تكلَّفَ ذلك وادّعاه كما سبقت الإشارة إليه (1) وأما المعلوم بمجرد السمع .. فيخصِّصُ أحد الجائزين بالوقوع ؛ فإن ذلك من مواقف العقول، وإنما يعرفُ من الله تعالى بوحي وإلهام ، ونحن نعلمه من المُوحَى إليه بسماع ؛ كالحشر والنشر والثواب والعقاب وأمثالها وأما المعلوم بهما .. فكلُّ ما هو واقع في مجال العقل ومتأخر في الرتبة عن إثبات كلام الله تعالى ؛ كمسألة الرؤية ، وانفراد الله تعالى بخلق الحركات والأعراض كلّها وما يجري هذا المجرى ، ثم كلُّ ما ورد السمعُ به ينظرُ : فإن كان العقل مجوزاً له .. وجب التصديقُ به قطعاً إن كانت الأدلة السمعية قاطعة في متنها وسندها لا يتطرق إليها احتمال (1) لأن الكلام إذا ثبت بالشرع .. لزم الدور ؛ لأن الشرع كلامه أيضاً انظر ( ص ۱۸۲ ) . ثلاثة
Chapter Two: On explaining the necessity of believing matters that come from scripture (sham‘) and reason (ʿaql) judges their permissibility. It contains an introduction and two sections. As for the introduction: what is not known by necessity is divided into: what is known by a rational proof without scripture, what is known by scripture without reason, and what is known by both. As for what is known by rational proof without scripture, it is the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the world, the existence of the temporal originator (muḥdith), His power, knowledge, and will. For all of that, if it is not established, scripture is not established; because scripture is built upon speech (kalām). If the speech of the self (kalām al-nafs) is not established, scripture is not established. So everything that precedes in rank the speech of the self cannot be established by the speech of the self or what is based on it. And the speech itself, in our chosen view, cannot be established by scripture. Some investigators have taken trouble over that and claimed it, as previously indicated. (1) As for what is known by mere hearing/scripture, it specifies one of the two equally possible things as occurring; for that is beyond the scope of intellects, and it is only known from God through revelation and inspiration. We know it from the one inspired by hearing: such as resurrection, scattering, reward, punishment, and the like. As for what is known by both, it is everything that falls within the scope of reason and is posterior in rank to the establishment of God's speech; such as the issue of vision (ru’ya), and God's exclusive creation of all movements and accidents, and what proceeds in that manner. Then every matter that scripture brings is considered: if reason deems it possible, then belief in it is absolutely obligatory if the scriptural proofs are decisive in text and chain, with no possibility of interpretation. (1) Because if speech is established by scripture, circularity would occur; because scripture is also His speech. See p. 182. Three.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a classification (muqaddimah) introducing Chapter Two. Ghazali divides what is not known by necessity into three types based on their source of knowledge: (1) known by rational proof alone without scripture, (2) known by scripture alone without reason, and (3) known by both. For the first type, he lists the temporal origination of the world, the existence of the Creator, and His attributes (power, knowledge, will). These must be established by reason before scripture can be accepted, because scripture is built on the concept of divine speech (kalām). If divine speech (as an attribute) is not proven, scripture as revelation cannot be proven. He notes a minority view that claims scripture can prove these, but rejects it. For the second type, known by scripture alone, it concerns specifying which of two equally possible events actually occurs (e.g., details of the afterlife). Reason alone cannot determine these; they require revelation. For the third type, known by both reason and scripture, these are matters that reason can grasp but are posterior to the establishment of God's speech (e.g., the beatific vision, God's exclusive creation of motions and accidents). He further notes that if scripture brings something that reason deems possible, then belief is obligatory if the scriptural evidence is decisive (qāṭiʿa) in both text (matn) and chain (sanad) with no possibility of interpretation. The footnote explains a circularity problem if divine speech is proven by scripture. The passage ends abruptly with 'ثلاثة' possibly indicating the start of the next section or page.

Key Terms
حدث العالم
محدث
كلام النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Scripture can prove the existence of God and His attributes.
Correction: Ghazali argues that scripture presupposes these, so reason must prove them first.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which of the following must be proven by reason before scripture can be accepted?
A) The resurrection and punishment
B) The temporal origination of the world
C) The beatific vision
D) The creation of movements

Key Takeaway
Reason must prove the existence of God and His attributes because scripture itself relies on the prior concept of divine speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If reason is the foundation for proving God's existence and attributes, have you ever critically examined the rational basis for your belief in God? Or do you rely solely on what you've been taught? Take time today to articulate to yourself a rational proof for God's existence.
?
Ghazali insists that even the concept of divine speech cannot be proven by scripture. Does this challenge how you view the Qur'an as proof? How does knowing that reason must first establish God's attributes affect your reliance on scripture?
?
If scripture is built upon rational truths, then studying Islamic theology (kalam) is not optional. What steps will you take this week to strengthen your rational understanding of the foundations of faith?

Module 8 · Lesson 53

Allah's Existence
intermediate
8 min
Scripture's Role and Combined Knowledge
Ghazali defines the second and third categories and sets conditions for accepting scriptural reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what role Ghazali assigns to scripture alone and to the combination of reason and scripture, and the condition for believing scriptural reports.

Core Idea
Scripture specifies possibilities; reason and scripture together confirm what is rationally possible.

The second category covers matters that only scripture can specify, like details of the afterlife. The third covers matters within reason's scope but posterior to establishing God's speech, like the beatific vision. Ghazali also imposes a crucial condition: if scripture brings something that reason deems possible, belief is obligatory only if the evidence is decisive. This lesson unpacks these categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second and third categories
Read the Arabic from 'وأما المعلوم بمجرد السمع' to the end of the passage. Identify the examples given.
Examples for second: الحشر والنشر; for third: الرؤية.
2
Understand the function of scripture alone
Scripture specifies which of two equally possible alternatives actually occurs, something reason cannot determine.
This is the 'تخصيص أحد الجائزين'.
3
Analyze the combined category and the condition
Matters known by both are within reason's scope but posterior to divine speech. Then Ghazali adds a general rule: if scripture brings something that reason deems possible, belief is obligatory if the evidence is decisive.
Note the terms 'جائز', 'قطعي', 'متن', 'سند'.

الباب الثاني في بيانِ وُجُوبِ التصديق بأمورٍ وَرَدَ بها الشمع وقضى بجوازها العقل وفيه مقدمة وفصلان : أما المقدمة : فهو أنَّ ما لا يعلم بالضرورة ينقسم : إلى ما يعلم بدليل المعلوم بالضرورة العقل دون الشرع ، وإلى ما يعلمُ بالشرع دون العقل ، وإلى ما يعلم بهما أما المعلوم بدليل العقل دون الشرع .. فهو حدث العالم ، ووجود المحدث وقدرته وعلمه وإرادته ، فإنَّ كلَّ ذلك ما لم يثبت .. لا يثبت الشرع ؛ إذ الشرع ينبني على الكلام ، فإن لم يثبت كلام النفس .. لم يثبت الشرع ، فكل ما يتقدم في الرتبة على كلام النفس يستحيل إثباته بكلام النفس وما يستند إليه ، ونفس الكلام أيضاً فيما اخترناه لا يمكن إثباتُهُ بالشرع . ومن المحققين من تكلَّفَ ذلك وادّعاه كما سبقت الإشارة إليه (1) وأما المعلوم بمجرد السمع .. فيخصِّصُ أحد الجائزين بالوقوع ؛ فإن ذلك من مواقف العقول، وإنما يعرفُ من الله تعالى بوحي وإلهام ، ونحن نعلمه من المُوحَى إليه بسماع ؛ كالحشر والنشر والثواب والعقاب وأمثالها وأما المعلوم بهما .. فكلُّ ما هو واقع في مجال العقل ومتأخر في الرتبة عن إثبات كلام الله تعالى ؛ كمسألة الرؤية ، وانفراد الله تعالى بخلق الحركات والأعراض كلّها وما يجري هذا المجرى ، ثم كلُّ ما ورد السمعُ به ينظرُ : فإن كان العقل مجوزاً له .. وجب التصديقُ به قطعاً إن كانت الأدلة السمعية قاطعة في متنها وسندها لا يتطرق إليها احتمال (1) لأن الكلام إذا ثبت بالشرع .. لزم الدور ؛ لأن الشرع كلامه أيضاً انظر ( ص ۱۸۲ ) . ثلاثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All religious matters are known only from scripture.
Correction: Ghazali shows that some matters require reason, and others require both.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is required for belief in a scriptural report to be obligatory?
A) It must be mentioned in the Quran.
B) It must be reported by many narrators.
C) It must be deemed possible by reason and the evidence must be decisive.
D) It must be known by reason alone.

Key Takeaway
Scripture specifies what reason cannot, and when both agree, belief is obligatory provided the evidence is decisive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran or hadith about the afterlife, do you truly submit to its details as decisive, or do you let your own reasoning doubt or reinterpret them? This lesson demands that if reason deems it possible, you must believe absolutely. Where in your heart do you hesitate?
?
Are there verses or hadith that you have avoided taking literally because they seem strange to your reason? Ghazali says if the proofs are decisive, belief is obligatory. What does that mean for your faith in the unseen?
?
How often do you treat scriptural reports as mere stories rather than decisive truths that require your absolute submission? Name one specific report you have not fully embraced, and ask yourself: is it because you doubt its possibility or because you haven't checked the decisiveness of its proof? This lesson calls you to examine your own consistency.
